THE RED PAGE.
SONNETS BY GEORGE MEREDITH
LUCIFER IN STARLIGHT.
On a starred night Prince Lucifer uprose.
JLired of his dark dominion swung the fiend
Above the rolling ball in cloud part screened,
Where sinners hugged their spectre of repose.
Jroor prey to his hot fit of pride were those.
And now upon his western wing he leaned,
Now his huge bulk o’er Afric’s sands careened,
Now the black planet shadowed Arctic snows.
Soaring through wider zones that pricked his scars
With memory of the old revolt from Awe,
He reached a middle height, and at the stars,
Which are the brain of heaven, he looked and sank
Around the ancient track marched, rank on rank
I he army of unalterable law.
EARTH’S SECRET.
Not solitarily in fields we find
Earth’s secret open, though one page is there ;
Her plainest, such as children spell, and share
With bird and breast; raised letters for the blind.
Not where the troubled passions toss the mind,
In turbid cities, can the key be bare.
It hangs for those who hither thither fare,
Close interthreading nature with our kind.
a j 1 bearing History speak of what men were,
And have become, are wise. The gain is great
In vision and solidity ; it lives.
Yet at a thought of life apart from her,
Solidity and vision lose their istate,
Eor Earth, that gives the milk, the spirit gives.
THE SPIRIT OF SHAKESPEARE.
i.
Thy greatest knew thee, Mother Earth; unsoured
He knew thy sons. He probed from hell to hell
Of human passions, but of love deflowered
His wisdom was not, for he knew thee well.
Thence eame the honeyed corner at his .lips,
The conquering smile wherein his spirit sails
Calm as the God who the white sea-wave whips,
Yet full.of speech and intershifting tales,
Close mirrors of us : thence had he the laugh
We feel is thine: broad as ten thousand beeves
At pasture ! thence thy songs, that winnow chaff
From grain, bid sick Philosophy’s last leaves
Whirl, if they have no response—they enforced
To fatten Earth when from her soul divorced,
ir.
How smiles he at a generation ranked
In gloomy noddings over life ! They pass.
Not he to feed upon a breast unthanked,
Or eye a beauteous face in a cracked glass.
But he can spy that little twist of brain
Which moved some weighty leader of the blind,
Unwitting ’t was the goad of personal pain,
To view in curst eclipse our Mother’s mind.
And show us of some rigid harridan
The wretched bondmen till the end of time.
O lived the Master now to paint us Man,
That little twist of bra.n would ring a chime
Of 1 whence it came and what it caused, to start
Thunders of laughter, clearing air and heart.
GRACE AND LOVE.
Two flower-enfolding crystal vases she
I love fills daily, mindful but of one :
And close behind pale morn she, like the sun
Priming our world with light, pours, sweet to see,
Clear water in the cup, and into me
The image oJLherself : and that being done,
Choice of what blooms round her fair garden run
In climbers or in creepers or the tree,
She ranges with unerring fingers fine,
To harmony so vivid that through sight
I hear, I have her heavenliness to fold
Beyond the senses, where such love as mine.
Such grace as hers, should the strange Fates with-
hold
Their starry more from her and me, unite.
THE COMFORTING COMMA
mHE golden rule in punctuation is :
| Do your punctuation your own way—
and the compositor will do it his.
And it is evident, from the specimen
printed hereunder, that it is quite time
Rudyard Kipling changed his compositor.
For so preposterous is the punctuation of
some of his later verses in Puck of Pook’s
Hill that Kipling can only plead in palliation
the machination of his compositor. The
trite thought in the following verses from
Puck is gracefully expressed—but the cor-
ruscation of superfluous commas !
Cities and Thrones and Powers,
Stand in Time’s eye.
Almost as long as flowers,
Which daily die:
But, as new buds put forth,
To glad new men,
Out of the spent and inconsidered Earth,
The cities rise again.
This season’s Daffodil,
She never hears,
What change, what chance, what chill,
Cut down last year’s :
But with bold countenance,
And knowledge small,
Esteems her seven days’ eontin uance
To be perpetual. ♦
So Time that is o’er-kind,
To all that be,
Ordains us e’en as blind,
As bold as she :
That in our very death,
And burial sure,
Shadow to shadow, well persuaded, saith,
‘ See how our works endure ! ’
A consideration of these verses will show
that Kipling, the rare artist in words, has
lost himself in a jungle of commas ; his rough
and ready rule seems to be-r-when in doubt,
put a comma at the end of every line. It is
only necessary to print these verses as prose
in order to show how distressfully Kipling
has come out in spots.
Cities and Thrones and Powers (comma) stand
in Time’s ey & (comma) almost as long as flowers
(comma) which daily die (colon) but (comma)
as new buds put forth (comma) to glad new men
(comma) out of the spent and inconsidered Earth
(comma) the cities rise again. This season’s
Daffodil (comma) she never hears (comma) what
change (comma) what chance (comma) what chill
(comma) cut down last year’s (colon) but with
bold countenance (comma) and knowledge small
(comma) esteems her seven days’ continuance to
be perpetual. So time that is o’er-kind (comma)
to all that be (comma) ordains us e’en as blind
(comma) as bold as she (colon) that in our very
death (comma) and burial sure (comma) shadow
to shadow (comma) well persuaded (comma) saith
(comma) “ See how our works endure ? ”
With the most liberal allowance, there are
at least half-a-dozen commas too many in
these verses. And Kipling sins not only
against the uncomplaining comma ; in his
verses quoted last week he has sentences as
slip-shod as this : ‘ £ He knows what far
snows melt; along what mountain-wall a
thousand leagues to the North.” Here he
has brutally misused a semi-colon. Let us
commend to this tiro in punctuation the
careful study of the thirty pages treating of
the proper use of the mere comma in The
King’s English (Clarendon Press), where he
will find such advice as this :
Any one who finds himself putting down several
commas close to one another should reflect that he
is making himself disagreeable, and question his
conscience, as severely as we ought to do about
disagreeable conduct in real life, whether it is
necessary.... Erasing a pair of commas may make
the difference in writing that is made in conversa-
tion by a change of tone from the didactic to the
courteous.... It is a safe statement that a gather-
ing of commas (except on certain lawful occasions,
as in a list) is a suspicious circumstance. The
sentence should at least be read aloud, and if it
halts or jolts some change or other should be
made.... Ungrammatical insertion of stops is a
high crimeand misdemeanor, whereas ungrammati-
cal omission of them is often venial, and in some
cases even desirable.... It is a sound principle
that as few stops should be used as will do the
work.... Everyone should make up his mind not
to depend on his stops.
ET CETERA
There is no need for the supernatural nowadays:
Kipling’s device of a magic-working Puck is hut a
clumsy expedient when contrasted with Jack
London’s scientific basis for recreating the past.
In Everybody's Magazine London begins a serial,
Before Adam, which promises interesting develop-
ments. He uses the simple device of the racial
memory surviving in a modern man in a super-
developed form, and vexing him with vivid dreams
of that prehistoric past when ape-man was just
beginning to desert his comfortable trees and walk
erect on the strange and terror-filled earth. Inci-
dentally London draws these pictures of his
Mid-Pleistocene arboreal mother and father.
“She was like a large orang-utan, or like a
chimpanzee; and yet, in sharp and definite ways,
quite different. She was heavier in build than
they, and had less hair. Her arms were not so
long, and her legs were stouter. She wore no
clothes —only her natural hair . . . And, like a
fury, she dashed upon the scene. She was gritting
her. teeth, making frightful grimaces, snarling. . . .
He was not an extremely prepossessing father, as
fathers go. He seemed half man and half ape,
and yet not ape, and not yet man. . . . His
face was broad and flat, and the eye-
brows overhung the eyes. The eyes themselves
were small, deep-set and close together. He had
practically no nose at all. It was . squat and
broad, apparently without any bridge, while the
nostrils were like two holes in the face, opening
outward instead of down. The forehead si mted
back from the eyes, and the hair began right at
the eyes, and ran up over the head. . . . There
was an elemental economy about his body—as
there was about all our bodies. The chest was
deep, it is true, deep ; but there were
no full-swelling muscles, no wide-spreading
shoulders, no clean-limbed straightness, no gener-
ous symmetry of outline. ... I remember that
he could not walk on the flat of his foot. That
was because it was prehensile, more like a hand
than a foot. . . . He came through the trees, leap!
ing from limb to limb, and from tree to tree ; and
he came swiftlv . . . swinging along through the
trees, a four-handed, hairy creature, howling with
rage, pausing now and again to beat his chest with
his clenched fist, leaping ten and fifteen-foot gaps,
catching a branch with one hand, and swinging*
on across another gap to catch with his other hand
and go on, never hesitating, never at a loss as to
how to proceed on his arboreal way.”
The reader will look forward with interest to
the description of the heroine.

The business side of American magazines.
This is hovy they ad vertise—for their advertisers.
Scribner's informs advertisers that it is “in the
200,000 class; your advertisements will go into
200,000 homes where your goods should find a con-
tinuous market.” Harper’s has “an exclusive
circulation among the refined ar.d well-to-do.. .
a powerful buying clientile.” McClure's “deals
with over 414,000 families.” The American quotes
its editorial staff with the remark that' these
writers “have added to circulation of
other magazines in 100,000 ' lots,” and for
the future “ their work will be devoted to
the up-building of The American.” The pick of
the bunch, however, seems to be Gunter's, which
“covers a territory that no other publication
reaches. This territory consists of Archibald
Clavering Gunter’s following.... This publishing
house can show a record of over ten million copies
of a dozen romances by this author, and 50,000 of
his following buy the magazine each month.” It
is apparently a wild distinction to be one of the
following of a person of the name of Gunter.
“O’Fipp ” : Isn’t it time the white-haired boy
of the “literature societies” was publicly re-
christened ’Damn Lindsay Gordon ? -
“ Mudd ” hazards another derivation for
boyangs. Suggests that as the miners of ’49
carried their sheath knives in the tops of their
Wellington boots, and later in their boyangs, the
word might have arrived from “ bowie-hangs.”
This derivation has, at least, the charm of ori-
ginality.
THE PIONEERS.
Poet, whose verse in lines that glow,
Shull tell lads yet to come
Of Hawke’s dread swoop upon the foe,
Or rattle of Drake’s drum, ;
. Bard of that bright sea-glory’s roll,
Where battle splendors play,
What can this bring to touch thy soul,
This land of mere To-day ?
Sundered—a hemisphere away ;
Distant—an empire’s length ;
Resistless, friendly myriads lay,
Great England’s shattering strength.
But here, strange hills and silent shore
Shut in the lonely few,
And o’er dark ridge and torrents roar,
The very stars were new.
Through gorge and flood, ’neath gale and sun,
They forced their trackless way.
Meeting in fight hard-fought, hard-won,
Brown warriors brave as they.
Through storms they jested, rode or slept ;
They fought with brightened eye ;
Baffled or conquering they kept
A stoic levity.
And then—the narrow days ; the numb
And slack, discouraged mood.
And coarse monotony, and dumb
Entombing solitude ;
The weary, spirit-deadening page
Of lean, resultless years.
These were their foes —more fell to face
Than soul-awakening fears.
Strong few, of solitary thought
And visionary ray l
Brave common many, who yet wrought,
Enduring for their day 1
Passing, they saw their race abide,
And now we know them great,
Whose hands took plain and mountain-side,
And graved thereon a State.
[From a scholarly set of verses, inscribed To
Henry Newbolt, by \V. P. Peeves, poet and High
Commissioner, in Oct. National Review .]
Lord Rosebery adds a personal note to Win-
ston Churchill's life of Lord Randolph in the
shape of a small volume published by A. L.
Humphreys, Loud, (per The Book Club). In his
balanced and sonorous prose Lord Rosebery, after
paying the highest of tributes to Winston
Churchill’s Life, draws this portrait of that
“shooting star of politics,” his dead friend.
Randolph’s personality was o‘ne full of charm,
both in public and in private life. His demeanor,
his unexpectedness, his fits of caressing humility,
his impulsiveness, his tinge of violent eccentricity,
his apparent daredevilry, made him a fascinating
companion; while his wit, his sarcasm, his
pieroing personalities, his elaborate irony and his
effective delivery gave astonishing popularity to
his speeches. Nor were his physical attributes
without their attraction. His slim and boyish
figure, his moustache, which had an emotiot»of its
own, his round, protruding eye, gave a compound
interest to his speeches and his conversation. His
laugh, which has been described as “jaylike,’’was,
indeed, not melodious, but in its very weird-
ness and discordance it was merriment itself....
He had also the vital mainspring of zest. To
whatever he applied himself he gave for the time
his whole eager heart. He was strenuous at
politics, but he was also at times devoted to
hunting, racing, and chess, and he took gastro-
nomy as seriously as Maeauley. But whatever it
might be, politics or pleasure, it possessed him
entirely; he did it with gusto, with every nerve
and every fibre,... He returned to England... and
went on the turf. This was a new passion with
him ; inferior, indeed, in excitement, as Lord
Palmerston once remarked, to politics ; but new
and absorbing. ... Racing remained a passion
with him to the end. Almost every letter that I
had from him in his last years of life was about that
sport. Let not ambition mock these homely joys...
He .will be pathetically memorable, too, for the
dark cloud which gradually enveloped him, and in
which he passed away. He was the chief mourner
at his own protracted funeral, a public pageant of
gloomy years. Will he not he remembered as much
for the anguish as for the fleeting triumphs, of his
life? It is a black moment when the heralds
proclaim the passing of the dead, and the great
officers break their staves. But it is sadder still
when it is the victim’s own voice that announces
his decadence, when it is the victim’s own hands
that break the staff in public.
fit
Arthur 0. Benson, who has a habit of publishing
his books anonymously, gi ves his impressions of
the reviewer in Oct. Putnam’s Monthly. He
believes that hooks are actually read by reviewers,
and rather carefully, though “ probably with a
certain practised speed that would be incredible
to a man whose business it had never been
to tear the hea»# out of a book and vivisect it.”
And the days of the old slashing and bullying
review are practically over. Modern readers do
not want style; they want amusement, an emo-
tional thrill. They want the characters to be
manly, womanly, typical. They want sentiment
rather than poetry, color rather than delicacy.
The trouble about reviewers is that they accept,
and do not educate, public taste. “ The public
seems to want a vast supply of not very masterly
novels, and there is no reason why they should
not have what they want.”
fit
“Leonardo”: “Imagine Esperanto, with ita
consistency and exactness, the universal scientific
and philosophic medium, and then think of
reading Schopenhauer, Haeckle, Voltaire, etc.,
straight from the minds of the authors, instead of
being retransmitted through the translators’
tinted spectacles.” Well, imagine it!
The primrose path. “ A Complete Apprentice-
ship to Literature” is advertised in a London
literary periodical. “Our success course, written
by a novelist whose name is a household word,
will«teach you the essentials which are necessary
to your success, and save you years of fruitless
striving.” Now, who is that novelist ?
Mirk Twain, in his Autobiography , discourse*
with R. L. Stevenson upon “submerged renown-
Stevenson had heard of a writer named Davis
who was apparently,the most popular author
the United. States. His books had to hav
freight-trains to carry them, not baskets.
“ Nobody has heard of Davis.. . . You never see
his name mentioned in print, not even in adver
tisement; these things are of no use to Davis, no
any more than they are to the wind and the sea
You never see one of Davis’s books floating 01
top of the United States; but put on your diving
armor and get yourself lowered away down anc
down and down till you strike the dense region
the sunless region of eternal drudgery and starva
tion wages—there you’ll find them by the million
The man that gets that market, his fortune is
made, his bread and butter are safe, for those
people will never go back on him. An authoi
may have a reputation which is confined to
the surface, and lose it and become pitied, then
despised, then forgotten, entirely forgotten—the
'frequent steps in a surface reputation. A surface
reputation, nowever great, is always mortal, and
always killable if you go at it right—with pins
and needles, and quiet, slow poison, not with the
club and tomahawk. But it is a different matter
with the submerged reputation—down in the deep
water ; once a favorite there, always a favorite
once beloved, always beloved ; once respected,
always respected, honored, and believed in. For
what the reviewer says never finds its way down
into those placid deeps; nor the newspaper
sneers, nor any breath of jthe winds of slander
blowing above. Down there they never hear of
these things. Their idol may be painted clay, up
there at the surface, and fade and waste and
crumble and blow away, there being much
weather there; but down below he is gold and
adamant and indestructable.”
Steele Rudd's Magazine, “all Australian,” for
Nov., contains an interesting critical article by
Arthur A. D. Bayldon upon the art of novel-
writing ; “A Policy for Australia ” ; a well-
illustrated chapter of the life of Robert Brough ;
and much cheerful Australian fiction, verse, and
many illustrations. Some of the latter do not
adorn even the advertisement pages into which
they have been callously thrust. Altogether a
readable issue.
Correspondents will note that the controversy
as to which poet balances himself “on the pinnacle
of Australian poesy ’’ is hereby brutally butchered.
The pinnacle is still to let.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
South Seas.
It has drawn, this dreaming isle,
Its white magic ring around me ;
I am captive in the circle of its reef.
With a woman’s quiet guile,
O, it beckoned and has bound me ;
I lie prisoned here beyond unrest or grief
How they call and call to me—
Those long blinding yellow beaches,
And the white spurt of the combers leaping high.
And the maddening blue of sea,
And its green, pellucid reaches,
And the splash of palms upon the silken skj )
So I lie for evermore,
(Hark, the boy a-beating kava !)
Idly listening to a brown girl’s lulling croon
Crouching cross-legged on the floor
In her dainty lava-lava,
With her little toe a-tapping to the tune !
And the spell of waving arms
And her voice of liquid sadness
Softly open in my soul a sudden door ;
I remember —like the palms—
All those long dim lives of gladness
That we dreamed through here together long
before!
Nikaulofa.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
My Troubles.
When purpling mists of evening fall
Upon the mournful tomb
That hides her fairness—teeth and all—
My life is wrapt in gloom.
Alas, the floweret’s fairest bloom
Soon passeth into Dusk !
Our most beloved find the Tomb
Wherein their beauties rust.
The earth is full of sad farewells,
New suns old Shadows cast;
And in our living Pleasures dwell
The spectres of the Past!
Of ebbing tides and stabs of pain,
Dead girls and gloomy night.
When I’m in melancholy vein
As properties I write.
And yet few girls that I have known
Are rusting with the dead ;
That into dust fair flowerets go
Gives me no special dread.
I am as gay as I know how ;
My soul is not downcast;
No silent sorrow lines my brow,
Or links me to the Past.
The only chain I ever own
Is mostly up the spout;
I’ve pledged it for a trifling loan—
The ticket’s near run out.
Yet there’s a Presence ever near
To plead for memory—
In fact, before my supper beer
My wife sits down with me.
And oft the thought will come to me
While she chats gaily there,
How truly happy we might be
Had she not dyed her hair !
N.S.W.
HAMER.
The Old Engines.
Upon the railway’s bye-ways
The worn-out engines lie,
And past them on the highways
They watch the trains go by.
And round their leaning smoke-stacks
The nesting swallows fly.
There, lame, and blind, and broken,
All bleared with smoke and grime,
They linger, an unspoken
Reproach on ruthless Time,
Those old and shabby actors
Of a bygone pantomime.
With paint and brasswork wonders,
And gay in gala dres3,
Adown the track there thunders
The arrogant express,
And the old and broken engines
Seem to watch it in distress.
With slow and ponderous action
The “ freighter,” coarse and black.
Her squat sides built for traction,
Goes groaning up the track,
And the ancients wonder dimly
If she ever will come back.
With lean and high-pitched boiler,
And quaint and spidery wheel,
They watch the modern toiler
Along the road of steel,
And strange, regretful yearnings
Their rusty hearts do feel.
Across the dim blue ranges,
When first the way was won,
With Progress and her changes
Towards the setting sun,
With deep-drawn breath they labored
Until their task was done.
Now shunted off the highways,
The worn-out engines lie,
’Mid grass upon the bye-ways,
They watch the trains go by ;
And to their rusty smoke-'staoks
The nesting swallows fly.
N.3.W.
HORACE HALLORAN.

11 oxo to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (ivith regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. r l'o send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 29, 1906.
THE PARTY OF HATREDS.
Dealing with tariff matters, Mr. Wilks said he wished
to be open and candid. He had been a Freetrader all his
life, and had fought against the followers of Protection.
But he now found men supporting 15 and 20 per cent,
duties and calling themselves Freetraders, AND IT WAS
REMARKABLE THAT ALL THESE DUTIES WERE FOR
VICTORIAN INDUSTRIES. He should still endeavor
to have all duties taken off the necessaries of life, and,
after that, if he saw the present system of levying taxes
for the benefit of Victoria was continued, he should see
that New South Wales was cared for. He declared openly
to Freetrader and Protectionist that, if loot and boodle
were to be distributed, he w'ould see in future that New
South Wales g»t its share. This was one of many ways
in which he declared he should stand free from any party
tie. —Sample of Anti-Sosh hatred.
The Bulletin quotes the above, not because
William Wilks, M.H.R. for Dailey (N.S.W.), is
of any personal importance, except for his bad
qualities, but because he is the type of a class—
almost the worst type of the very worst class in
Australian polities. The political party which
George Reid is leading at the present election
has gathered to itself all the notable hatreds in
the community. It is the thing with the muck-
rake, and Wilks is prominent becausehe represents
at the moment about the largest rake and the most
conspicuous heap of dirt. Reid’s party has
annexed the State Frights crowd (the old Anti-
Federal push under a new name) which slan-
ders and denounces the Commonwealth —and the
Commonwealth is Australia —on every possible
and impossible occasion. It has recruited the old
Cabbage Tree Mob of N.S.W., which built up a
coarse and savage hatred of the next-door State
for no visible reason save that Victoria com-
mitted the unpardonable offence of creating a
city which, for a time, overshadowed Sydney —
the mob which still grits the word “ Victorian ”
through its teeth as if it were uttering an adjec-
tive which embodied all the possibilities of
degradation. It has collected to its infected
bosom the insectarian party which puts up candi-
dates on a “religious” ticket, and with gross
hypocrisy talks of charity, goodwill and brotherly
love, while urging the denominations to all
hatred, malice, and uncharitableness. It has
accumulated to its party the people who
hate Australian industry, and lose no oppor-
tunity of denouncing Australian manufacturers
as robbers, and who decry Australian goods as rub-
bish that shouldn’t rank (simply because the goods
are Australian) in the same category with any-
thing imported—the class which is embittered at
the sight of an Australian artisan making fair
wages, or an Australian factory-owner making
fair profits, while there is a Roman Catholic
workman in Belgium who wants the wages,
or a Roman Catholic manufacturer in Bavaria
who could do with the profits. Also it in-
cludes the Anti-Settlement party, which is dis-
gusted at the idea of the people having land to
settle on if the great land monopolist chooses to
keep Australia empty, and which denounces all
proposals for making the great monopolist sell his
empty areas, even at their full and inflated value,
as “confiscation.”
• * • »
And these are not all. The banner of Anti-
Sosh is wide enough to cover, and its camp is
large enough to hold, other unclean elements, and
no matter how divergent may be their views they
gravitate to the Anti-Sosh camp quite naturally.
The common bond of being unclean and un-
patriotic keeps them together. Therefore, in the
following of Reid, who says he invented the term
“White Australia, ” are to be found the colored-
labor advocates like Bruce Smith— the men who
hate to see an Australian citizen have a job if it
is possible to get a nigger or a Chinaman to do it.
And in the same army are the slanderers who
incite class hatreds with gross falsehoods about
the alleged intentions of the Democratic
section of the community to “destroy the
marriage tie,” and “break up the home,”
and indulge in foul orgies of free love and satyr
revelry. With these are the people who de-
nounce all proposals that Australia should make
serious preparation for its own defence, and
desire to see the country left open to the first
Asiatic invader who may happen this way. With
them, also, is the Stinking Fish element—the
crowd which, for years, filled the papers of
England, India, the United States and South
Africa with falsehoods about honest English
hatters who were put in an imaginary gaol ; and
about honest colored seamen who, being wrecked
on an inhospitable coast, were wilfully permitted
to drown (according to one lie) or arrested and
gaoled (according to another Ananias) ; and about
capital that was all leaving the country, and a
population that was leaving so unanimously
that no one remained who could get away
(vide the utterances of the late Ebenezer
Vickery and the survivors of his class).
And all these hatreds are buttressed up
by the support of wealth and monopoly—
the huge fortunes and the huge acreages.
Tooralc and Darling Point are with Anti-
Sosh. The senile decay of the State Upper
Houses gives it a moral support. Worrall, the
screaming preacher who denounced the alleged
curse of God on Australia by way of a pulpit ad ver-
tisement, is understood to be with it. Maximilian
Hirscii, whose paper received a considerable sum
of money from the American Harvester Trust to
assist it against an Australian industry, is one of
its ornaments. On top of all is George Reid,
who once said (Federal Hansard for 1901-2, page
6800): —
We all hope that Australia will have a great woollen
industry, but that will have to come when we are not so
well off. . . . How are we going to compete with under-
paid, sweated countries until our own labor is underpaid
and sweated too?.. . In the plenitude of time, when our
millions become tens of millions, we shall have a crop of
misery which will solve the difficulty in regard to cheap
manufactures.
The leader who deliberately “hoped” for the
arrival of something which, he said, must in volve
sweating, under-payment, and a crop of misery,
is worthy of his followers, and his followers are
worthy of him. Such a “hope” represents
hatred in a condensed state.
* * * *
The writer commenced by quoting Wilks, and
then wandered away from that insignificant but
objectionable person. Wilks represents, in the
main, three of the hatreds of Anti-Sosh, and
represents others in a less degree. He would
split the community lengthwise by sectarian
hatreds, and cross-wise by inter-State hatreds,
and diagonally by the hatred he strives to create
between the 1, agriculturist, whom he professes to
regard as “the producer,” and the city artisan,
whom he teaches the bucolic person to regard as a
parasite living on his endeavors. His latest pro-
nouncement, quoted above, represents the effort
to sow dissension between States in its meanest
form. With a wonderful pretence at virtuous in-
dignation the old Foreign-trade crowd, which has
now developed into the Anti-Sosh crowd, professes
to see a conspiracy of Victorian industries against
N.S.W. industries, just as it professes to see a
Roman Catholic conspiracy against our national
institutions, and a Democratic conspiracy against
marriage and legitimate children. It alleges
that, in some weird, wild way, Victorian indus-
tries are protected and N.S.W. ones are not. It
doesn’t stop to explain, for explanation would
be fatal; it merely raves and hurries on. The
facts are against it; hence the lack of explanation.
The sugar industry, which is exclusively a
N.S.W. and Queensland industry, received heavy
Protection. The N.S.W. tobacco industry, which
was a large affair, was also heavily protected.
The boot industry, which was almost as large in
N.S.W. as in Victoria, judged by the number of
hands employed, got 30 per cent. Piano manu-
facture (an exclusively N.S.W. industry) got 20 per
cent. Woollens and agricultural implements, in
which Victoria and other States were largely
interested, while N.S.W. was but slightly con-'
cerned, got only 15 and 12J percent, respectively.
The timber-dressing industry, m which N.S.W.
was more than twice as heavily interested,
according to the number of hands employed, as
Victoria, was protected by a whole sheaf of duties.
Brewing, which was then and still is, largely a
N S W. industry, was heavily propped up by the
tariff, while distilling, which was mainly a
Victorian business, and was also an industry in
which N.S.W. had no interest at all, was
killed by the tariff —so f ar > T a J'„l eas V,
Victoria was concerned. Even N.S.W. s little
paper-bag industry was protected (there was no
paper-bag industry in Victoria). As a matter of
obvious fact, the tariff is the same for all Aus-
tralia ; an industry in one State gets the same
assistance as in another. The only trace of a differ-
ence seems to lie in this fact: The original tariff,
as drafted by Kingston, from South Australia,
and accepted by Premier Barton (from N.S.W.),
and Ministers Lyne and O’Connor (from N.S.W.),
favored all industries alike. But the fierce opposi-
tion of the Sydney Foreign-traders cut down the
Protection in the case of many industries in which
Victoria was more largely interested ; while the
same crowd made much less resolute opposition
in the case of the industries in which N.S.W.
predominated. Therefore, on the whole, N.S.W.
came out best, though the difference was not very
great at any time.
* • * *
The Bulletin doesn’t give William Wilks
credit for inventing the Anti-Sosh hoax that
the tariff protects Victoria, and doesn’t pro-
tect New South Wales (by some curious
mental twist it is only at rare intervals
that the Sydney Foreign-trader of the Wilks
class seems to realise that there is anything
else in Australia beyond N.S.W. and Victoria).
It only quotes Wilks as about the most virulent ex-
ponent of a silly fiction which seems to have come
unexpectedly to the front through some of the
other fictions getting sore feet and being unable
to keep up. The fiction itself is the lineal successor
of the other great Tory device for promoting inter-
state hatred—the perjury about how, in some
impossible and impenetrable way, the other
States were stealing the surpluses of N.S.W. and
Westralia, and spending them in beer and revelry.
Also it is a poor relation of the fiction that the
action of the Australian Parliament, when it
selected a site for the capital in N.S.W., and in
the district most warmly recommended by the
N.S.W. State official appointed to report on pos-
sible sites, is evidence of a fixed intention not to
select any site. All these inventions are linked
together to make up the creed of Anti-Sosh. And
on polling day Anti-Sosh will rise up as the Party
of Carefully Fostered Hatreds, and gather in votes
because of its hatreds. It appeals to a small sec-
tion of Anti-Federalists in N.S.W. to vote for it
because of their hatred of Victoria—a sentiment
created solely by falsehood and misrepresentation;
to a handful of misguided bigots of one church
because of their hatred of another church; to
the great landlords because of their hatred of
the small settler; to the importers and their
hangers-on because of their hatred of Australian
industries; to the black-labor crowd by reason of
its hatred of the whole white proletariat; to the
Stinking Fish fragment because of its hatred of
Australia in general. It would fain divide and
subdivide the brotherhood of the Australian
people into warring clans—six States kept apart
by State feuds, and these States subdivided into 12
sections by sectarian animosity, and again into 24
by tariff feuds, and again into 48 by class hatreds
based on the “ marriage tie ” fiction and such like,
and so on to an almost unlimited extent. And
out of all this dissension its miserable sordid gain
is that one-half of each hatred must vote for it.
It stands on the same level as the individual or
alleged individual who circulated fearsome reports
of alleged crimes and secret horrors perpetrated by
the Jewish section of the community, that he
might thereby get the Anti-Jewish support. He
created Anti-J udaism in that district, with much
destruction and riot, so that there might be an
Anti-Jewish vote to support him, and his simple
excuse was that he couldn’t get the Anti-Jewish
vote till there was one, and that he wanted some
kind of a vote badly. His position then was that
of the Reid party now—to a hair.
* * * *
The Bulletin urges the Australian people to
vote against the REiD-Tory-Foreign-trade-Anti-
Sosh party simply because it represents every
needless and purposeless grudge and quarrel in
Australian politics. It has created some of them ;
it has kept the others alive when they might
otherwise be in a fair way to extinction. The
inter-State feuds would be forgotten but for its
perpetual discovery of imaginary wrongs done by
“ the other States” to “our State,” which wrongs
can only be righted by the election of Reid and
Pumpkin Smith. The Anti-Federal sentiment
would be dead but for its constant finding in dark
corners of imaginary outrages done by us in our
Australian capacity upon ourselves in our State
capacity—outrages which can only be alleviated
by the return of Wilks and Kelly with large
majorities. The sectarian row would have just
about perished but for its incessant habit of
dropping on imaginary designs on the part of
“Rome,” the one way to avert which is the
enthusiastic sending into Parliament of Joe
Cook and Simian Phraser. All these things are
good enough for- Reid and Wilks and the rest,
who, for want of a constructive policy, have to
live on the base passions and mean animosities
of the community, but they are a grievous thing
for any true Australian to contemplate. And
just because there are many true Australians,
who want to see the last of State rows, and sec-
tarian rows, and Anti-Federal rows, The Bulle-
tin hopes that December 12 will be the day when
Anti-Sosh is buried, without any tombstone.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Lie That Shamed the “Telegraph.”
It looked for a while as if the busted yarn about
the enmity of the Labor party to the marriage-
tie and the sanctity of the ’ome had been removed
from Anti-Sosh’s stock-in-trade, Sydney Tele-
graph and other Anti-Sosh organs having pro-
tested that they were really ashamed to be seen
in the company of the slander. But as Anti-
Sosh must have something to go to the country
with, and as it has no merits of its own to offer,
and as most of its other perjuries have also been
busted, it is thrown back, apparently, upon the
yarn that the Telegraph was ashamed of ; and at
election times a yarn has to smell worse than
two dead whales before the Telegraph’s stomach
turns against it. Recently, before the Tele-
graph had given the perjury up, The Bulletin
implored somebody to send it for publication the
speech or writing of so much as one representa-
tive Australian Laborite in favor of abolishing
the marriage tie or in defence of free and pro-
miscuous “ love.” There are known to be a third
of a million Laborices in the Commonwealth ;
and, if they don’t believe in marriage as Anti-
Sosh alleges, it surely wasn’t too much to ask for
the declaration of just one of them to support the
yarn upon which Anti-Sosh asks for votes. After
a long expanse of silence, during which appar-
ently the heaps of Labor language and literature
were carefully raked for aspersions on the ’orae,
Liberty and Progress, which says it is the
journal of the Vic. Employers’ Federation, pro-
duced an article written by Tunnicliffe, one
of the Victorian Labor candidates for the Senate.
Boiled down, it says, Tunnicliffe’s article means
that men and women should be able to terminate
marriage at will, and that “boys and girls may
freely listen to the promptings of nature.” But,
instead of leaving it at that, the organ of the
Employers’ Ass. makes the blunder of printing
what Tunnicliffe wrote, and makes the still
greater blunder of printing in capitals the awtul
words upon which it mainly relies. And of tho
said words these are the chief :
The age of sexual puberty in the human animal is
between 16 and 20 years of age ; and to compel men to
live apart until they are 25 or 30 years is unnatural and
unjust.
The capitals stop right there. But Tunnicliffe
didn’t. He went on—
Wages are so low that marriage with its necessary con-
comitants is impossible, and
why shouldn’t The Bulletin use some capitals
too ?
ILLICIT INTERCOURSE ON THE PART OF WOMAN
BRINGS DISGRACE AND ENDS IN PROSTITUTION.
“Wages are so low.” That is the key-note.
Therefore, says Tunnicliffe, man cannot marry.
Therefore there is “disgrace and prostitution.
So far, however, from advocating “ disgrace and
prostitution,” Tunnicliffe appeals for a system
which will stop it—
Under the social democratic system marriage would be
more universal, and would take place at an earlier age.
So, instead of advocating no marriage, Tunni-
cliffe advocates more marriage ; instead of advo-
cating the “illicit intercourse” which he says
“means disgrace and ends in prostitution,” he
would abolish the sweating which prevents mar-
riage and produces prostitutes. And this is the
man, tlnd this is the argument, on which Anti-
Sosh relies to prove its case ! As an Anti- party,
it asks support, not on its own merits, but
on the alleged horrible nature of the proposals of
its opponents; it says the abolition of the mar-
riage tie is one of those horrible proposals; and
then, dri veil into a corner and challenged to pro-
duce one speech or one argument in support
of its infamous charges, it quotes the writings of a
man who implores the public to adopt a system
“ under which marriage would be more universal.”
So Anti-Sosh goes to the poll as the party of one
more Busted Lie. And, somehow, Busted Lies
about an opponent don’t seem to be much of a
policy to build a nation on. A man of very ordi-
nary common sense wouldn’t trust the building of
a hen-coop to a man who based his whole claim to
the job on the yarn that a rival hen-coop builder
was a bigamist and a person who ate two boiled
babies at a meal. And when the yarns were
proved to be mere lies—the lies of a man who
wanted the job, and cared not a straw about any-
body else’s reputation as long as he got the job
—well, he would get no hen-coop to build. He
would just get contempt and the heaviest boot on
the premises.
About a Challenge, and a Cheque for a
Hospital, and the Dead Silence of Senator
Gould.
The Commonwealth had no power, nor was such a
thing ever contemplated, of imposing a tax for the pur-
pose of bursting up big estates, and such a proposal was
a barefaced attempt to interfere with the State rights
and the State prerogative.— Senator Gould, of N.S.W.,
as reported in Sydney ’Graph.
This paper has now been waiting quite a long
time to hear from Senator Gould, solicitor, Tory
and Foreign-trader, on the above subject, and
has been calling in his ear every week without
getting any response. Mr. Gould alleges that
the Commonwealth has no power to impose a
graduated land-tax, and The Bulletin has asked
him persistently to produce the clause of the Con-
stitution which says that it has no power to do so.
If be can’t do that, then he didn’t tell the truth
when he made the above-quoted statement. If
he doesn't attempt to do it, there will be a very
general impression that he is a cocktail, a
backer-down and a creeper-out, also that he
is a political welsher inasmuch as he doesn’t
pay his political debts. For three weeks
The Bulletin held out a considerable cheque
as an inducement to Mr. Gould — or to any
of the multitude of Anti-Sosh politicians who talk
as he does in this matter—to come forward and
prove, by producing that clause, that the truth
was in them. It didn’t get an answer from one
Anti-Sosh politician in the crowd. They were all
just as dead-silent as Mr. Gould. It was the
most unanimous wilt and back-down and crawl-
out that this paper ever met. Anti-Sosh seemed
to wither up unanimously when invited to come
to the point. Even Wilks of Balmain was silent
—dead silent—and didn’t seem to want the money.
That cheque is at present in pawn, so to speak ;
although not one Anti-Sosh politician claimed it,
a demand was put in by a private gentleman of
the Single-tax persuasion in Melbourne. He bases
his claim on a rather extraordinary interpre-
tation of one clause in the Constitution;
still his case must be heard, and The
Bulletin has named its arbitrator and
invited the Melbourne Single-tax gentleman to do
likewise. If, by any apparent impossibility, the
Single-tax gentleman wins his case, The Bulle-
tin’s cheque will have to go to a Melbourne
institution instead of a N.S.W. one—all through
the backing-down and creeping-out of Senator
Gould, of N.S.W., and his Anti-Sosh friends,
including WTlks, and Lonsdale, and George
Reid, and Bruce Smith and Conroy, and not
forgetting Johnson and Kelly, and also remem-
bering Gibb who is running against Bill Lyne
at the Hume, and likewise keeping an eye on
Pumpkin Smith. Most of these people have
talked a whole lot about the alleged wrongs of
N.S.W., but if The Bulletin loses its case, and
through their cowardice that cheque goes to
Victoria, there will be a real, solid wrong to
N.S.W. and they will be responsible. If The
Bulletin, on the other hand, wins on the arbi-
tration, there is no reason at all why these
remarkably silent gentlemen should not have a
shot at the cheque, if they can find any
reason for claiming it apart from the busted
reason of the Single-tax gentleman from Mel-
bourne. It would be absurd to arbitrate a second
time on exactly the same case. Meanwhile,
though the Single-tax gentleman in Melbourne
has the first call on the cheque, there is nothing
to prevent Senator Gould clearing his political
character from any suspicion of gross untruthful-
ness, simply for the sake of his character. That is
to say, if he thinks his political character worth
clearing. And there is nothing to prevent Wilks
and Lonsdale and George Reid and Bruce
Smith and Conroy doing likewise, also Johnson
and Kelly, and not forgetting Gibb of the Hume.
They are simply invited to produce that clause
of the Federal Constitution which forbids a


Federal graduated land-tax, and which thereby
shows that such a thing was never contem-
plated,” as Gould puts it. If they haven t a
copy of the Constitution, they can come to this
office and look at The Bulletin’s copy if they
like.
The “ S.M. Herald’s ” Hatred of Australian
Industries.
It is difficult to find any falsehood or misrepre-
sentation th#t is too gross for the Foreign-trade
papers when they think they see a chance of
injuring or obliterating an Australian industry,
destroying Australian capital, and depriving
Australian workmen of employment, lhis from
the Herald (26/11/’O6) is a sample:—
The local newspapers in the Hume electorate are giving
their readers a full, true and particular account of
William Lynb’s action in creating a monopoly tor
the Sunshine harvester. Mr. Lyne is so emphatic
in his denunciation of monopolies that the facts are
being brought home with additional force. William Dyne
admitted that before the additional duties were imposed
the harvester combine fixed the prices to be paid by
farmers. Eventually some of the members withdrew
from the combine, with the result that farmers were able
to buy their harvesters at £65. Since the re-arrange-
ment of the tariff, the price of harvesters has been fixed
for the next twelve months at £7O, £75, and £BO. The
result of this spoliation of the farmer is that a tremen-
dous profit goes into the pockets of Mr. M Kay, who
admitted that last year he made a profit of £30,000
on the sale of 2000 Sunshine harvesters. A Corowa hrm
claims that the increased duty is quite unnecessary, as
under the old duty of £5 they could sell harvesters at con-
siderably less than the price fixed by William Dyne, it
must take a good deal of explanation on his part to con-
vince the farmers of the Hume that he is doing them a
service by compelling them to pay an extra £5 for their
harvesters.
There are three points worth replying to here,
and they represent the whole of the Herald s
alleged case:—
(1) It is an outrageous falsehood to say that the dut.V
“created a monopoly for the Sunshine harvester,’ which
is M‘Kay’s implement. There are in Australia about a
dozen other firms besides M ‘Kay’s which make harvesters.
The Herald knows that perfectly well, for a list of the
names was published in the Sydney papers only a few
days ago. A man can’t have a monopoly when he has to
meet a lot of competitors. The Herald knows that also,
but its hatred of Australian manufactures makes it swipe
out at random. It doesn’t matter what it hits so long as
it is something Australian.
(2) The price of harvesters (largely imported) was from
£75 to £95 before Bill Lyne interfered with the tariff.
The price, as the Herald admits, is now, under higher
Protection, £7O to £BO. The alleged £65 was a paper
quotation which was made by the foreigners, apparently
for advertising purposes, when they found they were
going to be crowded out of the field. There is no
record that The Bulletin can find of any machines being
sold at that price, and even the Herald doesn’t say its
Corowa firm actually sold any. It says they could sell
them. That means nothing. The S.M. Herald could
give £20,000 to the funds of the Protectionist Association,
bat it doesn’t. , . , .
(3) The Herald says that M‘Kay “admitted making
a profit of £30,000 by running an Australian industry of
harvester-making. It would be well for the Herald to
say when he admitted it, and to whom. A few weeks ago
the Foreign-traders were charging M‘Kay with refusing
to state his profits. A little later they said the £30,000
was an “estimate.” To-day they say it is “admitted.
If M‘Kay does make that amount, it is cleaner money
than the reputed £50,000 a year of the Herald, made as
the organ of the party which tries to destroy Australian
industry. Curiously enough, the Herald never throws
mud at the many thousands a year made by the great
importing firms. It hasn’t a word to say about
the fact that, if its yarn is true, the foreign
harvester-makers who were flooding the Australian mar-
ket, and selling at exactly the same price as M Kay
before the latter made his recent reductions, must also
have made £30,000 on 2000 harvesters. That is all right
in the Herald’s view. It is only when an Australian
manufacturer is alleged to be making money that the
thing becomes utterly hateful in the Herald s eyes.

The Federal Stinking-Fish Party.
It is surely time that something stringent in the
way of solitary confinement or hard, labor should
be done to that unscrupulous gang of selfish and
unpatriotic Tories which is filling the atmosphere
just now with venomous, anti-Federal lies and
perversions. This party is foaming at the mouth
because the Australian Parliament is more demo-
cratic and progressive than the most advanced of
the State Parliaments ; and because it is begin-
ning to “ think in continents ” instead of meditat-
ing in parishes. The anti-Federal party s speakers
are continually referring to Federation as a
failure” or “an experiment” ; and they moan at
great length, in the anti-Federal press, over the
awful prospects of a deluded country. In addi-
tion to these more or less open attacks, a lot of
underground work is being done. The country
press is used for all it is worth. Leading and
other articles, with a savage anti-Federal bias,
are being supplied free, and the silly country jour-
nals are gaily helping to cut their country s throat
by publishing them. A sample from just one of
these “supplied ” leading articles
It is the fact that the Commonwealth Government has
fallen so entirely short of the great expectations on which
it was built—that it has been nearly six years in existence,
and has done little or nothing to advance the most press-
ing interests of the nation and the people It has done
absolutely nothing to save the States a penny by the
anpointment of a High Commissoner, or by the federation
of State debts Its willingness to launch into a trans-
Australian railway scheme of £5,000,000, and other ex-
pensive projects. , i • i i
This is a fair sample of the lying rubbish with
which the country is now being flooded. Taking
the last three items seriatim
When the Commonwealth Parliament spoke about
appointing a High Commissioner, it suggested that the
States could then save money by doing away with their
present Agent-Generals, who are all expensively dupli-
cating each other. The reply was that the Australian
Government could mind its own business; and, out of
six States, only two would even look at the business-like
offer. Also, the Federal Government was accused of
“ reckless extravagance.” .
Everyone knows that the whole obstacle in the way of
federalising the State debts is the impish and insect-like
opposition of the State Governments. Only the other
day Bent said that he didn’t want the Commonwealth to
bother about his debts, as he was quite able to look after
them himself. All that has been accomplished is the re-
sult of hard and tactful work on the part of the Federal
Treasurers.
The transcontinental railway is a highly-necessary
undertaking, as its opponents would soon find out it a
foreign nation landed an army at Perth. But the Aus-
tralian Government hasn’t expressed its willingness to
build it. It only asked for a survey ; and Parliament
hasn’t even granted that. So what is all the row about ?
And while State insects in the east are thus denouncing
the Australian Parliament for a willingness (which it
hasn’t shown) to build the railway, State insects in the
west threaten secession because it hasn’t built the rail-
way. What Australian Government could satisfy these
insects ?
A Parson Who is a Man.
What the average parson says when he preaches,
or where he goes to say it, is, on the whole, an
unimportant matter. The Church nowadays is
more or less of an effete institution in the spread-
ing of practical manly ethics. Its melancholy
priests are either preaching weird verbal tangles
regarding “doctrine,” or are screaming hysteri-
cally about harmless indulgences against which
the moral sense of the community refuses to revolt.
Yet all the while there are for parsons T or for any-
body else—magnificent opportunities in the work
of moral regeneration. In Australia there are tens
of thousands of men and women of a standard of
morals and intelligence which prevents them from
being the useful citizens they ought to be. ihey
are mostly people who are poor m this world s
goods. People who are well off and decently
educated are immoral and unworthy citizens,
because of viciousness or depravity, and many
who are poor are unworthy through ignorance.
At the Anglican Congress, the other day,
bishops and other things wailed that the
out-back parts of Australia abounded in men
who were indifferent to religion and ethics
and morality. To a certain extent their wail
was true. But how many bishops (and
things) do anything to alter that undesirable
state of affairs ? The parson arrives on a station ;
he talks to the squatter, and his wife, and his
daughter, and he gets it announced that he will
also preach a sermon to the men. Jrom his
superior niche between the squatter and the
squatter’s wife he preaches (generally patronis-
ingly) about the wickedness of being wicked. And
what the devil do (say) the shearers care about
him when his conduct says so plainly • What the
devil do I care about the shearers ? While the
Anglican parsons were discussing these thongs
there came along a Man—a parson, but a Real
Man. He was Brother Charles, of Dubbo
(N.S.W.), alias the Rev. C. H. S. Matthews, and
quoth he: —
One of the greatest obstacles is that the church is re-
garded as undemocratic. I don’t wonder at it, when
vou charge five bob—l mean five shillings; I forgot 1
was speaking in a town (laughter)-for peop e to come
to this congress and hear a lot of parsons speak ! I think
it’s disgraceful! The men where I come from say that
the church cares for the squatters and for those who
have got the money ; that the parson goes where he can
get the collection, but that he doesn't care for the work-
ing man. There is too much truth in it. What have we
done for the swaggies-the men who go about with
“ Matilda up ”or “ humping the bluey, ? My collea ue,
Mr. Campion, is forming a Wayfarers Union for them,
and they say, “ It is the first time the has ever
bothered whether we have souls or not.”
Again, to a very great extent, we are a foreign church.
We import our bishops when they are too old to learn
anything about the conditions of Australia. (Laughter
and applause.) I hope that the day is coming when we
will havenothingbut an Australian bishop foran Australian
diocese... They don’t care a rap whether you are High
Church or Low Church in the bush. They want men who
are in earnest. (Applause.) They want young Australian
priests to come out and work in the bush. There is a
great cry about importing Englishmen into the plum
parishes of Victoria. (Laughter.) But there is no out-
cry about importing them to the bush, where we get
Englishmen so stiff in the legs that they can hardly put one
leg over the saddle. (Laughter.) I hope that the day is
coming when we shall have an Australian democratic
church for Australian democratic people.
And then the Real Man sat down, and there was
a moment of embarrassment instead of a collec-
tion.
Re Charles Atkins.
Melbourne Age, in apology for discarding its
former-protege, Charles Atkins, re his candida-
ture for the Senate, offered the very reasons
which will commend him to a great many electors
as a desirable man. “ His whole life,” said the
Age, “ has been devoted to the pursuits of busi-
ness. He is the right stuff, but he is not ripe
enough. He may have a political future, seeing
that his face is turned in the right direction, and
that he has such a fund of energy on which to
draw. But, without deprecating him, he cannot
certainly claim a place beside three political
veterans such as Messrs. Styles, Fraser and
M‘Coll.” The truth is that Atkins, an alert,
middle-aged man of sanguine temperament,
is unripe only in his knowledge of poli-
tical tactics—the knowledge which soon
comes to members of Parliament, and which
the best of them are sorry to have to acquire. As
a business expert, he has proved himself the most
luminous Protectionist among the Senate candi-
dates. In dealing with Tariff anomalies, his all-
round quotations from the volume of commercial
facts are equally apt and numerous. With the
question of an Australian Navy looming large in
the future, and the troubles of marine labor
always demanding attention, there is a particular
reason why Atkins should be preferred to such
“ ripe ” —not to say rotten —politicians as Fraser
and M‘Coll, anyhow. He didn’t begin life in a
merchant’s office. Atkins had some years of
experience at sea in his youth and young man-
hood, and, even after he had opened a little office
in Melbourne, he worked his passage back to
London on an Orient boat witn a view to
more business. If there’s anything of a shipping
Labor vote in Victoria, it should certainly go to
Charles Atkins, the ex-seaman, who, as a hard
and fast Protectionist, aims at restricting Aus-
tralian State imports, as far as possible, to the
freights of Australian coasters, and who would
be in sympathy with the toilers of the trade
afloat and ashore.

The Professional Schoolboy.
A marvellous development in professionalism
has taken place in Australian educational circles
in recent years. The professional schoolboy is
now a recognised institution, like the professional
footballer or the professional tote proprietor.
The moment a State school pupil distinguishes
himself he is pounced upon by the drummers
of the big public and private colleges, who
desire to secure his services, in order to obtain
records for their individual institutions.
The morning after a State school exhibition ex-
amination, it is safe to say that every successful
candidate will find sundry touts waiting on his
doormat before breakfast, eager to outbid one
another, and all determined that the hated con-
tinuation school —the State’s institution foi
secondary education—shall not secure the coveted
genius. They offer various inducements, such as
payment of train and tram fares, free lunches at
school, free books, and, in a few instances, parents
have asserted that the touts have undertaken
to refund to them the £8 yearly allowed by
the Government to the institutions which impart
secondary education to the winners of State
school exhibitions. But the public and private
schools go a step further than this. Not content
with bidding for brainy youngsters to boom their
educational methods, they chase just as eagerly
after promising athletes. Whenever a bullockmg
football follower or a tricky bowler is discovered
on the playgrounds of the State schools, his
parents will be waited on by a polite gentleman,
who will point out the advantages to be gained
by attending the Calithumpian College or the
Church of St. Judkins’ Grammar School. 1 roba-
bly the old man, with his clay-stained bowyangs
still round his moleskin pants, will remark that
it’s hard enough to get seven bob a day as it is,
and he’s about to apprentice Harold to a
slaughterman. Whereupon the urbane gentleman
will assure him that a little thing like that needn t
trouble him, as the school committee has scholar-
ships to give to promising pupils, and it needn t
cost Harold a brass farthing as long as he plays
up to form in the school eleven. If the old man
demurs, the free fare, free lunch, free book oner
comes along, and if this doesn t suffice for the pro-
spective loss of Harold’s wages m the blood-
manure business, an arrangement will be made tor
the reimbursement of expenses. V hether Harold
can read words of two syllables or add two rows
of figures matters not a scrap. As long as he can
kick four goals out of five shots or claim a batting
average of 20 he goes into the St. Judkins’ as a
bright youth worthy of the higher education, and
lives the life of a fighting cock, until his whiskers
become too manifest or the old man thinks it up to
him to earn more than the college can afford. Ihe
Vic. public and private schools, which are ex-
tremely loud in their howls for the preservation
of pure sport and amateurism, are maintaining
as fine an assortment of gladiators as the world
has seen since the Coliseum had “ Standing Room
Only ” hung on its doors.
A Find of Land.
Victorian Minister for Lands Mackey has made
a proposition for providing more land for settle-
ment. Between Victoria and S. Australia there
is a strip of No Man’s Land containing about
330 000 acres. Victoria says it owns this De-
batable Land, and S. Australia says it doesn t.
Anyhow, Victoria holds the land, but on an
agreement with S. Australia that none of it shall
be alienated in any way. That this agreement
was necessary was shown by the fact that just
before it was made, some friends of the Govern-
ment of the day acquired the fee simple of 20,000
acres of the best of this strip of country for a
mere song. Since the agreement was arrived at
grazing licenses, for terms of seven years, re-
vocable oil six months’ notice, have been issued,
and the land is of such a quality that the grazing
rights have been sublet for 2s. an acre per annum.
In this manner 190,000 acres have been leased ;
but the total rental that the State receives is only
£2700 a year. The tenants, however, are
not allowed to clear the land, and are not
allowed to cultivate it. Previous Ministers
have considered that the agreement with S.
Australia means that the Debatable Band must
not be interfered with in any way. Minister
Mackey, however, has fixed his eye earnestly on
the old document, and comes to the conclusion
that it does not forbid clearing or cultivation, so
long as there is no alienation. He has found
80.000 acres of first-class wheat-growing soil in
the Debatable Land, and this is to be cut up into
handy blocks, and let out on permissive occu-
pancy for seven years. The effect will be to
bring in an income of about £lO,OOO a year, and
to cause'an increase of about a million bushels a
year in the wheat production of the State. One
great object-lesson will be gained from this
settlement. The Tory party declares that if
agriculture is to prosper, the cultivator must own
the freehold. It declines to sell the freehold to
its own tenants, but it talks this kind of mullock
when it wants to buy its leasehold sheep run
from the State. The Debatable La,nd is a big
region in which there can be no private owner-
ship. So the experiment that is about to be made
there will be interesting.

S.M. Heralds “On the Land” column fairly
fizzed with indignation, the other day, at the
barrenness of the present session of JN.S.W.
Assembly in Class Legislation for the pro-
ducers.” One thing that vexed the Herald was
that the “producers” [i.e., the farmers) hadn’t a
Minister ali to themselves, instead of sharing one
with the mere miners who, of course, don’t pro-
duce anything ; and it was moved to speak these
burning words :
It is so plainly evident that the whole scheme is badly
in need of a thorough shaking-up and re-invigoration
from top to bottom that the neglect of the Government
and of Parliament clearly proves that the average politi-
cian regards the institution as a mere means of providing
matter for political speeches.
And then the old paper got even more infuriated :
A greater question is that of water conservation and
irrigation. Unless some of these matters receive atten-
tion, and are brought to a practical issue before the end
of the session, members who seek at the next election
for a continuance of the support of the producers should
certainly be asked to account for an absolutely barren
session so far as producers’ legislation is concerned.
Boiled down, the great anti-Socialist paper
shakes a hostile gamp at the Assembly and says :
“Give the anti-Socialist ‘producer’ more State
Socialism, and plenty of it, and give it darn
quickly, and come along mighty sudden with
Class Legislation, or you’ll be emptied out on
your heads.”

Theke was a great land-sale at l.umut (N.S.W.),
last week, and the Sydney papers headed it
“Closer Settlement.” At the sale were buyers
from S.A., Victoria, and all over N.S.W., and
farm land realised as much as £33 per acre, while
quarter and half-acre township allotments fetched
as much as £4O. The cause of all this great financial
whoop is that the Cootamundra-Tumut railway
runs right through the land. The State buiit
that railway, and didn’t charge the private land-
owner any of the cost, though the line doubled or
trebled the value of his property. Ihen it carried
the landowner’s produce so much below cost that
the line had to be subsidised out of the taxes,
largely paid by the town and city workers, to the
extent of £14,000 a year. This subsidy further
boomed up the value of his land. Next, the
State didn’t charge the landowner for his own
roads. They came out of loans and general
revenue ; that is to say, the city worker pays for
his own roads and streets in local rates, and also
pays in general taxes a large share of the cost or
roads in the country. This further subsidy again
boomed up the price of land. Therefore, because
the community paid all these things for the lan -
lord, it has to pay twice or three times as much
for the land as that land is fairly worth. „ And
the result is called “Closer Settlement. it
should be called “Close up to Robbery. And
when it is proposed to only partly counterbalance
all these aids, subsidies, grants, beggings and
steals with a graduated land-tax, the big land-
owner cries out that such a thing is con
tion.”

The Bulletin invites attention to the distress of
the Rev. Hopkins, of the South Sea Islands, as
voiced at the Church Congress the other day.
The Rev. Hop. is crying out against the repatri-
ation of Kanakas from Queensland, and urges the
Church to do something, probably with a view of
inducing Australia to abide by its Kanaka, ihe
reason why this good shepherd does not want
the Kanaka back on his fertile isles is that
he plays fire and brimstone with the local
Christian flock when he gets home, and already,
on the tiny isle of Malaita alone, there
have been 14 murders perpetrated by repa-
triated “boys.” Worse still, the returned
laborers, with guns on their shoulders and watches
in their pockets and strange, eloquent curses in
their mouths, are accepted as chiefs by the un-
enlightened nigger, to the ruin of missionary
authority. This wail is in direct contradiction
with the hard sob of the piebald Australian
churchman to the effect that it was iniquitous to
ship the Kanaka back to his native isles, because
his unregenerate countrymen would batter the
intelligence out of him with a club as soon as he
landed. Likewise, the statement strikes the pie-
bald Australian churchman on the other ear, inas-
much as he pleads that the Kanaka should not be
excluded from the blessings of civilisation as
these things are in Queensland, and now it seems
that the church lias so neglected him in Queens-
land that he has acquired nothing there save guns,
rum and devilment. And the piebald church
is smitten on its third ear, inasmuch as lots of
parsons went up to look at the Kanaka in the
canefields, and put up at the planter’s house, and
heard his story, and went round with him, and
saw what he wanted them to see, and then came
back and gave the nigger a certificate of character
that only required a hole in the middle to be a
halo, which certificate now seems to have been a
foolish lie from first to last. These were the days
when the church said the Kanaka should remain
in Queensland because of his goodness. Now it
says he should remain because of his badness. It
always wants him to remain.
A little behind-the-scenes history of the har-
vester duties in the House of Reps., as told by
Government Whip, Hume Cook, is interesting.
A careful count indicated that the £l2 duty was
in jeopardy—it might go under, or it might get a
majoritv of two —and Hume Cook set out after
the Opposition Protectionists. McLean was
absent, and paired against the duty; McColl
was hostile, and only McCay remained. Ihe
Whip cornered the man who wants Crouch s
Corio seat, and asked him how he was going to
vote. Replied McCay : “I may as well tell you
that I have promised the Opposition to vote
against the Bill.” Then quoth the Whip : And
I may as well tell you that if you vote against the
duty, the Bill is lost, and I will see that public
attention is drawn to the fact that, although you
say you are'a Protectionist, you voted against a
Protectionist duty that would have been of great
value to the farmers.” Thereupon an alarmed
McCay scurried off, and had a long talk with his
deputy leader, Joe Cook, and seemingly that con-
scientious Freetrader gave him some politic advice,
for at the division McCay voted against his
deputy-leader, and for the duty. As it happened,
the Government didn’t want McCay th©' duty
was carried by tour votes. Still the episode is
interesting, as showing what a foolish-looking
person a politician may be when he sets up in the
“rat ” business. A final touch of humor comes in
the fact that McCay is now posing before the
Corio electors as a most enthusiastic supporter of
the harvester duties.
The Producers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The farmer man is right enough,
’Tis not for him I frame the curse,
But for the sempiternal bluff
That this good fellow, hale and tough,
Is holding up the universe—
That all the corn and all the oil
He’s busy tearing from the soil,
And that we grind his weary bones,
To feed the vicious city drones.
The tiller well performs his task
Who sows the field or tends the vine,
But artisans who make the cask,
Or mould the bottle or the flask,
They also help to make the wine.
And in the city, by his bench,
With chisel, hammer, saw and wrench,
The man who builds the stripper true
Is something of a farmer too.
The old petition, “ Speed the plough,”
Too oft asserts the narrow creed
Of canting fools who won’t allow
That as the tool is fashioned now
The city worker finds the speed ;
And like an old and spavined cow,
Would be the farmer’s wooden plough
For slowness, if ’twere not for these
Despised “unnatural” industries.
The farmer all the bards admire,
And sing him monarch of the soil,
But in the foundry, by his fire,
The iron-worker may aspire
To rank a swarthy king of toil.
He beats to shape the grand machine
That cuts the furrows deep and clean,
And he will plough, when all is done,
Ten acres to the farmer’s one !
The clerk behind his counter may
Be helping on the oil and corn,
The smith is making butter pay,
And at the titter’s bench to-day
Some fifty million sheep are shorn.
So, Hayseed, be not proud, I beg,
Nor heed the rogues who pull your leg.
For in the arid city street
We too are growing Mallee wheat.
VIC.
EDWARD DYSON.

SYDNEY
More Petriana.
The S.M. Herald, on Tuesday, discovered another
horrible Petrianlt affair in the treatment of the
shipwrecked Chinese who were on board the
steamer Australian. The Telegraph, with ex-
actly the same information at its disposal, curiously
enough failed to see any great horror in the circum-
stances, and printed them in rather smaller type
than it gives the prize pumpkin. The Herald’s
screech was to the effect that 25 shipwrecked
people were refused a landing in Australia, and
were “hustled”on to “an old coal hulk,” and
moored off the land, where they were “huddled
together like animals among huge masses of
fuel.” That is the sober Herald’s way of
putting it. The Telegraph, which didn’t or
wouldn’t seize the chance to throw mud at
Australia in order to advance its party interests,
got the same facts, as before mentioned—
the whole information consisting of a brief wire to
the agents. Yet there wasn’t a word in it about
the Chinese being “ hustled,” there wasn’t a word
in it about them being “huddled like animals,”
and, instead of it being an “old coal hulk,”
it may be the newest thing in lighters for
all the message mentions. Likewise, the “huge
masses of fuel ” that the Chinese were cast
amongst exist only in the unpatriotic Herald’s
imagination. Further, instead of the Chinese
being “absolutely forbidden to land on our
shores,” they were, according to the Telegraph—
with the same information at its disposal as the
Herald had—brought by a Government steamer
from the wreck, “ and landed with other passengers
at Port Darwin .” From Port Darwin some of
their number appear to have gone to Thursday
Island, and there, in that place of mixed breeds,
where it would be next to impossible to sort these
men out from the crowd, they were accommodated
with floating quarters in the harbor pending the
arrival of the Changsha. Out of the mouth of its
Anti-Sosh fellow-sinner, the Telegraph, theS.M.
Herald is therefore convicted of gross prevarica-
tion, and of grosser exaggeration. It has been
proved once again an enemy of Australia—a
contemptible print that doesn’t hesitate to foul
and slander the country that keeps it, in order to
fool its readers into voting for its own political
joss.

G. H. Reid wobbled into Tamworth (N.S.W.),
the other day. and, according to S.M. Herald,
threw this off his chest
The Federal Parliament had no right tojburst up big-
estates. II they began to allow the Federal Parliament
to invade their State rights, and deal with them IN A
WAY THE CONSTITUTION DECLARED WAS AN
OUTRAGE, the day would come when they would not
like it.
That leaves G. H. Reid self-convicted as a wilful
perverter. If the Constitution did declare a
graduated land-tax to be an “ outrage ” — George
Reid knows well enough that it doesn't —there is
no such word as “outrage” in the Constitution
from first to last—then the said George Reid
wouldn’t foam so much. He would simply, when
the tax came along, take a case to the High
Court, point to the section of the Act with
“outrage ” in it, and get the whole thing annulled
as unconstitutional, and cover himself with glory
and fees in the process. It is just because there
isn’t any such clause to fall back on that the
Scarce Land party says wildly there is such a
clause—in hopes of persuading land-taxers that it
is no use attempting to do anything.

About a heavy smoker :
Chief Commissioner Tait has introduced into the Vic-
torian railways the worst feature of American railway
business—the secret rebate. Nothing in railway manage-
ment lends itself more easily to corruption, or creates
more discontent, or is more opposed to the spirit of a
State-owned department. Tait has done the thing
cleverly, from behind the ambush of his cigar, and no one
knew that the secret rebate existed until it had been in
force for some time. In the Western District, where
the big squatters dwell, there are two classes of
competition for the railways to face—the waterways and
the road. Tait set himself out to kill both. Agents—
special, confidential agents—were sent all through
western Victoria, interviewing the men with wool and
other produce to send to market. The schedule of
freight rates, fixed by the Commissioners under Act of
Parliament, was ignored, and the best rate the agent could
get was accepted as the freight rate for that particular man.
The consignors soon found out what was happening, and
those who had been left to pay the schedule rates raised
a howl, while even the cheap-rate folks wanted to know
why there were others who paid a cheaper rate still. The
whole system of secret rebate is unjustifiable in State-
owned railways. The railways are for the benefit of the
people as a whole, andif the Departmentcan afford to carry
one man’s wool at a low rate, it can afford to carry all
wool at that rate. A State Department should never
be used for differentiating between individuals. Tait has
been mixed up with Private Enterprise so long that he
cannot be brought to see that he is not running an
“ octopus ” now. It is certain that he has not by any
means heard the last of the present trouble. Unless
Tait puts his cigar down for a bit, and alters his methods
considerably, his days promise to be shorter in the land
than he expected.
