“ Squidge ” on “ the Chow and the Law ” ;
Premier Bent’s attempt to protect the white men en-
gaged in the furniture trade of Victoria from the unfair
competition of the Chows provoked the usual storm of
opposition from the strongholds of sweating the
churches and the furniture warehouses. As a counter-
blast to the God-and-Mammon deputation that urged the
Government to take special care of its yellow berruther,
and allow him to eat as far as he chose into the vitals of
Victorian industries, a deputation of white employers
and employees in the furniture trade asked the Anti-
Sweating League to support Bent, and the League
appointed a committee to investigate the conditions of
the trade. This committee took evidence all over the
country, and heard both sides impartially, but the Chows
convicted themselves out of their own mouths. They
showed that the Chinese section of the trade had evolved
a system of work in direct contravention of the Fac-
tories Act, and the decisions of the Wages Board.
The first provision made under the Wages Board was one
restricting' work in the trade to day labor. It was
thought that as the Chows would have to receive the
minimum wage, the white man would then meet them
on equal terms. But the Chow, with his usual indiffer-
ence to other people’s laws, ignored that provision, and
nearly all the Chinese are on piece-work, and earn much
below the minimum fixed by the Wages Board, and far
less than would suffice to keep a man, according to the
white standard of living. The hours were fixed at 4S per
week. But the Chows work from daylight to dark in
their little hovels, and always work on Sundays and sta-
tutory holidays. The result is that the. furniture
trade is passing out of the hands of Australians. The
cost of making the Chow obey the law would be enor-
mous. It would take an army of spies and inspectors,
and even then the Chow would onl} r more obstinately
refuse to obey. He has been given a chance, and
a long chance, and he has ignored it. There-
fore, the committee before-mentioned came to the
sensible conclusion that either no more Chows should be
allowed to enter the trade, or else the number employed
in it should be limited, and rigidly kept within the limit.
At present the law requires all Chow-made furniture to
be stamped so that it may be recognised ; but furniture
is made all oyer Victoria by Chows and sent down to
Melbourne without any distinguishing mark, to be sold as
Australian-made. It is admitted that the Chow can’t be
made to obey our laws, so he should be treated like any
other confirmed law-breaker, and barred from the busi-
ness in which he won’t keep straight. He should, at the
least, be dealt with like a hotelkeeper who won’t keep the
law—persistent offending should lose him the chance of
breaking the law, by altogether barring that calling to
him. We make our own discredited white Bung take
that medicine ; and the worst Bung is no greater public
danger than the Chow who brings the white cabinet-
maker and his family to the point of starvation.

It is as well just now, when the Foreign-
trade and Dear Land party is spinning many
weird fairy yarns about the harvester duties, for
the Australian elector to refresh his memory as to
the methods by which foreign harvester manu-
facturers tried to throttle the Australian industry.
This slab from the evidence given by Mr.
Lincoln Holland Cowles, general manager of
the International Harvester Company, to the
Tariff Commission, at Melbourne, in January,
1906, may supply a little of the necessary re-
freshment :
Did your company in Australia have anything to do
with the distribution of a special issue of the Reformer
on the harvester question? We furnished some of the
information embodied in that article.
Did you request its publication? According to my
recollection it was first suggested to us by the editor of
the Reformer.
Name, please ? Mr. Hirscii.
Did you enter into any arrangement with Mr. Hirsch
as to payment of any of the cost of this ? We did.
Will you tell us how much you contributed to the cost
of this special issue? I cannot tell you off-hand.
Examination of Mr. Cowles was continued next day :
, Can you give that now ? Yes ;it isas follows : Amount
paid to Reformer. Answering question asked by Mr.
Higgs, I beg to advise that we paid on account of the
special edition of the Reformer the sum of £l9O 12s. 4d.
How many copies of the special issue did you receive
for £l9O 12s. 4d. ? I think we had 15,000. That is sub-
stantially the number.
Did you distribute these copies at your own expense ?
Yes.
This little monetary transaction helps to explain
at least some of the Australian journalistic fury
against the harvester duties. Mr. Max. Hirsch,
by the way, the man who, at the bidding of
American gold, tried to destroy a great Aus-
tralian industry, now wants the Wimmera (Vic.)
electors to return him to Parliament, presumably
to continue his good work there. But Hirsch’s
chance is a footless stocking without a leg.
Two serious things have, within the month,
happened to the Danvsz experiments, undertaken
to determine whether there can be introduced to
rabbits a fatal disease uncommunicable to other
animals. The experiments are being carried on
at Broughton Island (N.S.W.), where Dr. D.anysz
and three others contracted a disease described as
ptomaine poisoning. It is said that the eating.of
mushrooms was responsible. If so, the disease
with which the doctor is experimenting may be
found not guilty. Still, four days before the
report was published a rumor was circulated that
there was sickness on the island, and that calls
for investigation. The second awkward thing
is the arrival of a report from the Bureau
of Government Laboratories in the Philippines,
stating that draught cattle there have developed
haemorrhagic septicaemia. Now, haemorrhagic
septicaemia is the disease with which Dr. Danysz
hopes to destroy rabbits, and which, he says, is
not communicable to other animals. A medical
writer in Faulding’s Medical Journal (Ade-
laide) says—this was before the Danysz party
suffered from mushroom-eating : —-
. The two medical reporters in question state that there
is not the slightest doubt that organisms resembling
those of haemorrhage septicaemia are found in rivers and
in soils in the Philippines.
This must add to the onus which will have to be
borne by any authority that permits the distribu-
tion of the Danysz disease in Australia.
In cases in which suspended or abolished
solicitors make application to N.S.W. Full Court
for re-instatement, Chief Justice Darley now
makes it his first query, “ Has the money been
paid back?” The question, to judge by the
ghastly expression on the countenance of the
applicant’s counsel, is usually a disconcerting
business. One barrister is said to have fallen
back in his chair and feebly demanded a glass
of water when asked if the money had been paid
back. Time was in N.S.W. when being struck off
the rolls was like the life sentence which expires
in 15 years—after a certain period ex-Gentleman
One applied to be re-instated, and, on his counsel’s
assurance that there was no affidavit against the
re-admission, he came back automatically. Nobody
asked then if the money had been paid-in fact,
no one sterued to expect that it had.

Meynell and Gunn are great on matri-
monial pieces. Their first production at Sydney
Criterion was “The Fatal Wedding ” ; then came
the “ Rake’s Wife,” and on Saturday night the
upheaval was named “ The Midnight Wedding.”
With which of these samples of matrimony
the “Little Stranger,” who appeared recently at
the Palace, was connected is not known. The
matrimony which was done in the dead waste and
centre of the night is by Walter Howard, listed
on the programme as the author of “ Why Woman
Love Men” and “Under the Russian Flag.”
Walter seems to make plays on the simple plan by
which Hermes acquired cattle, for the nocturnal
marriage is in the main a re-hash of Stanley
Weyman and Anthony Hope. The story is
located in the Kingdom of Savonia, where Prin-
cess Astrea von Strelsburg, bound by her father’s
will to either marry Captain Rudoif von
Scarsbruck, a villain, or take the veil, does
neither. There is no alternative, which is why,
when she is discussing the situation with her con-
fessor in the private chapel of the Straddle bugs,
and Paul Yalmar, sword master to the Red
Hussars, comes through the window in a wild
hurry to esca,pe from the consequences of having
run his toasting-fork through his superior officer
(Astrea’s brother), the confessor insists that he
shall marry the lady. He has never seen her
before, and she doesn’t know him from a crow,
and his sword is stained with the blood of her
own brother. Nevertheless she marries him, and
Paul hurries on to escape the penalty of outraged
discipline. When at last he is brought before the
Croton Prince of Savonia, Paul, very naturally,
turns out to be the potentate’s own son by a mor-
ganatic marriage. The rest of the drammer tells
how these two curious people come to love one
another through the plots of Scarsbruck and
Astrea’s brother to kill Yalmar and get the Prin-
cess, who seems to have the family splosh, married
off again. The comic relief is mostly supplied
by a private soldier and a barmaid, who
exchange their clothing in sentry boxes, and by
soldiers who talk about yellow-stockinged girls
in wine gardens. In the end the Crown Prince
becomes the King. In his presence Valmar and
the chief villain fight a duel, which he watches
very unemotionally in company with Astrea, and
Scarsbruck is killed. Scarsbruck falls very artis-
tically, and Valmar declines to became Crown
Prince, because he is very much in love with
Astrea, and though the lady is a princess, her
blood, for some complicated reason, isn’t suffi-
ciently royal to make her a possible queen. At
this point a really scientific dramatist would have
the lady’s blood analysed by a competent chemist,
and he would put things straight by finding it to
contain the royal microbe in sufficient quantities ;
but this point is omitted.
* * * *
Take it by and large, “ The Midnight
Wedding ” is one of the best melodramas Sydney
has seen for a long while. The atmosphere of
Anthony Hope and Stanley Weyman is artistic,
and there is a sense of relief in getting away from
the squire and the old stock properties. Further-
more, the appearance of Harcourt Beatty in the
role of Paul Valmar gives that rather priggish
young man a reality and vigor with which the
author failed to provide him, and the parts of
Captain Scarsbruck and Lieutenant von Strels-
burg are excellently done by Charles Vane and
Conway Wingfield. As the Crown Prince
Leopold, Herbert Leigh tramps around with
royal dignity. Miss Dina Cooper doesn’t get any
opportunity to speak of, but makes a lot of what
she does get; and Miss May Congdon is cast in a
part that somehow does not seem attractive.
“The Midnight Wedding” will probably be a
boom—at least this paper lays a shade of odds to
that effect.

“The Little Stranger’s” run at Sydney
Palace will be brief ; the unpleasant little beggar
will be partly cooked in the monstrous hanging
lamp for the last time on Friday night, and then
“ Turned Up ” will be staged.
** * *
“ The Virginian ” will run to the end of
the Waldron-Humphrey season at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, though there seems to be a call for a
brief resurrection of “The Squaw Man,” apiece
which looks better the more you consider it.
Sometimes the front view of a drama as it is
coming towards you isn’t specially attractive, and
yet the back view, after it has departed, is full of
interest. Afterwards Sydney and Maoriland
swop Miss Ola Humphrey and Miss Tittell Brune.
The latter comes back to fill in with “ La Tosca”
till Christmas and “Parsifal” arrive. The latter
being far too heavy fare for any Australian who is
too young to have a vote, “Alice in Wonderland”
will frivol by day while “ Parsifal ” thunders by
night.
* * *
“The Lights o’ London ” are still twink-
ling at Sydney Royal over the woes of Walter
Baker Armytage and the sins of his villainous
cousin, Clifford Albert Norman. One grievous
fault about “The Lights” is the obscurity in
which the future of Hetty Preeneis veiled. Does
she get away with the Armytage diamonds, or
does she lose them and have to pawn the magnifi-
cent gown in which she gloats over the stolen
jewels while Pa Seth does his stretch in Dartmoor,
or wherever he expiated his monstrous crimes?
Of course, it is only surmise that Seth expiates
worth a cent; perhaps he gets off for turning
King’s evidence in the kerbstone court presided
over by the detective. The future career of a
business-minded girl like Hetty Preene should be
interesting. She is the ideal character to make a
success in a land of unrestricted Private Enter-
prise like England, and “The Lights” is doing
yeoman service at this election-time in showing
what deplorable characters are bred under Anti-
Sosh conditions. Walter Baker and the lodge-
keeper’s lovely daughter are good, painfully good,
and horribly incapable. Albert Norman, Arthur
Styan, and Hetty Ireland Preene are all ener-
getic, capable, and shockingly villainous. This
sad state of affairs will perhaps be remedied
now that England has got a Labor party.
Madame Amy Sherwin will give three
®* tra concerts this week at Sydney Y.M.C.A.
Hail, and no one who isn’t seriously ill or in gaol
ought to miss hearing her, and the pair of gifted
boys who help to make the concerts a crowded
two hours of melody. Basso Gauge is growing on
his audiences—-at first his voice startled them
some, as did the Saxon legions sweeping over
■Britain—but his management of his finely-trained
squad of notes and legions of tones has conquered
criticism. Besides, the girls have discovered
that his teeth and ties are first-class. Pianist
Harvey, too, has unfolded a reverential feeling in
his playing. He studied for the R.C. priesthood
tor some years, and has had a cathedral in his
voice ever since.
Newest thing at the Tivoli is more freak
music this time aluminium bars, out of which a
gloved hand rubs melody of much the same sort
as the ungloved hand used to produce from the
musical glasses.” The lady of the Stewart-
Lorraine musical pair is responsible for this deft
performance. For the rest, the Alaskas, “Fisli-
ing, ’and Cassie Walmer are in the bill; and a
bill holding those three items is always worthy of
respect.
** * *
A revival of “Dr. Wake’s Patient,” at
Melbourne Princess’s, shows a marked improve-
ment in the performance of a somewhat excellent
comedy-drama, and this improvement mitigates
the artificial effect of the plot whilst enhancing
the charm of the dialogue. The second act of
“Wake,” with its frank and unaffected comedy
scene in the doctor’s waiting-room, and the short
after-scene in his solemn sanctum, where he earns
a large income by prescribing simple remedies for
dyspeptic bishops—the second act is now as bright
as the best work ever associated in Australia with the
honored name of Brough. Four women characters
—one of them, Miss Temple’s, being merely inci-
dental to one scene—are played to admiration.
Mrs. Brough has settled down comfortably into
the part of the Countess, who derives nerve-
nourishment from a smelling bottle, and bewails
the awful impossibility of her light-hearted
daughter. Miss Day, ever on the up-grade of her
acting, gives new interest to the robustly senti-
mental Lady Gerania; also. Miss Gordon Lee is
more simply natural, yet not less incisive than
before. On a previous occasion the talk
of the three women in the doctor’s ante-room
was interrupted by a very effusive young man of
the modern ladylike English type. He reiterated
his catch-word, “ absolutely,” with great success,
but he made one sorry for the dear old Mother
Country. Moreover, he was too effeminate for
the part, which is now presented quite as humor-
ously by versatile Gregan McMahon, in whose
creation there is the desired suggestion of mas-
culine feeling. Such gussie mummers as the
original exponent of Duff Wynterden, and the
later new-comer who minces around in “Wig
and Gown,” are almost destitute of masculine
suggestion. They ought to be called Gwendoline
every time. The plot of “ Dr. Wake’s Patient ”
doesn’t improve on acquaintance, but in so far
as Herbert Flemming, Pickford, and even
McKeown, as the angular earl person, have taken
artistic steps towards the goal of probability, the
play looks all the better for its revival. It is
essentially a play to please the womenkind. Old
Mrs. Wake (Miss Major) makes a specially plump
and tender appeal to the maternal instinct of the
she sex, and her heroic son is deeply beloved by
matrons in general because of his having presented
“Mawther” with a spring mattress, which she
found very soothing, although it caused her to
“ bounce a bit ” at first.

Afc Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the bird of
giddy omen seems to have moulted a feather from
its narrative as the result of the regrettable illness
of Reg. Roberts. Last week the invalided tenor
was replaced by his understudy—a placid warbler,
with no grain of ginger in his composition. On
Monday of this week Arthur Crane was to take
up the advocate’s robe of the original Gustave, and
burst into conjugal infidelity the moment he had
shaved off his beard. If Crane can cheer-
fully impersonate a learned Chicken who goes
astray in the Spring, the mixed entertain-
ment at Her Majesty’s may be guaranteed to
continue until the pantomime season sets in.
Tuneful songs and the quaint climax of the
first act have decided Melbourne in favor of a
show which merits the severest disapproval of
Judkins and his fellow moral endeavorers.
** * *
Considering that “ The Power and the
Glory ” gives several members of the Anderson
Co. one or two chances to put a dramatic situation
on a reasonable basis, the highest interests
of Art will not be served by a withdrawal from
Melbourne Royal of the angelic apparition in
favor of “The Face at the Window” next Satur-
day (Ist). All the same, it is certain that “The
Face” will prove a shocking success. A number
of people who witnessed this gruesome spectacle
in the past have been shuddering ever since at
the recollection of it. On the other hand, “The
Face” is reputed to have cured several cases of
paralysis by making the patients jump, and the
trumpet of Fame has blown so many stories re
the marvellous results of Anderson’s drama that
public curiosity is raging high.

A musical event, dated for Saturday, at
the Town Hall, is another Wagner-Tschaikowsky
orchestral concert, to be conducted by Frederic
Beard. Two or three concerts and song recitals
have happened, or will shortly occur, at Glen’s
Hall. The dancing academicians are in their
annually excited state. The Misses Hyams
rally their pupils for a demonstration at
St. Kilda Town Hall this week, and Mrs. Gt ;en
promises a big display at the Town Hall on the
10th. Also, The Bulletin is reminded that
“Dreamland,” at St. Kilda Esplanade, invites
patronage every day until further notice. The
side shows, which were idle fancies a fortnight
ago, are being converted into realities one by one,
and the first faint indications of a Melbourne
summer will give “Dreamland ” a much-needed ad.

“ T.” writes :
Miss Lizzie Newbold, much boomed as a “ young pheno-
menal soprano,” gave a mild entertainment, labelled
“grand concert,” in Glen’s Hall (Melb.), last week.
Neither the occasion nor the singer justified such grand-
iloquence.
The phenomenon was supported by several other im-
mature performers, but she bore the brunt of the pro-
gramme on her own plump shoulders. She sang,
amongst other ambitious numbers, the “Shadow
Song” and “ Lo ! Hear the Gentle Lark,” which latter
was very suggestive of the concert itself. She showed
accuracy and facility of technique in her management of
an unexceptional voice, with a quaint falsetto range high
up on the ledger lines. The piping notes in altissiino
have the charm of novelty, hut that doesn’t atone to a
tired audience for mediocrity in the legitimate portions of
the work. Miss Newbold reproduced the phrasing and
interpretations of experienced artistes in all her efforts;
hut she didn’t once illuminate them with any spontaneity
or understanding. She merely sang with passionless
precision.
This concert w'as intended as a forerunner to a big
benefit which would enable Elizabeth to pursue know-
ledge and fame in the Cold Country. Well taught she is
now. The essential sympathy of voice she lacks in great
measure. Short-skirted and pig-tailed, on the model of
other more-or-less juvenile debutantes, Miss Newbold is a
pleasing figure, but that isn’t any reason w 7 hy the general
public should be induced to subscribe to an attempt to
send her to compete in an over-loaded market, with the
world’s best artists.

A pair of banjo players —Casselli and
James—are just now twanging the favorite
implement of nigger melody with exceptional
shill at the Opera House. The breezy club-
juggler and plate-shifter is another recent arrival
who finds favor, and would appear even more re-
markable if his name were not Selbo. Public re-
spect for assumed names that end in “o” has
been somewhat exhausted by the long strain upon
it. In other respects the Rickards bill gives no
cause for complaint. The Moto Girl is still a
draw, also Les Cabiacs ; and Duncan and Godfrey
have changed their dialogue. The new conversa-
tion is mainly conducted by Godfrey, who scores
a larf in several places.
X * X
About a show, from the Melbourne dra-
matic critic’s understudy :
Vic.’s Parliamentary Jubilee was a variety entertain-
ment. It opened with the depressing spectacle of 200 ex-
legislators assembling in the (Queen’s Hall and gazing at
each other as if to ask what the other fellow was
doing there. Nobody looks so hopelessly “ex”as an ex-
legislator. Half of them were elderly and well-dressed;
the other half of them looked as if they had strayed
out of the Salvation Army’s shelter by the back
door. After they had been standing sufficiently long to
wonder if they were forgotten, they were led into
the different Chambers. In the Council, centre-
man Wrixon said nothing in mellifluous tones, and
invited Mr. Fitzgerald, who had been sitting in the House
of Fossildom for 42 continuous years, to say the same
thing. Fitzgerald made the exactly similar remark in
the way anybody would expect from a person who had
been immured in the Council for 42 years. Then the
Council, past and present, arose and crowded into the
Assembly, where Bent was doing his best. Bent sur-
passed his effort on the occasion of Parson Worrall’s ap-
pearance at the bar of the House. “I remember
Butters there,” he said. “The first time I saw him
he had his whatyecallums on—those Scotch things—kilts 1
go on, not kilts—that other thing. You know. Yes,
that’s it—his sporran. What are yer grinning at,
Thompson Moore? Eh, what’s that? And I went home
and told my mother I’d seen a man with the finest pair
of legs I’d ever seen.” After Thomas had thrown off
statesmanlike stuff of this description for half-an-hour,
Wrixon said some more nice, mellifluous nothings, and,
just as the whole affair was degenerating into sheer
burlesque, Deakin came along and saved the situa-
tion. Meanwhile Gov. Keggie Talbottle had assembled
in the hall, and Wrixon presented him with the address
to the King, which had been carried by both Houses.
Talbottle wasn’t supposed to have seen the address till
then, yet he read a cablegram, said to have been already
received from Edward VII. in reply. This cast a gloom
of suspicion over the scene. It was too much like Wren’s
alleged cablegram from Jack O’Brien. Then, when people
were just wondering whether anything else was going to
happen, one newspaper man tried to collar the sacred
copy of Ned’s message from another newspaper man,
and the gilded assemblage was treated to the
view of a short, fierce struggle, in which one
scribe got an inky twist on the gullet of his reptile
contemporary, and compelled him to let go a badly-
crumpled piece of gilded vellum just as his eyeballs were
about to spill on the red baize floorcloth. Before the
combatants could be struck dead or immured in a for-
tress, they disappeared, and the crowd, which had turned
up to see some fun, and hadn’t got anything else but the
scrap, rushed the refreshment rooms and made
the waiters lead the strenuous life. There was
pathos as well as shrieking humor in the scene.
Some of the pathos was supplied by the presence of poor
old barrister Fisher, now an inmate of the Austin Hospital
for Incurables. It is difficult to imagine any place where
an ex-member could be more completely “ex than a
hospital for incurables. It represents an obliteration
that is more obliterative than the grave.

The Gilbert and Sullivan Co. has passed
on from Adelaide Royal, much regretted.
“Utopia” was splendidly dressed, and, being a
complete novelty, filled the city with enthusiasm.
Howard Vernon played with the brightness and
energy of 20 years ago, and his wife showed un-
expected powers of comedy. Miss Olive Godwin,
who is getting splendid chances, played the Prin-
cess. She also had the lead in “Princess Ida,”
for Dolly Castles again stood out, though Dolly
obliged with a song on the farewell night, when
Henry Bracy—the gallant Prince of the original
production at the Savoy, London, in 1884 came
in to take the part of Cyril. The Royal is now
occupied by a moving-picture enterprise on a
large scale, and this affair says it is going to show
the rest of the world what Australia is like.
Adelaide streets were duly taken one afternoon,
when sundry artful individuals, and somebody s
thirty-shilling suits, obtained much bold adver-
tisement by fleeing across the path of the camera.
Outdoor evening shows are becoming frequent,
hut the weather, stormy and un-November-like,
has discouraged them a good deal.
It costs but a small amount to keep Cham-
berlain’s Colic, Cholera, and Diarrhoea Remedy
always on hand, and it is economy in the end.*
“J.A.C.”: “Attacked by Octopi 1 ’ is
S.M. Hekald’s heading of an up-to-date fish
story, concerning cuttle-fish. “ Octopi ”is good ;
I don’t know what it is, but it is good. “Octo-
puses” is the English plural of “octopus”;
“ oktopodoi ” would probably be the Greek, and
“octopedes” the Latin, if there were any such
word in these languages. But there isn’t; in
both languages the name of the creature is
“sepia.” There is in Latin a word “octipes,”
signifying an eight-footed animal, but not a
cuttle-fish. An octopus is not an octopus, but a
decapus, for it has ten tentacles. Ido not give
all this as gospel, for my Latin and Greek are
shaky. But “octopi” is barbarous; perhaps it
is Choctaw.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“J.A.C.”: And yet another! Word comes from
London of the death of Wybert Reeve, at the age of 75.
Fully a third of these years he spent in Australia, but
for a much longer period he was pretty widely known
as an intelligent actor, not brilliant but consider-
ably above the average. I first remember him about
the end of ’77 or beginning of ’7B, “starring” in the
English provinces, and about a year later I ran across
him a <r ain in Maoriland. He was best known thiough-
out Australasia as Count Fosco, in his own drama-
tisation of Wilkie Collins’ “ Woman in White,” arid,
as Henry Beauclerc in “Diplomacy,” an English
version of Sardou’s “Dora.” In the former character
he earned a well-merited reputation, although in appear-
ance he was singularly unlike the ideal Fosco. Wilkie
Collins describes that sinister personage as pluming him-
self on a certain resemblance to the first Napoleon, and
as in that assumption, always appearing clean-shaven,
while Reeve would never sacrifice his moustache, which
was ornamental. Yet he satisfied the author, with whom
he was well-acquainted, and concerning whom he recently
published some reminiscences, for Reeve could write as
well as act. When he first came to these parts he was
a bit upper-crustish and stand-offish, but it was only a
veneer and soon wore off and revealed a genial nature.
He however, could never stand any vulgarity on the
stage, and when the late Sam Poole, who was not the
pink of refinement, was once engaged to support him, he
was not delighted. The company was producing “ All
For Her,” and Reeve was one day conducting a rehearsal.
He was born in London, and never got over the Cockney
aversion to the letter “ r.” In explaining relative positions
on the stage, Reeve said to the leading lady, “ You stand
theah, t.hechildisonhawkneesheah,andyou,MistahPoole,
theah ’’ pointing up stage, “ with yaw back to the audi-
ence.” “ Back to the audience ” didn’t suit Poole, who
got on his dignity and replied, “ What, heah ! No blanky
feah ! ” and walked away, which little diversion cost Sam
his engagement. For a number of years Wybert Reeve
was lessee of the Adelaide Theatre Royal. He also repre-
sented the Dramatic Authors’ Society, and in that
capacity put a spoke in the wheel of numerous brigands
who had been drawing fees for plays to which they
had no earthly right. Reeve wore a jasey, an unmis-
takable jase.v,'which never could pass muster for any-
thing else. (I may here mention that “jasey”—l don’t
guarantee the spelling—is theatrical slang for “wig,” but
for a wig worn in private, not on the stage. A stage wig
is simply a “ wig,” nothing more.) He had two of those
adornments, one a trifle longer than the other. On the
approach of summer he would pass his hands through the
flowing locks and say, “Getting very wawm; must get
my ha-ah cut,” and next day on would go the shorter-
haired wig of the two.

“ But, tell me Frankly what do you weally think
of Mr. Watson's pwesent allitude? ”
The late Wybert Reeve always used to claim that it was
he who gave Henry Irving his first real chance. A lead-
ing juvenile was wanted for “ The Belle’s Stratagem ” at
Manchester. Reeve had the offer of the part; but he
was fully engaged at the time, so he said, “ Give that
young Irving a show,” and they did.
Strange coincidence that Oily Deering and Wybert
Reeve should have died in the same week. Oily was a
member of Wybert Reeve’s co. what time the English
actor was playing his first few seasons in Australia,
where he subsequently went into semi-retirement.
Reeve introduced two of Pinero’s plays—the earliest—to
this country, and in one of them, “ The Squire,” the
quaint comedy part of Old Gunnion fell to Deering.
Wybert Reeve’s last effort in playwriting, probably,
was his adaptation of an Italian farcical comedy which he
produced at Melbourne Bijou twenty years ago under the
title of “ Only Dust.” Maj'eroni did the translation from
the Italian and Reeve wrote it up to its English setting,
playing the principal part himself. Other old identities
who figured in that rather feeble piece were Signor
Majeroni, Henry Harwood, J. R. Greville, and Immense
Augustus, otherwise Gus Glover, all dead now.
“F.” sends another Wybert Reeve biography:—
« Wybert Reeve, who died in London last week, was the
best English-reputationed actor of all the old Australian
stage identities, with the exception perhaps of George
Rignold. Reeve was a cockney, born in 1831, and as far
back as ’52 he made a little mark as a dramatic author.
He produced a farce under the prophetic title of ‘ An
Australian Hoax.’ In after years, he worked many Aus-
tralian hoaxes, notably his three delusive wigs with which
he almost succeeded in concealing his age from himself.
He changed his wigs on a moonlight system. There was
the beauteous ‘ half moon ’ or moderately hairy wig, giving
place in four weeks to the ‘ full moon,’ or long-haired wig.
Another month passed, and Wybert, passing his hand
through his thickened locks, would say ‘By Jove 1 I
must get my hair cut.’ Next day he emerged from the
barber’s inner sanctum with an absolutely convincing
‘ new moon,’ or hair-cut wig, the sprucest veteran young
man in the theatrical profesh. But to resume. Wybert
Reeve was still a young man when he started to run
his own show and stage his own literary efforts, and he
seemingly had something of a name in London before he
made a 'big success as Count Fosco in Wilkie Collins’s
drama, ‘ The Woman in White.’ The other Fosco fell
ill, and Reeve took his place. He had played the charac-
ter more than 1500 times in Britain, America, and Canada
before he came to Australia, a man of 50 or thereabouts.
Although he stayed some years in Australia he didn’t
linger long on the stage, and the present generation in
Sydney and Melbourne knew him only by name as the
lessee of Adelaide Royal before he went back to his birth-
land to write his reminiscences, and, in due course, to
die. He presumably allowed himself to go bald ere he
reached the good old age of 75.”
The influence of the local German autocracy in Mel-
bourne musical criticism could be detected or fairly
suspected —in the Argus notice of Signora Dane-Valenti s
song-recital. Miss Nora Dane, of yore, went to Europe
with a valuable Australian voice, which hadbeen leu -
tonised in its production by a course of training at the
University Oonservatoriuni, what time Marshall Hall was
the Professor and Madame Wiedennann schooled the
singing- pupils. In Europe she studied a littie while with
Marchesi before reconstructing her warble on orthodox
Italian lines, and now that she is back in Melbourne as a
Signora, from whose vocal method the old Conserva-
torium brand has been completely erased, she gets no
enthusiastic praise from the German musical govern-
ment. The Age seemingly ignored her song recital, and
the Argos man wrote in disparagement of her biggest
efforts (the operatic numbers) as compared with her
singing of little songs in German or English, two of the
latter being very trivial compositions by Madame Wie-
dermann’s daughter. This unhandsome criticism of
Nora Dane-Valenti, an artist travelling on her recognised
merits, has been contrasted with a recent glowing Argos
notice of Madame Maggie Stirling. Lady Northcote and
Lady Talbot were present at Madame Stirling’s recital,
and the Argus man enthused accordingly. “It was a
perfect feast of song, such as we have not had since
Mdile. Dolores left us. .. . As a singer of Lieder and
chansons and ballads, Madame Stirling need not feat-
comparison with anyone. Her voice is rich and resonant
and of exceptional compass, and her interpretations are
all full of genuine artistic feeling.” These utterances of
the oracle should be borne in mind by Melbourne people
who have heard the one singer since her return from
Europe, but have not yet heard the other.
The majestic calm of Asia was disturbed and
broken up by the overthrow of Buttan Singh in Mel-
bourne the other night. One large buck Hindu provided
much amusement by his antics at the back of the
“ stage.” He was about 6ft. 4in„ and must have weighed
16 stone in his socks. He had as much black hair about
him as a bull buffalo, and his bunch of coiled whiskers
would have upholstered a train. He was a truly for-
midable object, and his elephantine antics when Buttan
got first fall upset furniture and imperilled the lives and
limbs of inoffensive citizens, while his roars shook the
building. When Buttan was thrown and staggered to
his corner all out, a beaten nigger, the huge Asiatic was
struck dumb. Suddenly a small boy in his vicinity
attracted his attention. The boy was about thirteen,
and undersized at that. He was shouting: “The white
man’s won ! Hurrah, the white man’s won ! ” This was
too much for the Indian ; he uttered the bellow of an
infuriated bull, and bore down on that boy like a
mammoth on a grub. “What you say?” he yelled.
“ What you say? White man win ! I fight you myself.’
The boy dodged, and some men got in the way. “Yes,
yes,” thundered His Whiskers, “I fight you myself!”
There was a scuffle, and the hoy backed away, and fell off
the staging, and Asia was foiled again.
“Squidge”: There isn’t enough money put up yet to
allow George Musgrove to engage artists for his grand
opera season. He is to make contracts with enough prin-
cipals for a double company, a la Oarl Rosa. Conse-
quently Dukes of Hesse Darmstadt and Kaiser Bills, and
other long-named folk, are giving permission to Mussy to
engage their pet artists ; but Mussy, although he has
made all his arrangements, even to the color of the flower
he will wear in his coat on the opening night, cannot
definitely fix a single singer. The secretary of the Aus-
tralasian Grand Opera Co., Limited, has to resort to
insidious paragraphs in the papers suggesting that if the
public really wants to hear the very grand opera co. at
all, it must hurry along and drop its £2 into the box.
Face is that people who buy shares like to see a chance of
profit behind the scheme ; and people who really dote on
grand opera are usually a dreamy, unpractical lot, with-
out the price of a drink in their clothes.
The death of Miss Mabel Lane recalls her last appear-
ance at Melbourne Royal. She was supposed to have
been incarcerated in a lunatic asylum by the villain, her
husband. She escaped, and, having dashed vitriol in his
face, she raved for about five minutes, and afterwards
wandered in and out without any visible means of sup-
port. It was the irony of Fate that kind-hearted, bright-
eyed Mabel Lane, who entered the profesh with high and
justifiable hopes of presently starring as Juliet, should
have finally quitted the stage iii the character of an awful
victim of Andersonian bellowdrama. circumstances.
“Ven ”: The Melbourne girl has been accumulating
superfluous sentiment ever since the departure of the
last beauty mummer. There is now absolutely no one on
the boards in Melbourne on whom she can lavish her
affection—not one has the power to give her “ that ten-
der twilight feeling.” Doesn’t J.O.W. see the want ?
“Shooting the Chute" has had a brief, inglorious
career at Brisbane, and has now shot into liquidation. It
lived a hare six months at Earl’s Court before it died, and
it was really dead most of the time it professed to be alive,
but was merely shooting to save the burial expenses of
its chute. In fact, it was horn dead, and it is merely as a
matter of courtesy that it is alluded to as living during
the six months in which the corpse uttered an occasional
remark. Brisbane never took kindly to the chute, and
several enterprising Valley tradesmen are left to lament
a ghastly investment. They are solvent men, however,
and the late lamented will pay twenty tombstones in
the £.
“ f.” writes : Apropos Myra Kemble. This lady was at
Melbourne Theatre Royal long before she ever saw
Sydney, or Sydney saw her. She was brought into pro-
minence by mummer Harry Leston, at a benefit given to
clown Tom Lewis. Harry and Myra did an Irish act
together. Manager Harwood advertised the pair as
“ Harry Leston and his pupil Venus.” In that pantomime
she used to recline on a large fern leaf as Venus, and was
lowered from the flies in the transformation scene. Miss
Kemble’s first appearance in Sydney was at the Theatre
Royal, when it was opened by Sam. Lazar, in 1875.
H. O. Willard, the long-standing- villain of Anderson’s
dramas, will sneer no more upon any stage, poor fellow !
The strange “skating gait” and evident feebleness of
Willard during the last months of his stage usefulness
were explained by a wasting disease which is said to have
shrivelled one of his legs until it is now some inches
shorter than the other. The afflicted actor, who was a
very competent and somewhat picturesque performer in
his wicked line of business, has been “ resting ” at Ade-
laide of late, and, if his misfortune were announced by a
matinee benefit at Melbourne Royal, the public, Ander-
sonian and otherwise, would doubtless be glad to sup-
port it.
“ Cuss ”: Re Dan Demosthenes O’Connor and his 5000-
caser proposition (B. 8/11/’O6). I opine that the Spald-
ing referred to in the cable is Al. G. Spalding, baseball
magnate and impresario, who flirts with prize-fighters,
cake-walkers, freak-lecturers —any old thing that he
thinks he can shake a dollar out of. I figure that he is
running Dan as the great Irish-Australian song and dance
artist (with a French accent, acquired at great expense
from Sara Bernhardt). Spalding holds the fort at
Chicago.
Annette Kellermann, the Australian mermaid, is now
travelling about Scotland with a circus, but is not
rejoicing much in a success that entails having the ice
rubbed off her shapely form after every turn.
ROBERT HOWE, who runs against William
Wilks, at Dailey (N.S.W.).
“ J.A.C-” : On October 9 died Adelaide Ristori, one of
the greatest actresses the world has ever seen. She was
buried in Rome on the 11th. Rome’s chief citizens
assembled to honor her memory, arid the circular vege-
table came from all parts. Ristori was born in Friuli
(Italy) in 1821, and was consequently 85 at the time of her
death. The stage was her inheritance. Her parents
were actors ; when three months old she was carried
on in a farce ; at four years she was playing children’s
parts; and at 12 she did soubrettes and sentimental
melodramatic heroines. At 20 she was appearing in
Goldoni’s comedies. At the age of 26 she married the
Marquis Capranica del Grillo, and retired from the stage,
but not for long. A casual appearance for a charitable
purpose brought with it a longing for a return to the
same old boards. But she determined to fly at higher
game than before, and pursued the tragic muse. She was
swift on her feet, and soon overtook it. In her new
capacity she made her debut in Rome, in 1849, in Alfieri’s
tragedy of “ Myrrha,” and was an overwhelming success.
For the next 25 years she toured Europe and America,
her fame ever increasing, playing such parts as Maria
Stuarda (Mary Stuart), Medea, Lady Macbeth, Giacomet-
ti’s Queen Elizabeth, Marie Antoinette, and the like.
She played a whole herd of queens in her time. In 1874,
being then 53 years of age, she set out on a tour of the
world, visiting South America, the Pacific Islands and Aus-
tralia. She was accompanied by her husband and children,
and a complete Italian company. Among her followers
were the Majeronis, husband and wife, afterwards so well-
known throughout Australasia, and a very excellent
actor with the very un-Italian name of Glech. Madam
Majeroni was Ristori’s niece, and her brother Tessero
(Ristori’s nephew) was also with them. Tessero remained
here for many years as a theatrical manager and agent,
and was also an hotelkeeper in Sydney and Melbourne.
He fell from being Ristori’s nephew to being Mr. Public-
house. There were also the two youthful Majeronis,
Mario and Giorgio, now fast making a name for them-
selves in the Australian dramatic profession. They then
appeared as two children (a boy and a girl) in “ Marie
Antoinette.” The company reached Australia in 1875,
and opened in Sydney on 21st July. In later years
Ristori essayed acting in English. One of her last enter-
prises was the leasing of the Theatre Royal, Glasgow, in
the autumn of 1882. There she played Lady Macbeth in
English, the Macbeth being our own Walter Bentley, who
did the Maeßichelieu in Sydney.
It’s a pity Dean Phelan (R. 0.), of Melbourne, did not
accept Manager Williamson’s offer to put on a matinee
performance of “The Spring Chicken,” solely for his
benefit, that the priest might judge whether it was as
wicked as he had believed it to be when ha exhorted good
Holy Romans to stay away. The audience and the per-
formance would have supplied a most interesting new
version of “ A Question of Propriety.”
“Skull”: My first impression is that Manager Bill
Anderson has secured a most capable actress in Miss
Helen Burdette. She has a good appearance, a fine voice,
shows cultivation and intelligence, and can convey an
excellent idea of vehemence without “ tearing it,” as the
good gods would say. The new woman makes the com-
monplace claptrap of melodrama sound human and
reasonable, and that takes a bit of doing. I have heard
much clamor of effusive greeting raised over worse
actresses—only they were imported.
“J.A.C.”: Tenor Caruso, who lately got into trouble
in New York, suffers from enlarged head. An instance
of this affliction occurred in ’Frisco immediately after the
earthquake. Caruso was discovered in a vacant lot,
guarding his goods and chattels, when a brisk little man
came along carrying a “grip-sack.’’ He not only put
this down on the same planet that Caruso was using, but
on the same lot, and right under the great man’s nose.
When the immense being recovered from the shock of
the outrage he threw the little man’s “ grip ” into the
next State, and the little man retaliated with his fist,
which caused the eminent’s head to swell to an even
greater extent, f
“Ah, my beloved, fill the cup.” You
may just as well fill it with the purest spirit avail-
able. Wolfe’s Schnapps is the liveliest stimulant
and best tonic for mind or body.*
“ Captain Starlight ” was, a month ago, a star turn at a
London hall, with Frank Gerald as the desperado. Aus-
tralia lost sight of Gerald 15 years ago ; up to that time
he and Duff toured the country with Gabbett, Good Mr.
Dawes, and other Australian properties. But a cold
frost set in, which cured Duff, and sent Gerald and his
wife to England.
THE VIOLINIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sing, violin, sing soft, sing low,
Cry like a voice, throb like a heart,
A simple song of long ago,
Until their eyes grow dim and smart.
Sing, violin, sing fierce, sing loud ;
Rise like a storm, sink like a gale,
Then tear the dead Love from its shroud.
Sing, violin, you shall not fail!
Sing, violin, sing slow, sing sweet,
Live like a sob, die like a kiss,
Like a wild wave arise and beat
Above the bed of buried bliss.
Sing, violin, sing soft, sing low ;
Cry like a voice, throb like a heart—
A simple song of long ago,
Till they are sobbing, heart to heart.
VIC.
DOROTHY McCRAK.
THE PASSING SHOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In some old haven, where the wine is red,
We’ll sit and watch the sober world go by—
The eve of that fell day when maid doth wed
The weaker of us two—is’t you or I ?
And, ere we part, we’ll drink some Burgundy
To Friendship and the Free-and-Easy Days,
And pay the score to Mistress Margery,
And say farewell, and go our sep’rate ways.
VIC.

TAKING ONE CONSIDERATION WITH ANOTHER.
“ George, dear, you are very gray for so young a man.”
“My dear, you must remember we have been married nearly ten years.”
A loyal English paper indignantly repu-
diates a base American journal’s assertion
that King Edward finds it increasingly hard
to keep awake. If there is anything in the
Yankee yarn the sleeping complaint is pro-
bably hereditary. “The Early Years of His
Royal Highness the Prince Consort,” which was
"compiled under the direction of Her Majesty
the Queen ” (Victoria), gives the information that
“ from his earliest years the Prince had to struggle
constantly of an evening against the feeling of
sleepiness. . . . Nor did the Prince, manfully as
he strove against it, ever entirely conquer it.”
Still, despite all this drowsiness in high places,
the Empire manages to jog along somehow.
Sydney and its suburbs are swarming just
now with she-furies canvassing in the Tory
cause—supercilious harridans from the fashionable
suburbs, and haughty maidens who brush aside
all doubt about their incredible assertions by a
reference to the authority of “Pa.” There is an
abounding recklessness and a huge disregard of
consequences about these women who put on their
best hats and rush in where angels fear to tread.
They assure everybody that whoever opposes their
Tory candidate is a Socialist, and that the
Socialists are going to “confiscate the home.”
They don’t mind, at a push, inventing particulars
to back up the statement. One who recently
called at a boarding-house in Kelly’s electorate
stated to the harassed lady of the house that her
premises and furniture would be confiscated, and
her poor little hash business taken from her, and she
would really have nothing left but her clothes,
if the Tories didn’t win. The alarmed landlady,
hoping against hope, asked who announced these
dreadful things, and the lofty one waved her
hand and implied that her assertion ought to be
quite sufficient. Being further pressed, she got
reckless, and said that Mr. Watson had per-
sonally announced the wholesale confiscation
policy in her hearing. And she is only one of a
number of fashionable and incredible Sapphiras
in petticoats, who, when cornered for evidence,
allege cheerfully that they “heard Mr. Watson
say it,” or something of the kind. There are
quite a lot of the fashionable women now canvass-
ing for Reid or Kelly or Bruce Smith or their
kind, who would stand a very fair chance of
getting three months in gaol for malignant
slander if Watson were a less affable gentleman,
and cared to proceed against them for the accusa-
tions of robbery and immorality that they throw
at him, and the astounding infamies which
some of them are prepared to state that they
heard him advocate. These reckless beings in
silk stockings and chiffons and picture hats go
round takiner chances that an old political cam-
paigner wouldn’t even dream of.
* * * •
Justice Street, of N.S.W., has just done a
wrong thing. The late Peter Tyson left £lOOO
by will “ for patriotic purposes,” and the execu-
tors applied for instructions as to how they should
use the money. Plainly the testator’s idea was
that the money should be applied to election
purposes to help to keep George Reid out of
office, but Justice Street, with this clear intention
staring him in the face, ruled that the bequest
was unintelligible, and cancelled it.
A half-caste native girl at Mount Gambier
(S.A.) lias been made the subject of a successful
gamble by the barrister who defended her on a
charge of murdering her infant. The verdict was
manslaughter, with a recommendation to mercy,
and her counsel boldly took the point that it was
bad in law, the thing being either murder or
nothing ! In short, he tossed strangle or quits.
So all parties went before the Supreme Court,
and the barrister, in effect, threw up his client,
and she came down tails—acquittal. Gordon, who
tried the case, was for letting the girl come down
heads (or rather neck), but the other two judges
were against him. The barrister probably gam-
bled on the sporting instinct in man. Legally it
might be plain, obvious murder, but there was a
feeling that the girl had had one run for her life,
and that it wasn’t a square thing to turn her out
again with the same dog after her.
More casualness. At Wellington (M.L.),
t other day, an Austrian fisherman named Jacob
Something rose early and tied himself by the
neck to a beam. Later, the boarder (evidently a
well-trained boarder) arose for the purpose of
lighting the fire. Passing the outhouse contain-
ing the corpse, he (the model boarder) observed
the suspension of Jacob, but not wishing to make
breakfast late, he (the well-trained boarder) pro-
ceeded with the kitchen conflagration. Later, he
(the aforementioned well-trained and model
boarder) and the “missus” adjourned to the out-
house and considered the suspended attitude of
J acob, but came to no decision, and the matter
was hung up. Considerably later in the day a
friend, who chanced to call round, passed a knife
through the cord, and Jacob fell to the ground
with a dull thud. Almost the same day, at the
Hutt (M.L.), a boy dropped into a store and made
two statements. One was that the old man had
died an hour previously, and the other that he
(the boy) wanted some fireworks. A staggered
grocer asked if he was going to explode the
crackers at his father’s funeral, but it transpired
that he wanted them on account of Guy Fawkes.

“The Girl at Blue Creek,” by George
Checkley, is a decidedly amusing volume, just out,
described as “a Book of the Bush, with Some
Mention of Town.” It contains sixty-odd illustra-
tions by Laurie Tayler, and appeals as well to the
grafter on The Edge as to the “towney” in his
luxurious summer camp. Post free, 2s. 9d., from
The Southern Press Agency, 243 Collins-street,
Melbourne; or 2s. Gd. on the bookstalls.
“U.”: I have often wondered what the
“ converted ” lover of melodrama finds to oc-
cupy the place of the plays he or she used
to patronise, for even the most eloquent
shepherd’s discourses hardly seem
to provide as much excitement
as “ The Best Barmaid in Mt.
Boppy,” or “The Night
Fiends of Brewarrina.”
1 wonder no longer.
My landlady’s Mary
Ann, who has been
converted for quite
three weeks, and
has abstained f rom
stage melodrama
dur i ng that
period, kindly
allows me to
read specimens
of a cheap im-
ported series of
“Thrilling Life
Stories of the
Masses,” which
are published by
“ The Thrilling
Stories Commit-
tee” at an address in Man-
chester, and purveyed in
our suburb by one of Mary
Ann’s church sisters. I
don’t know who axjpoints
the committee. Possibly it
consists of a publisher who
is a good judge of tripe.
Anyway, the whole melo-
dramatic box of tricks is
used in the production of
the stories—wrongful accu-
sations of murder and other
crimes, missing wills, “se-
cret of your birth,” and so
on. The only variation upon
the ordinary stage dialogue
is that the villain lays the
monstrous charge in a speech
interlarded with Scripture,
and the hero supports his
refutation of it with texts,
and the heroine protests
that she is in-no-cent, as anyone can see who
will turn up the third Epistle to the Corin-
thians, chapter two, verses five and six. Also, the
walls of the Old Manor House are mostly hung
with texts, and the fair young daughter of the
squire is fond of bursting forth into hymns in
which “rain” rhymes with “same.” But, apart
from the texts, it’s just plain Han Barryism and
threepenny barnstorming.

The pig-tailed ambassador from Pekin
heard himself very rudely spoken of, the other
Monday, in Melbourne. After figuring gorgeously
in the vice-regal enclosure, at the Albert Park
review, his stolid Serenity was left to get home as
best he could. Northcote and co. had their car-
riages in close attendance to take them right away
from their reserved space, but the appalling
visitor in the distinguished dressing-gown had to
push with the crowd and hail a cab. Just as he
was beginning to look very conscious of his
strangeness in a strange land, an impetuous young
Australian, staring wildly at the apparition,
shrieked to his mate, “Come here, Bill! Come
quick, and look at this Chow ! ” Bill, coming up
in time to catch a glimpse of Tong Gong before he
drove off, thrust his hands into his pockets and
said, “My oath ! What price is he ticketed ’ ”

“ The Traveller ” : Melbourne Age, in a
brief report of the railway accident at Lara (Vic.),
on the Geelong line, was chiefly concerned be-
cause the smash caused a delay to the 2 a.m.
goods train from Melbourne. The 2 a.m. is the
“Paper Train,” or “Davy Syme,” as the rail-
way men call it. These paper-trains are a source
of constant mental worry to the Spencer-street
goods yard staff. For half-an-hour or so before
they are due to leave, the “ departure ” lines must
be kept clear, and shunting operations are prac-
tically at a standstill. Should the signals be
against a driver who wishes to pass the Dudley-
street goods junctions in the very early morning
he knows it is useless to whistle for “ line clear ”
For “Davy Syme” is about to be published.
The pick of the North Melbourne loco, depotmen
and engines are commandeered for the paper-
trains. Only half a load is put on in order that
the great Melbourne dailies may scoot like all
Gehenna to Ballarat and Bendigo, and arrive in
time to compete with the local sheets. (The
North-Eastern line doesn’t matter so much
There’s no big daily to suppress there.) Each
morning the first duty of the heads of the
branches at Spencer-street is to peruse the
special messages (which must be there, or some-
body would be sacked) relative to the safe running
of these trams and their furious and headlong
arrival. Verily, “Davy Syme” is a great Vic-
torian institution.

Panton, Melbourne’s decayed P.M., was
quite sympathetic to tlie crew of the steamer
Lord Antrim who took law proceedings against
their captain. Panton said that “this kind of
seaman had a good idea of justice and if fairly
treated did not cause trouble.” But it was a
Chinese crew. Panton, tottering superfluous on
the stage, decides against the white seaman with
hardly a fair pretence of hearing the case, and
gives him beans, and he couldn’t glare at him
worse if his eye were a tram-light.

THE CREDITOR: “ I see. He'd rather lic awake
thinking of his debts than get up and work

In Melbourne General Sessions, last week,
Judge Hickman Molesworth laid down the law
of the Immigration Restriction Act straightly.
The case was that of Ah Cheong and Ah Yin,
aged 17 and 15, sons of Loo Loon Hock. Loon
first transpired in Lilly Buck-stleet in ’95. In ’O3
he went home to visit his wife, or his wives
possibly, returning in ’O5. Shortly afterwards
Mrs. Loo, the mother of these young sons, died,
so Loo decided to bring them to Australia and
train them to carry on his business. But they,
naturally, failed to pass the test. As prohibited
immigrants, P.M. Panton ordered them to be
detained pending deportation. Thereupon Loo
appealed on the ground that, he being domiciled
here for eight or nine years, his sons were likewise
domiciled here. Judge Molesworth, however,
supported Panton in a firm voice ; so Loo will
appeal to the Pull and probably to the High
Court. Said his Honor :
Putting aside the question whether these two immi-
grants could be legally considered the sons of Loo, they
having been born in a country where polygamy is legal
and customary, the real question was that the Act of ’O5
repealed all previous Acts, and under it the only test was
the language test—and the appellants had failed to pass
it. The Act was passed to enable the Government to
keep out of the Commonwealth certain undesirable
persons, and it might well be that certain persons were
undesirable as Australians, no matter what their conduct
might be.
And, anyhow, was Loon ever domiciled in Aus-
tralia ? It’s a very well-established principle of
law, on which many a would-be land dummy has
been fired out, that the home of a married man is
where the wife is, and the more wives he has, the
more thereabouts, one would think, his home
should be.

The Vicar of Wakefield, an ancient inn in
Harbor-street, Sydney, goes the way of all old
inns. A generation ago one George E. Wake-
field kept, at the corner of Dixon and Liverpool
streets, an old-time pub, known as the Hand and
Heart, and about the same time he was under-
stood to have an interest in the property in
Harbor-street, upon which a Mr. Mann erected
the Vicar of Wakefield pub. Wakefield after-
wards kept it, but the house is chiefly notable for
the fact that Edward Parley, the one-time
favorite basso, and well remembered as General
Boom in “The Grand Duchess,” and Devilshoof
in “ The Bohemian Girl,” was proprietor for a few
months, and sold the unkempt “colonial beer”
and the tall pewter of porter to the humble
worker threabouts. Next door to the Vicar, in
times long gone by, the “big Tooheys,” J. T. and
J. M., commenced business as ale bottlers.

THE ISLE OF MANN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, will you tell us, Mr. Mann,
Exactly what to do ;
There’s quite a lot of unemployed
At Day Dawn and at Cue,
And in the places further back,
And right along the outer track,
The hungry drifters sorely lack
A leader such as you.
Prom Southern Cross to Marble Bar
We’ve limped along in pain,
We’ve borne the white man’s burden far
Beyond the Byro Plain.
We’ve called at every town and place
Within the intervening space,
And now, alas! we have to face
The same old trip again.
But is there not a country where
The workers do not roam,
But sit around and smoke and swear
(There is no place like home) ?
Oh, is there not a land wherein
’Tis counted as a deadly sin
To be morose or sad or thin
At sunrise or at gloam ?
And we have heard that in that land
A princely wage is paid
For painting rails, and shifting sand,
And resting in the shade ;
And that the gents who represent
The working men in Parliament
Have proved, by clear experiment,
The beauties of Freetrade.
Oh, had we but a leader true
'To take us by the hand,
And guide our weary footsteps to
The country, rich and grand,
Where bosses never are annoyed,
And everyone is overjoyed
To help the local unemployed
To settle on the land !
Where is this Freetrade Isle of Bliss ?
Oh, mark it on the plan,
And we will struggle out of this,
And get there if we can.
That land must full of beauty be,
Where everything is duty free ;
Plexse tell us, on the strict Q.T.,
Where is this Isle of Mann ?
WESTIIALIA.

AN INJUSTICE.
FLORRIE (returning from her first visit to school) : “ I don’t fink much of the teacher, mum ;
she slapped me ’cos she couldn’t count.”
MUM : “ How’s that?”
FLORRIE: “She says: ‘Spose I give you two apples, an’ then two more; how many’ll you
have !’ I says five, an’ she says four lots o’ times; an’ then I shows her one I had in
me pocket, an’ she got wild.”
An Auckland (M.L.) Band of Strivers
lately hurt a young Striver’s feelings fearfully by
discussing an alleged weakness of his. They may
have said he couldn’t recite the Book of Revela-
tion, or that he had lost a hymn book or some-
thing. Anyhow, the Striver rushed off and
printed a couple of big tickets, saying that be
forerave his fellow Strivers, and that his body
would be found in the harbor, though his soul,
etc. The police found a coat hung on near
the water, and, having read the Striver’s farewell
message, they sprinted for the grappling irons.
They didn’t find any dead body, because the
Striver wasn’t dead. He was at home drying
himself. When charged with a failure to commit
suicide, he told the magistrate ho really did try to
drown himself, but the harbor was too cold and
wet. He was going to England, he said, because
his fellow Strivers were unkind to him. As an
advertiser he ought to make Judkins envious when
lie has a bit more experience. Why doesn’t Jud-
kins drown himself?

“ Relay ” writes from Melbourne :
Once more has the public been gulled by an abortive
and scandalous “official inquiry,” conducted by the in-
competent military authorities here. The scandal hushed
up this time is the shameful overloading of the Govern-
ment launch Vulcan, and its despatch in such weather as
caused the old tub to all but turn turtle. One of the
submarine mining company (by pure luck not the lot of
us) was drowned. Of course the inquiry was private,
and only the findings were published, but as a survivor
and one who is in touch with the witnesses, I know that
startling facts were tendered in court as evidence. The
presiding officers, however, treated the affair very super-
ficially : ~ , .
(1) It is said that there were only 87 aboard (see Argus).
We know there were at least 100.
(2) It is claimed that the launch will carry ISO, but 100
appear to have been enough to cause it to almost founder.
(3) It is said the launch was only 100 yards from those
who fell overboard when it turned. The fact is, the
skipper was noton the bridge when the accident occurred,
he having other duties to attend to, and by the time he
had got there and had signalled the engine-room, the
launch was at least 300vds. away.
Only three buoys were available when wanted, and it
the launch had turned turtle (as it all but did—but
nothing was said of this), there was only a small boat to
carry what would have remained of the two companies
and crew. In other words, out of 100 men eight might
have been saved.
The devil only knows if this affair will cause more care
to be exercised in future. Not having seen the devil
lately, my information stops here.
The latest news is that the shut-up Inquiry is
likely to be re-opened, so “Relay” may yet get
a chance to blow off some evidence.

Parson Tregear’s collection of escaped
Convent girls in Melbourne still numbers only
four, but additions are expected. The enthusi-
astic young parson has hopes of taking over the
whole of the Abbotsford Convent girls, and has
visions of himself leading an army of escaped
laundresses to force 'the hand of the Government
in the matter of bringing the Convent, which is a
huge steam laundry, under the provisions of the
Factories Act. Mr. Tregear may be having his
leg pulled with a 40-h.p. strain, but the Roman
Catholics of Melbourne would be wise to invite
a thorough investigation re the charges of con-
tinuous sweating, cruelty, and actual bondage in
the case of females immured in the institution,
which charges the raging Methodist is scattering
broadcast. The Convent employs hundreds of
girls in the wash business ; it pays no wages, and
is exempt from supervision, and escapes all the
provisions of the factory legislation. An insti-
tution so situated cannot expect to satisfy the
outside public when it answers circumstantial
charges of the gravest nature with silent con-
tempt. Charitable legislation like the Vic. Fac-
tories Act should be very welcome in a charitable
institution. A large, enthusiastic offer on the
part of the R.C. magnates to support a Bill
placing their charities under the Factories Act,
provided that it applies to the charities of all
denominations alike, would be a grievous turning
of the flank of Mr. Tregear if the R.C. charities
can bear inspection. It would amount, in fact,
to a sudden turning up in his rear, and the
capture of his ammunition waggon. All depends
on how much investigation things can stand.
The fussy Anglican prelates in Melbourne
grew mixed over their arrangements for the gaudy
ceremonies at St. Paul’s Cathedral, where they
opened their congress. G.-G. Northcote and
Plain-G. Talbottle were among the invited guests,
but when these dignitaries arrived the parsons
were so busy getting into their purple nightgowns
that they forgot all about the necessary reception
on the Cathedral steps. Governor Talbottle came
first and, acting under instructions from Arch-
bishop Lady Clarke (at least, he looks like Arch-
bishop Lady when he has his official skirts on),
drew up outside a locked gate. The flunkey in
front of the carriage shouted “Hi!” and
Talbottle and Victor Nelson Hood stuck their
heads out of windows and shouted “Hi !” also,
but not even a lay reader appeared. Talbottle,
who stickles considerably for his dignity, began
to bite his moustache as fiercely as ever he did in
the Soudan, and Victor Nelson Hood used
language which vastly increased the multitude’s
respect for that small-fashioned but largely-
named person. Eventually the Gov. got into the
Cathedral by forcing the catch of the scullery
window, or something of that sort. The G.-G.’s
carriage drew up in the midst of a line of fish-
carts near the side-walk, and the G.-G. and his
dame wandered forth unattended to find entrance.
The G.-G. is suspicioned of having lifted his wife
in through a window, thereafter the vice-regal
persons scowled from their reserved seats while
Bishop Stretch preached that the Church’s mission
was the perfecting of social order. The relations
between Church and State have never been so
badly strained before, and it wouldn’t be at all
surprising if either King Ned or the Archbishop
of Canterbury were forced to resign.

Australian Civil Service Commissioners
wrestle a good deal with the problem of what
constitutes a civil servant’s household when the
expense of removing him from one locality to
another has to be considered. Perhaps the rule laid
down by Tam worth (N. S. W.) Wesleyan Synod with
regard to the removal of a parson might serve as
a guide. That thoughtful body of experts defines
a parson’s household, for removal purposes, as
including the parson, his wife, children, or
adopted children, his father and mother and his
wife’s father and mother, if they reside with him.
It seems comprehensive, yet it leaves him to pay
out of his own pocket for the shifting of his bed-
ridden grandmother, his maiden aunt, his uncle
who has taken to drink and is being reformed,
anc l if he is a man like the Rev. Tregear—any
escaped convent girls he may have on hand.

Sydney people look askance at the
ministering angel in the natty nurse’s attire just
now. A few days ago two members of the low-
voiced, soft-handed, tender-hearted sisterhood
were ordered to Goulburn gaol for two years, there
to remove the fresh and innocent-looking costume,
and wear their hair according to Government
regulation, and inhabit small cold apartments,
and hustle at servile tasks, and bear themselves
reverently before the matron. This was the result
of a long course of acquiring assets by false
pretences. Later on, the younger of the pair was
brought back to court at her own request,
and proceeded to confess largely, though it
was, to a great extent, a case of confessing
how bad the other nurse was. She stated
tearfully that a case heard last year in which
her fellow-nurse had, with her aid, demanded
damages from a Sydney citizen, was only
another example of their joint wickedness. The
citizen, one Benjamin, had hired the two bright,
tender creatures to attend his wife, and one_ of
them, corroborated by the other, accused him,
after a while, of assault, battery, and partial
strangulation. For these injuries she wanted
£lOOO, and she actually got £l3O. Also, Costs
were a serious item to Mr. Benjamin. The pair
put up a very weak case against Benjamin, but
they looked guileless and gentle in their pretty
clothes, while Mr. Benjamin only looked like a
plain male citizen with an umbrella and bqll-
topper. So the jury overlooked the discrepancies,
and Benjamin fell in. Which was desperately
rough on Benjamin. The same sort of thing is
liable, also, to be rough on the next Benjamin
unless he can get a blind jury. A further item of
the confession was that the same pair had
put up a wholly false charge against a lady,
which led to her husband suing for a divorce,
and naming Dr. Chisholm Ross, of _ Sydney,
as co-respondent. The charge, it is said, drove
the lady mad, and she cut the proceedings short
by suicide, but there is nothing in the confession,
so far as it was published, to show that the weight
of her tombstone lay at all heavy on the chest of
Nurse Daisy Greville or on the bust of Nurse
Kathleen Dwyer when they went to bed at night.
Wellington (M.L.) appears to have a plea-
sant sort of parson as chairman of its “Benevo-
lent Trustees.” The other day an old blind man
who can’t read or write complained that he was
never allowed to move out of the “ home,” and he
would rather, he said, be found dead in the streets
than kept always a prisoner. “You can please
yourself about that,” said the sympathetic rev.
chairman. It is by such tender words of pity,
dear friends, that we help our afflicted brothers
to bear their crosses.

The Law further advertised its incom-
petence, the other day, when it escorted a Bal-
larat woman off its premises with a
vacant expression and an assurance that
it couldn’t do anything. The lady had ,
a daughter of 17, employed at a res-
taurant. The miserable girl had suc-
cumbed to the blandishments of an un-
employed half-caste Chow, and the
pair had eloped to Lilly Buck-stleet
or thereabouts. The mother assured
the police that she had no objection
to the damsel marrying a white
man, though it might be better for
her if she waited a year or two, but
she viewed with natural horror the
union of her clean, white Australian
daughter and a semi-Chow with no
work to do. And all the Law could
do was to tell the worried mother
that if the damsel had stolen six-
pence it would arrest her, but that
as she was only ruining herself, dis-
gracing her family and polluting
the race, it could do nothing. Then
it murmured some regrets, and the
episode closed.
Cow, that ghastly uddered tyrant which
persuades dairymen to put their children into
slavery, assails even the church. Archdeacon
Hindley mourned to his Anglican brethren
m conference that owing to the demands of
Cow, dairymen stayed away from church. He
urged that in Cow districts services should be
held at times which would suit Cow, and that the
churches should be built right alongside the
milking yards, so that Cow might not be neglected
too long. The parson-community will yet begin to
realise the excellence of Australia’s most popular
objurgation : “You Cow ! ”

The relative value of man and beast, as
shown by the “ Country Notes ” of a Taranaki
(M.L.) daily:—
Mr. James Smith, of Koru, had the misfortune to lose
a very fine Berkshire boar on Tuesday last ; the animal,
which was a prize-winner at Waitara, Inglewood, and
Opunake, unfortunately ate a large quantity of poisoned,
meat intended for birds.
Mr. John Brown, of Mokau, lost a son last week.
Mr. M. Williams, of Raglan, was out riding on Monday,
when his mount, a fine, thoroughbred by St. Michael, out
of Tinplate, bolted and ran into a boy ; the horse received
nastv scratches on the shoulders, but with care may be
well"in a fortnight. It had a providential escape, and at
first the accident, it was feared, would prevent its appear-
ance at the Waitotara show. The boy expired next day.
Evidently neither the son of John Brown nor
“the bov” had ever won prizes any where.

Lindsay Russell, the engineer of the Lily-
dale and Warburton (Yic.) Railway Trust, whose
account-books weirdly disappeared just before an
audit was due, has written a dramatic finish to
the mystery, and closed the book with a bang.
The Trust sued Russell for the recovery of the
bulky tomes. Russell said they had been stolen,
and a number of letters were produced from
alleged “tugs,” who, in shocking English and
incorrect slang, wrote to the shire president and
HEAPS AND HEAPS.
HE ( explaining what a s weepstake is): “You pay
in a pound. Then you draw lots”.
SHE : “ How easy ! And if you pay in three
pounds you draw lots, and lots. and lots ?
others explaining that they had stolen the books
because they owed a pound or two to the 1 rust,
and wanted to obliterate the debt, of these
letters lauded Russell to the skies as the very
model of a modern engineer. The Judge and
iury disbelieved the story, and ordered the
engineer to pay £5O damages or restore the books
and pay £25 damages. Russell’s reply was to
consult with his solicitor and then go to the
detective office and solemnly run himself in on a
charge of having embezzled several hundreds of
municipal money. He makes about the tenth
trusted but ill-paid Vic. municipal official that
has gone up in the last year.

FATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Most of us seem to forget in the turmoil of the Present
the «nd for which we are destined. Surely that end
approaches, and which of ye shall be prepared ?—Extract
from the same old sermon.
What is the end of a cat or dog,
When the bill of its days is due?
Of course, you may say it ends with a tail,
And a very good answer, too ;
But think of the harmless oyster-folk
That end in an oyster stew.
The turtle that wanders wild and free
By his caves on the wave-worn coast,
In the commonplace cause of turtle soup
Shall give up his greasy ghost;
And the bright little quail that whirrs and tweets
Will soon be a quail-on-toast.
The turkey that thumps his lordly chest,
With a vain-consuming pride,
Notes not that a spectral waiter stands
And menaces by his side,
And mouths at the words that will soon be said :
“ Turkey, sir, b’iled or fried? ”
The jocund lambkin that gambols free,
’Tis well that he knows or sees
No fateful presage of mutton chops,
Mint sauce, and sweet green peas ;
’Tis well that the beeve recks not of beef
As he chews his cud at ease.
And you and I, and the banker's clerk,
And the civil servant, too,
The tradesman smart, and the working man,
And the struggling cockatoo,
Are just small fry for a Fat Man’s pan,
Or a snack for a hungry Jew. .
VIC.
A.B.C.
