There is no doubt about it—Joseph Carruthers
is a great financial reformer, with a soul above small
i“* a “ * or ™ a "y y ears the otherwise-uninvested
funds in the control of the Master in Lunacy, being at the
command of the Treasury, carried 3 per cent.—the same
rate as sums left with the Government Savings Bank.
But when Joseph Carruthers was producing a surplus to
make his halo with, he cut down this 3 per cent, to 1 per
cent. . If a family has the awful misfortune to see its
head put under restraint through mental trouble, Joseph
the Reformer promptly fines it 2 per cent, per annum on
the funds in the hands of the Master in Lunacy : he cuts
down the interest on the lunatic’s money bv that much
. e sane man can get 3 per cent, from the State ; the
insane only 1 per cent. The woman who is something
rather worse than a widow would appear to most men as
rather a worthy subject for sympathy and gentle treat-
ment. But that is not the way of the modern Reformer,
ue says : Hullo, here’s a woman with a madman for a
husband. She can’t squeal, and if he does nobody will
take any notice of him, so let’s sneak two-thirds of their
I v«M°! ne Tk ro “ “! e *" nds in hand '” There’s reform for
you ! There’s high finance !
* * « «
Treasurer Carruthers has been giving informa-
tion about the N.S. Wales Government’s fire and marine
insurance business. For a couple of years the State did
its own fire insurance, and had already built up a re-
°f > fc hat is, on a two years’ run a
profit of £2304 was shown on the business. When Car-
i uthers decided to grab this accumulation and use it as
revenue which he did last year—it was at least expected
that the Government would establish an Insurance
Department, which would simply mean setting aside
a Treasury clerk to attend to the State’s insur-
ance business, just as Goldsbrough-Mort’s and other
concerns of that sort do, and just as the Vic-
torian Government also does. The result of that
would have been that the Government could have
been listed”—just like the Victorian Government,
and just like big private firms with much insurance to
do That would have meant a rebate of 5 per cent., so
that the insurance cos. would really have paid the
insurance Department’s expenses. Instead, however of
doing anything so sensible, the Reformer puts the busi-
ness through wholly superfluous brokers and middlemen.
u? words, the Reformer abolished the system under
which there were no pickings for anybody, passed over a
system (adopted by the Victorian Government and all
financial houses of any size) which would reduce the
p issible pickings to little or nothing, and adopted the
system which gives the opportunity for the maximum of
pickings. And sooner or later the State, of course, has
to pay for the pickings.
The N.S.W. Government was apparently so
anxious to bring in the system which would provide
pickings that it doesn’t seem to have even troubled to
give any instructions about the placing of the business.
Ihere are reputable Australian offices quite capable of
doing all the business that offers, but, according to the
answers to questions put in the Assembly, a large amount
of the business is given out to foreign offices. For some
time a Canton office did the Government’s marine busi-
"e®s - has been stopped ; but, though at least two
iLb. Wales cos. have offices in London, the N.S.
Wales Government’s marine insurance on shipments
from London is farmed out to English offices and their
foreign connections. Moreover, risks which don’t appear
to have been covered at all while the State did its own
insuring, are now covered. While insurance didn’t mean
commission, the need for insurance was not seen When
however, insurance meant commission, the need for in-
surance appears to have been seen pretty soon. That may
of course, be merely a tribute to the persuasive powers of
the brokers. On the other hand, it may indicate how
willing someone in authority is to pay out £lO of the
public’s money in order that friends of someone in
authority may pocket £l.
* * * *
Once more a Companies Act is going through
N.S. Wales Parliament without any provision to oblige
cos. carrying on business in the State to have an office
within it. They need not, under either the existing or
the proposed law, even have one in Australia. Though
they may carry on business nowhere but in N.S. Wales
their only office may be in England—or in Germany’
France or Guernsey. At least two Australian cos. have
their offices in Guernsey. Under common circumstances
this absence of an office within the State means no more
than a little delay in the transfer of shares and the
payment of dividends. But supposing a man dies with
shares in one of these cos. as part of his estate—shares
for instance, in Lloyd’s copper mine, so much dealt in
lately—the tedious and expensive process of re-sealing
has to be gone through, because technically the property
is not in N.S. Wales, but in the country where the
register is.
* * * •
Forty million rabbits, or thereabouts, will be
shipped from Australia and sold this year, and the trade
seems good for as long as there are rabbits to sell and the
little beasts are not suspected of being diseased. This
rabbit trade is a considerable set-off against the rabbit
plague; and if Australia does anything to destroy the
trade while leaving the plague just about where it was it
will be the two ends of a fool. That is the whole
situation with regard to the rabbit-disease experi-
ments. If Dr. Danysz can undertake to wipe
the little beasts clean out, well and good * the
only question that will remain then is whether he will
also wipe other things out at the same time. But if he
can only reduce the number, without making a clean
sweep, then he ought to be ruled out; and he will abso-
lutely kill the trade even if he only wipes out one in every
100 rabbits by disease—the public won’t risk getting the
one diseased animal in its pot. Last year the export
trade was worth £389,738 ; this year it will probably run
to about £sso,ooo—more than the value of the frozen
beef trade, and about half of the value of the exports of
mutton and lamb. This isn’t a business to be fooled
with. Australia would be better off if it didn’t have any
rabbits at all to make such a trade possible ; but' as the
trade is a certainty, it can’t part with that certainty
for an uncertainty. And if Dr. Danysz is merely going
to reduce the rabbits, then he will be doing* no more than
is being done now. The difference would be that the
reduction in one case would cost money and cover the
country with a bad smell, while in the other it puts half-a-
million a year into Australia’s pocket, not to mention the
quarter-of-a-million or over derived from the sale of the
skins—exact figures for 1905 were £244,400.
* « * *
When Yic. Premier Bent bought Werribee
Estate from the unhappy-looking Chirnside, at £l2 or £l3
an acre, it looked a mighty bad bargain. (It is a mightv
bad bargain when it is remembered that Chirnside got
it from the State for next to nothing, and made a fortune
out of it before he sold it back.) But Bent had something
up his sleeve, and if his proposal comes off, as seems
likely, he will show that the old boomster can still do a
cute trade in land. Bent’s proposition is to bring in
water from the hills round Bacchus Marsh and Ballan.
Enough of the drinkable can easily be collected to irri-
gate about 15,000 acres of Werribee land, and the effect
will be to jog it up to the neighborhood of £2O an acre—
perhaps £25 and £3O. A big irrigated area like this,
within half an hour’s journey of a city of half-a-million
people, has a fortune in it, if the watering process can be
done by gravitation.

Even in the boom days of the late ’Bo’s and early '9o’s such
profit as is shown for the past half-year was not reached,
although in those times a dividend of 17J per cent, was
paid. But that was on a smaller capital than the bank
has to-day. Just at present, depositors and shareholders
alike have a good deal to be thankful for in the fact that
London wants money badly ; whether, as a huge borrower
in London, with many loans falling due shortly, Australia
in general has the same reason to be thankful is another
matter. But for this demand for money in London,
either Australian depositors must have suffered through
a further reduction of deposit rates, or shareholders
'vould have been obliged to put up with smaller profits.
In Sept., 1903, the deposits of this one bank were
£20,628,463 ; they are now £24,812,021, or no less than
£4,183,558 more. Meantime the advances have, in the
three years, increased by only £758,285. Had the other
three-and-a-half millions or thereabouts found no more
profitable employment than to lie in the bank’s vaults,
or, at the best, had they been earning the old
rate of 1£ per- cent, in London, the situation would
have been a good deal different from what it is.
But London has taken large sums at almost phenomenal
rates ; no later than two years ago this bank’s short-call
money there amounted to only £200,000, whereas to-day
it is £2,520,000—a quite unusual outlet, which has also
helped the bank by stiffening the rates for accommoda-
tion in Australia. One way and another, in the past six
years, Australian banks have had a lot of luck, as, for
instance—(l) They made big profits by taking large risks
during the drought; then a run of remarkable seasons
followed the drought, and wiped the risks out, but the
big profits remained good. (2) They have been able to
pass on their pastoral assets, etc., at prices which they
hardly even hoped for. (3) Now, with a large surplus of
money, London has come in as a customer.
Comparing present figures with those of
12 months ago, these results are obtained :
Sept., ’O5. Sept., ’O6.

So in the past 12 months the debt to the public has in-
creased by £2,280,515 ; and of that amount £1,566,945, pr
nearly 70 per cent., is on short call in London and in
other liquid assets. Taking the whole of the liquid
assets, they represent 42 per cent, of all liabilities to the
public—which is well over the proportion (say 35 per
cent.) regarded as the limit of safety. As to profits, the
earnings on total funds (capital and reserves) have lately
been as follows:

Considering the exceptionally large amount of public
money with which this bank is favored, this is not a high
rate. Only just over 10 per cent, of the money in the
business belongs to shareholders ; for every £1 of its own
it has the use of over £8 of the public’s. This, of course,’
should mean large profits for shareholders ; it necessarily
also means large risks, seeing that the capital and
reserves are the public’s guarantee for the return of its
deposits, &c., intact. What will happen when the Lon-
don demand eases 'off it is hard to say. Some of the
money may be used to pay Off London loans, in which
case it will still go on earning perhaps 3J p,er cent. And,
if Australia is wise during the next few weeks, and elects
to Parliament men pledged to High Protection, a great
deal more of it will be absorbed in financing new indus-
tries.; and that money will earn a good deal more than
3J per cent.

In these figures the half-year shows an improvement •
but the real improvement isn’t as great, because in the’
March half-year £2500 was paid to the Provident Fund
and in the half-year before that the officers got a bonus
of £3350, while in the past half-year there were no extras
of this sort at all. Moreover, in running one eye down
the Profits column, the other eye might be kept on the
fact that £106,250 of new capital has been got in since
1902, £56,250 of which, being premium, does not count
for dividend. Still the 20 per cent, dividend even on the
1903 lot of shares, which were issued at a premium of
125 per cent., means very nearly 9 per cent. ; and if
shareholders can go on getting a return of that sort on a
recent investment, they are a very lucky lot. Last year’s
issue—at 100 per cent, premium—shows alO per cent,
return; and that is something more glittering still. As
a matter of fact, so far as the published figures show
the shareholders’ funds are not earning that 10 per
cent. —in other words, the new capital is not earn-
ing what is being paid on it in dividend ; and that
means, again, either that it should not have been
issued at so little as 100 per cent, premium, or that
the present dividend should be reduced. A bank whose
funds earn less than 10 per cent, has no right to pay a 20
per cent, dividend if it issues new shares at a premium of
only 100 per cent.; and if it does issue shares at that
price, it must expect a less dividend later on. The
earnings on funds (capital and reserves) for some terms
past have been :

The 20 per cent, dividend allows a lay-by, it is true "of

bearing interest. 011 this and some other points the fol-

There has been a notable increase in advances ; but as
Westralia shakes down, the chances are that, though
risks will grow less, rates for accommodation will also
shrink, and there will have to be a pretty large increase
in advances to counterbalance that. There is, however,
only one way in which this bank can safely increase its
advances far beyond the present point, and that is by the
turning of the large current account balances into fixed
deposits ; and that, of course, costs money. The
whole situation appears to be that the bank has
had its period of boom profits ; and, though it is quite
possible that through extra-good management it may
have no boom-burst losses, no amount of good manage-
ment will be able to maintain the old rate of earnings or
secure for the bank the benefit of so much non-interest-
bearing money. From the depositors’point of view, it
looks reasonably safe ; its own funds represent 6s. 3d. in
the £of the assets liable to depreciation. A shrinkage of
a good deal more than 6s. 3d. in the £ in goldfields secu-
rities is no doubt easily possible ; but, presumably, larg e
margins of safety have been required, and the 6s. 3d. in
the £is extra. Still 6s. 3d. in the £is not so much
in such a State as to suggest that there is room tor much
increase in the advances.

The Australian Mortgage, Land and Finance
Co., which through foreclosure and otherwise has become
one of the biggest landholders in N.S. Wales, especially
in the parts where Drought is mostly king:—

It is a marvellous recovery; no wonder that in good seasons
the real Australian bush is a Siren. Markets and seasons
have both favored the squatter ; he has netted more from
his thousand head than a few years back he could net
from 2000 ; and, having to carry only the thousand head
where formerly 2000 ran, the country has had a chance
to recover from the shameful eating-out process to which
it had been subjected. The serious question at present
is whether the eating-out process won’t again be forced
upon occupiers. The biggest movements on to the land
have taken place under just such conditions as prevail
now. With the country looking at its best, with high
market prices ruling for products, men took up land at
extravagant prices, and got what proved to be extrava-
gant advances from pastoral-finance cos. and banks—the
advances were proved extravagant by the immense losses
of capital made by these concerns. In the desperate
struggle to keep their heads above water when the
seasons changed, and the wool and stock markets weak-
ened, every possible hoof was crowded on to the
country ; and when drought arrived the country was in
the worst imaginable condition to stand it. So the fine
profits made by such a co. as the A.M., L. and F. must be
considered soberly, or trouble will follow.
Partly through not altogether losing its head in
the former days of pastoral boom, and partly through
close economy since then, the A.M., L. and F. has come
through better than almost any other concern of the
kind. It has, besides its capital of £520,000, £2,000,000
of debenture stock, £1,900,000 of which carries 4 per
cent, and the balance 3 per cent., and it has kept the
interest paid. The amount shown above as written
off applies to reserve fund investments only ; the present
appropriation brings the co.’s holding of Consols down to
85 in the books. Amounts written off pastoral holdings are
charged to profit and loss direct. What these amounts
are is not disclosed; but there have been considerable
realisations, or considerable sums have been written off.
For the position compares in some respects with that of
five years ago:—

Apparently on account of some flaw in the
Companies Aet--now in course of amendment —the
.North Queensland Insurance Co., instead of itself taking
accident business, formed, during the past year, the
North Queensland Accident Insurance Co., with a paid
capital of £13,437. For seven months, to Sept. 31sfc, it
shows a profit of £1613, or at the rate of over 19 per cent,
per annum. The expenses ran to about 29 per cent, of
the net premiums, and the losses were as little as 10 per
cent. though accident-insurance rates have
been cut down, and are still being cut, it is even yet a
highly profitable business—for the Private Enterprise
that sells the insurance. This co. pays 4 per cent, for the
seven months—equivalent to just under 7 ner cent, for
the year.

Broken Hill Junction Mining Co.

Mar., 1903 .. £2740 £1766 £17,914

Sept., 1905 .. 2468 1303 39,252
Mar., 1906 .. 6802 1249 47,303
Sept., 1906 .. 3731 1177 52,211
Just 10 years ago the 100,000 shares of £l, into which this
co. was then divided, were worth 625. each. They were at
that time paid to 16s. 6d. When the calling power had
been exhausted, the 100,000 shares were divided into
200,000 of 10s., with power to make calls equal to ss. on
100,000 shares. Of this ss. (£25,000) all but £5OOO has
now been called up, and the shares were recently
down to about 4s. They are now double that —pre-
sumably on the strength of the continuous losses shown
above. Since, 10 years ago, they were boomed to 625.
and more, they have paid only one dividend ; but they
have paid about a dozen calls. It is a little bit of
Broken Hill history that will probably pay a good many
people to read up just now. In 1896 there wasn’t a more
fashionable Barrier stock ; there seemed to be no end to
the dividends—they rolled out. Finally, English in-
vestors nibbled at them, and tried to get hold of a block
of 30,000 shares. And then disaster happened. It
started with a smash in the mine ; it ended—well, it
hasn’t ended yet. The co. has now £9582 of a surplus of
liquid assets over liabilities ; and its ore reserves are
estimated at 17,000 tons ; but they can’t be got out
economically. The eo. is liable to get into a pipe or
deposit of good ore at any time, and so the shares have a
value as a gambling counter. Ten years ago they were
included among the “ investment stocks.”
* * * *
One of the best-known of Papuans, Nelson, of
Woodlark, tells Tins Bulletin : “ Warn them against
this new rush. I believe there’s good gold there, hut
men simply can’t get at it. They can’t get ‘ boys.’ The
old hands can’t, and if they can’t, the new ones haven’t a
hope. It’s all the same whether you have £lOO or £lOOO.
For months past all the boats have been going round
recruiting, and they can’t get ‘boys.’ And, of course,
you can’t get there without them. The nearest store is, at
the least, 12 days’ journey from where the gold was
found ; it’s five days from the nearest field, but there’s
no store at that place. As to the gold the prospectors
got, it wasn’t 20000 z., as I’ve read. It wasn’t a tenth
part of that. In fact, it wasn’t lOOoz. What they might
have got I don’t know ; as it happened, they had to push
through, and didn’t get a chance to work the find.”
•* * * »
In theFingall, Day Dawn (W. A.), the thirteenth
level has, at latest advices, been driven over 700 ft. For
the most part it is worth only 2dwt. or less, though about
150 ft. are worth about 6dwt. This practically means that
the bottom of the shoot has been reached, and a new
shoot will have to be looked for—or a new mine. At
the last annual meeting Algernon Moreing told
shareholders that a vigorous policy of prospecting
would be introduced, but so far it isn’t visible. The co.
has a big cash balance, and has written down its equip-
ment and property to merely nominal figures ; and, so
far as bookkeeping goes, is easily the strongest co.
in W.A.
* * * *
Lake View Consols, Kalgoorlie, although still
not much to boast of, is looking rather better than it did
a year ago. The ore reserves have been increased from
87,000 tons, worth 365., or eight months’ supply, to
121,400 tons, worth 30s. 9d., or 11 months’ supply. The
lower levels have always been disappointing, but are
opening up rather better in quantity, though the grade
continues low ; but to some extent this has been balanced
by reduced costs. Co. is interested in various highly
speculative ventures. However, £73,500 bought half the
Broken Hill South Blocks, and this holding is now valued
at £250,000, or about 14s. per L.V. share ; but what the
shares would realise if they came into the market is
another matter. They are probably only worth £250,000
until the co. tries to sell them.
* * * *
Phillips River Gold and Copper, Ravens-
thorpe (W.A.),, has shown a great amount of energy since
its flotation in January last. Co. acquired eight separate
properties by purchase, and three on option, containing
637 acres, and has pushed along developments eagerly.
Klug, formerly manager of the Fremantle Smelter, and
latterly of Boulder Perseverance, is general manager,
with Fred. Morgan, formerly of Golden Horseshoe, as
mine manager—a very fine combination. The parent eo.
is now floating the Mt. Catlin, of 30 acres, into a separate
concern, with a capital of 200,000 shares, of £l, of which
100,000 are set aside to provide working capital. As it
is eventually intended to float off the other ten scattered
properties, it is certain that the present flotation will be
a success. Bull-Cohen is behind the venture, especially
that part of Bull-Cohen known as Rothschild’s Ex-
ploration Co.
* * * m
Flag Gold and Copper is another Ravensthorpe
(W.A.) venture offered to the public. It consists of 50
acres, from which a good deal of gold and copper has
been won. At the Government Smelter, bought by
Kaufman, 205 tons of ore from the show have been
treated for a return of £4BOl, or £23 Bs. per ton, £l5 19s.
representing copper and £7 9s. gold. This was excep-
tionally rich picked ore ; but, all round, 1222 tons have
realised £7 per ton treated. So far the prospectors have
sunk to 114 ft., where the reef is sft. wide. This co is
capitalised at £25,000 in 100,000 shares of ss. each, of
which the vendors get 60,000 and £750 cash. The Flag is
a purely local concern with its office at Ravensthorpe,
and may be handicapped for want of cash. It certainly
will, he handicapped if, in trying to make a market for its
shares, it starts out to distribute any profits it may make
in its early days. That is just where the Queensland
mine, the 0.K., somewhat similarly situated, made a
mistake.

Meekathara, Murchison (W.A.), some 92 miles
from Nannine, is coming to the rescue of the country.
For a distance of 1£ miles, nine mines have been opened
up down to water level (about 150 ft.), and all have given
satisfactory results. Thousands of tons of stone are
awaiting treatment, but, owing to scarcity of water, the
Government mill has only been running one shift daily
and cannot overtake the demand. To date, the Halcyon
has won 12660 z. from 1750 tons, Marmont 58250 z. from
1480 cons, Fenian 54160 z. from 1401 tons, Ingliston Ex-
tended 38490 z. from 6550 tons, Ingliston Consols 45540 z.
from 1519 tons, Commodore South 889 oz. from 316
tons, Commodore 25440 z. from 1655 tons, Inglis-
ton United 104 oz. from 197 tons, and Ingliston 6610 z. from
390 tons. The Ingliston Extended is a Bull co. of 60,000
£1 shares, but all the others are locally-owned, struggling
* * * *
Mararoa, Norseman (W.A.), held under option
by Prnieess Royal Central, still continues to develop
well, and the lode at 240 ft. is worth 60s. per ten,
the same as at 150 ft. With the advent of the railway
working* costs should be reduced. Consulting* engineer
Bill Henderson has great faith in the property, and
there seems little doubt that it will eventually turn out
trumps. The pending* calls are the drawback.

SYDNEY.
Whitford Reward shareholders the other day
(22/U/’O6) gave the directors power in Adelaide to dispose
of the 12,000 shares in reserve. Immediately afterwards
the board accepted Sh offer of Is. 6d. a share for the lot.
This would be all right except for the fact that there was
a market at about 3s. lOd. for the stock, and buyers were
strong. Shares were Is. Bd. the previous day ; but they
were more than double that when the reserved shares
were sold at Is. 6d. Under the circumstances it would
be just as well for the directors to say who the pur-
chasers were. Possibly the sale was really made before
the meeting was held ; in which case, of course, the
meeting was a farce. As the market is still over 3s. some-
body has had a chance to make a fine scoop. The co.’s
intention in raising the new money was to get a working
option over the Callion mine at Davyhurst (W.A.).
The Broken Hill cos. proposed to give 12J per
cent, rise in wages to their workmen when lead is over
£l6 per ton. Rundown this list—the half-yearly aver-
ages of lead, from B.H. Block 10 report, and then ponder
over the generosity of the proposal:—
Mar., 1900 .. £l5 14 9£ Sept., 1903 .. £lllllO
Sept., 1900 .. 17 6 9 Mar., 1904 .. 11 8 9|
Mar., 1901 .. 15 111 Sept., 1904 .. 11 15 8
Sept., 1901 .. 12 3 3J Mar., 1905 .. 12 11 6
Mar., 1902 .. 11 4 6 Sept., 1905 .. 13 8 0
Sept., 1902 .. 11 5 9 Mar., 1906 .. 16 0 3
Mar., 1903 .. 11 8 lj Sept. 1906 .. 16 17 9
Present price is £l9 155., or thereabouts; and share
values are apparently based on that —if they are based, on
anything. Seeing that, instead of the output declining,
it is increasing, while no new outlet for the metal has
been discovered, this list also holds out mighty poor hope
of the price staying where it is.
•k •* * m
The Broken Hill miners and owners have fixed
up their wage-scale. The grotesque proposal to pay an
increase only with lead above £l6 has been abandoned;
and a rate has been fixed for two years instead. The rise
runs from 15 per cent, to about 11 per cent.—the 7s. 6d.-a-
-day surface men get the 15 per cent., and the men who
have been getting 9s. a day or over are to get an extra
Is. A sliding scale, based on metal prices, would be the
nearest approach to a scientific settlement of the
problem; but, if they can help it, the Broken Hill men
won’t have any settlement that doesn’t mention a fixed
time. They remember that they had an open agreement
once before ; and suddenly, without a day’s warning,
that agreement was terminated just at the moment when
it might have done the men some good. So they are
“off ” open agreements.
* * * *
There may be nothing important in the fact
that Block 10, Broken Hill, had a mullock pass squeezed
in, the other day, but it at least shows that the force
which some months back crushed in the upper levels is
still dangerous. The last movement was purely local and
caused no interference with ore-production. Some
slight modifications have been carried out at the mill. It
was previously thought that if the crudes were crushed
to a fine grade' a better concentration would be
effected. But experience proved that the idea was
wrong. Fine grinding resulted in too much sliming, and
the proportion of concentrates was consequently reduced.
A coarser product is now returned from the first crush-
ing, and the adoption of this principle has already had a
good effect. It has reduced the percentage of slimes and
added correspondingly to the concentrates produced.
The grinding pan is not yet installed in Block 10 mill, but
one is on order. All the mines along the line of lode are
going in for grinding pans.
* » * *
Additional slime-dressing appliances
are to be used at Block 10 mill, Broken Hill. The re-
covery of lead in concentrates on that field has improved
of late years, but the improvement depends very much
upon the class of ore treated. Block 10 mill had to deal
with ore assaying 13T per cent, lead and 13'loz. silver ;
for every unit of lead there was an ounce of silver. The
ore was hard and fine-grained, producing an excessive
quantity of slimes. The recovery of 65'69 percent, (or
nearly two-thirds) of the lead looks low, but it is really
very fair work. The recovery of 36'4 per cent, of the
silver seems even worse, but when a large quantity of
slimes are made, heavy losses of both silver and lead are
inevitable. The value of the lead recovered from Block
10 crude ore about equals the value of the silver that is
lost; in other words, as high a money-value is lost in the
tailings as is saved in concentrates. Out of 13 T per cent,
lead ore, Block 10 saved B'6 units and lost 4'5 units
per ton of ore. North B.H. mine, milling 17 per cent,
lead ore, of a coarse grain and good milling quality,
recovers 76 per cent, of the lead, or nearly 13 units ; it
consequently loses four units, or half a unit less than
Block 10. But note the difference in the ore. Block 10
probably did as good work as any other mill could have
done on similar stuff.
* * -Jr
The various zinc-process people are very busy
in Broken Hill, and the battle for the survival of the
fittest should soon begin. There is bound to be a lot of
preliminary fireworks, and only after the smoke has cleared
avyay and the rocket-stick has had time to come down
will the public he able to estimate the real va ue of these
processes. Gillies, inventor of the Gillies process, has
been working night and day for months past, trying to
get a move on his small but compact and expensive plant
on Block 10, but so far has not been able to do much. De
Bavay is also hard at work at his plant on the North
mine, trying to get a sufficiently large production of the
“really beautiful” zinc concentrate he obtains to justify
working costs. On the British mine Huntley, manager
of the Zinc Corporation, with zinc-expert Queneau, is
“ shaking things up,” and this time the co.’s representa-
tives mean to do or die. The lightly-mentioned
“ modification ” of the Potter plant meant the de-
molition of the whole disappointing caboose, and the
disrespectful “ chucking out ” of every acid-smelling pan
in the place. Quite a pretentious building is now being
erected on the ruins of the old experimental plant, and
no expense is being spared to make things “ gee ” this
time. The Elmore process man, who tickles the liquid
product with an electric current, thus securing separa-
tion of the metal particles, is also burning a small experi-
mental candle to attract the festive manager-moth. The
Elmore plant is to rise from the ashes of the Australian
Metal Co.’s dismantled works at Railway Town, and for a
start will be supplied with tailings by the Zinc Corpora-
tion. The Zinc Corporation, having no process of its
own, is glad to encourage anybody who is even suspected
of having such a thing about him.
* * * *
Rumor is busy with the Junction North shaft.
Three times during the past fortnight has she knocked
the shaft out of plumb, and then found out that she
was the same old liar as the ancients found her. There
need be no fear for the Junction North shaft as long as
the Junction ground holds up.
* * * *
The British mine, Broken Hill, was short of ore
last week, owing to the shifty nature of the ground in
Marsh’s section, adjoining the Junction. No firing could
be done on account of the risk, and the process of barring-
down had to be treated with the utmost caution. There
are hot arguments at Broken Hill, based on recent obser-
vation, about what becomes of the ore bodies in Marsh s
section. A knowing crowd says they are all running into
the Junction North and North mines. Those who sup-
port the theory have this to stand on, that Marsh’s ore
body has not been proved below the 600 ft. level, where it
apparently enters the North mine.
As The Bulletin pointed out two or three
months ago, the New White Leads, which is supposed to
have a continuation of the Broken Hill lode, has not yet
got the true ore body at the 400 ft. level. All the time,
however, a few seams of galena, with patches of sulphides
at intervals, have been foisted on the public as the true
ore body coming down from the 300 ft. level. One of the
directors now admits that driving south will still have to
be continued for some distance before any hope can be
entertained of picking up the shoot of ore.

“Adelaide”: “Of the 960,000 Broken Hill
Prop, shares, only a few short of 700,000 are now on the
English register ” (B. 8/11/’O6). Yet there was a time (and

laide owned more than London and Sydney and Melbourne
together—owned more than half of the whole thing.
* * * *
At present the holding in Broken Hill South,
the coming Barrier mine, is cutup something like this:—
Adelaide and Broken Hill, 70,000 ; Sydney, 55,000 ; Mel-
bourne, 45,000 ; London, 30,000. The principal recent
movement has been towards London.
» * * *
The men who play in the present Broken Hill
gamble in the one hope that they won’t be holding the
baby when the school breaks up got cold shivers last
week. In one day Souths tumbled from £8 18s. 6d. to
£8 4s. Things like that set them thinking that when the
slump does come it won’t come in pence.
* * * *
The Budgery shows (N.S.W.) are now practi-
cally all at work, either sinking or costeening—the latter
mostly scratching that should have been done before the
public was asked to come in. Considering its situation,
and that it was so early on the market, Budgery South is
the slowest; and it has much need to hustle, for, though
it must get the lode if the lode carries that far, the
chances are that it will have to do a good deal of sinking
to reach it.

Harden (N.S.W.), having got thi-ough with the
additions to its treatment plant, pays a sixpenny div. in
December ; and there’s sixpence a month in sight for 12
months after that.

“J. Long, mine manager,” writes at vast
length to assert that the West Harden (N.S.W.) is a better
mine than it was given credit for in 8., 16/11/’O6. Two
recent crushings are mentioned —30 tons, which gave
15dwt.; and 90 tons, which gave loz., without the sands.
Such crushings were not disputed. What was affirmed
was that the makes of stone are small; and, though the
property is worth a test, it isn’t worth a boom.

The greatest depth now attained on gold-bear-
ing stpne at Wyalong (N.S.W.) is 1200 ft. At that depth
the True Blue Co. has a 2ft. to 3ft. channel, carrying both
battery and sulphide ore.
* * * m
After many months of prospecting at Mulyan
tinfield, Connolly and party have brought in to Wyalong
13 tons of tin ore, which yielded 1 ton of pure tin con-
centrates over the battery. Taylor and party, from the
Buddigower tin find, treated 50 tons for 1J tons of con-
centrates. With concentrating appliances, the tin de-
posits at these places should pay as long as good markets
favor them.
* # * *
Latest thing on the market is the Elsie Tin Mining
Co., a New England (N.S.W.) proposition, with some of
the Commonwealth Oil Corporation people behind it.
Proposed co. is in 25,000 shares of 10s. each, of which the
promoters and vendors take half and £lOO, though appa-
rently all that the vendors and promoters have done is to
put in a few pegs, apply for the land and write a prospectus.
Two reports are submitted. One says that there is
£520,800 worth of tin available ; the other says the gross
value of tin likely to be won is £185,492. There seems
enough discrepancy between these figures to suggest that,
if the ground has been tested at all, it has only been tested
in a very perfunctory manner; and the public has a
very good right to sniff with suspicion at estimates put
forward under such circumstances.
* * * ■*
The weak point in most of the Tingha (N.S.W.)
tin propositions, in which Melbourne is much more inte-
rested than Sydney, is showing up already ; the first
month of summer finds them threatened with an early
failure of the indispensable water supply.

Baker’s Creek (N.S.W.), 350 tons for 9840 z.,
including concentrates ; yet the return did not cause a
ripple in Sydney. Adelaide, however, jumped the shares
up a little. A number of weak holders got out at ridicu-
lously low prices, if last crushing is any guide ; but, un-

* * * *
New Moon, Bendigo, will give its shareholders
a Christmas box of 4s. per share. It has now divided £l3
per share, and the promise of future prosperity is assured
if the directors agree to let the manager infuse some
more life into the working of the property. It needs a
little hard driving to turn to account the low-grade stone
at the south shaft. The best thing the directors could do
would be to send the manager to Westralia before he starts
to joggle out the south shaft quartz. The co. can do
cheap work, for to date it has put through 300,000 tons
at an average cost of about 14s. per ton. As the grade
of the dirt has been 9|dwt., dividends have come with
regularity. Furthermore, discretion has tempered the
beating-out of the stone. This is more than can be
said of one or two mines near by. The New Moon’s
record looks well indeed. Capital called up, 3s. 6d. per
share on 24,000 shares; total paid capital, £4200; tons
treated to September 30, 389,405 ; gold won, 175,8480 z. ;
dividends paid, £312,328. Divs. work out at 53 per cent,
of the gold won, and working expenses have been 40 per
•cent. South New Moon, next door, shows better figures,
but it has come pretty well to a standstill. They do for
comparison, however: Capital called up, 17s. 7d. on
32,000 shares, or a total of £28,133; tons crushed,
203,319 ; gold, 163,7510 z. ; dividends, £421,600. There-
fore, the difference in favor of the South is over
£lOO,OOO in divs., the high-grade stone yanked out during
the last years enabling it to establish this record. To
makeup a handicap of £lOO,OOO is a long way to travel,
but New Moon is so well handled and has such chances
with its south shaft stone that there seems to be every
hope that it will at least get level with its neighbor. But
as it has now low-grade stone tp deal with, the old
methods will hardly do. .
* * # »
Chalk’s No. 3, Carisbrook, has got some cash in
hand, after a long up-hill drag, which tired out most of
the old hands. Co.’s accounts show up a shade worse
than those of the majority of deep lead concerns, and are
instructive as proving the heap of cash that is swept
away before profits are reached with such mines.
Capital amounting to £78,383 has been called up,
and the gold won is valued at £133,430. All
this has been mopped up, as well as a Government grant
of £5OOO. Two shafts have had to be sunk, and a big
block of the claim has been worked-out to get to the
present stage. Wash now averages 14dwt. per fathom,
and it takes 12dwt. to pay for, this if the work done at
No. 2 shaft is debited, as it ought to be. The co. has a
block of ground ready at the No. 1 shaft. This will soon
be drawn on to its full extent. There is more than a
little market uneasiness as to its limits and as to
the width of the run of gold. In the No. 2 shaft
the wash is still green and ought to be let stand to drain.
The co.’s report just out isn’t half as explicit as that of
the Chiltern Valley or Great Southern, Rutherglen.
These latter give details of cost, and tell plainly the width
of the runs of gold. The public won’t jump at Chalk’s
No. 3 until there is definite evidence on this doubtful
point. It is this hiatus in the report that makes the
directors’ assurances that there need be no fear over the
area of the wash at No. 1 shaft appear so exceedingly
thin. The market takes the rumor to the contrary rather
seriously, and it isn’t often far out.
* * ■ te ■*
Ballarat takes a very low place in the gold list
nowadays. As a producer, Bendigo, during the Septem-
ber quarter, headed the fields of the State with 45 2980 z.
and divs. £68,624; Walhalla, with only the two’ Long
Tunnel mines, is second with 11,5570 z. and £13,200 divs
Then Buckland, Harrietville, and Bright, all dredging
districts, gave 63200 z. and divs. £8240 ; next Chiltern and
Ru.therglen, 12,8180 z., divs. £5BOO, all from the Chiltern
Valley and Great Southern ; then Pitfield and Berrinea
gold 10,2200 z., divs. £3700 ; and then sixth and penulti-
mate, Ballarat, 13,7910 z., divs. £2lOO. p
Band and Loch, Ballarat, shows some remark-
able figures, even for that mining Sahara. As the Sir
Harry Loch it paid consistent divs. ; but then the shoot
of gold pitched into the Star of the Bast, and lean years
followed. This brought about amalgamation with its
neighbor, so that to-day the co. owns to the name of the
Band and Loch United. As such the mine has yielded gold
worth £124,000, and shareholders have paid up £46,000,
with nary a div. to cheer them on a cold and chilly call-
paying course. On the crushing done last half-year this
ought not to be the case, for 4100 tons averaged fully
lOdwt. The mine, however, is worked on the dog’s-hind-
leg system, and there can be small hope of anything
better until Homeric attempts are made to cheapen costs
by putting the mine in proper trim. As it is, every level
the co. sinks under present conditions handicaps it more
and more. If Manager Mason hits the shoot of stone he
is searching for at 2303 ft. he ought to tackle the problem.
Otherwise Band and Loch, like the rest of the Plateau
mines, must continue to be a mining delusion.
* * * *
Up comes an old titled friend in the person of
the Duke of Wellington, Bamganie (Vic.). The result of
the Duke of York corner prevented any like prac-
tices with the Duke of Wellington, so it had to
live on its gold. This turned out so well as to save
the fields, and to hold the allegiance even of those who
did not like the smell of the Duke of York coup. But
the show now has dwindled down to a contract claim of
the Ballarat order. Those who work down below on that
field know what that means. It probably explains, too,
the item “ contractors, £908,” in the last balance-sheet.
Co.’s calls last half-year totted up to £1261, or about
half the value of the gold won. Law costs ate up £6OO,
and, lumped on to “misappropriation, £521,” just about
took all that the shareholders subscribed. The deficiency,
by a neat bit of sarcasm, has been debited to “working
account.”
* * * *
In north-east Victoria just now they talk little
except buckets and barges. At Bright the various
Tewksburys are gladdening the hearts of scrip-holders
(mostly Sydneysiders) with big divs. The other lot are
also mostly on payable wash. Fletcher’s claims on
Yackandandah Creek are on good gold. Lower down the
tributors on Mrs. J. A. Wallace’s Rowdy Flat barge are
getting something better than wages. The widow’s barge
at Allan’s Flat is giving a satisfactory return, and her
claim at Baranduda is now doing a little better. There
is also a chance of the reefs at Twist Creek being worked.
The English co. now working at Rocky Point has taken
up the Polar Star lease, and is erecting a battery. A
Yackandandah syndicate has been raising ounce stone for
some months from a reef near the Polar Star, and every
reef in the locality has been pegged out. A cyanide plant
has also started at Twist Creek.
* * * •
The Maori Queen gold dredge, Bright (Vie.),
is a cheap worker. The money value of the 5300yds. of
washdirt handled per week averaged s’3d. per yard ; ex-
penses took 2'57d., renewals 0'57d., and profits were a
shade over 2d. per yard lifted. Profits should amount to
over £4O per week on this basis ; say £2OOO a year. But
depreciation is heavy, and the margin of profit is so
narrow that there is no room for high capitalisation of
stock.
* * * »
“Old Bendigonian ” (who isn’t an interested
party): Apropos G. D. Meudell and the Bendigo City
float. Every statement made by the promoters concern-
ing this mine can be amply verified on the spot. As to
the “ little swag of paid-up shares,” those shares are only
paid up to the amount of the first allotment money.
Whenever it is necessary to make a call—that is, in the
event of the money placed to the credit of the co. not
being sufficient to put the mine on payable gold—these
so-called paid-up shares will have to pay the same as the
rest. The Golden City Co.’s lease is right inside the most
highly auriferous square mile of the Bendigo goldfield,
and, until the ground is proved, no one can say
that it will not be as rich as the best. For nearly 50
years it was occupied with building allotments,
and anyone who knows the terrors which promoters
have to face under the Mining on Private Property
law, will not begrudge the little lot of paid-ups, &c.
Bendigo, from the surface down to 400 ft., was, generally
speaking, much richer than Bendigo below 400 ft. All the
grand old Bendigo mines whose names are before the
public were rich above 400 ft., and that is why they have
since been carried down to such great depths. The land
of the Bendigo City Co. was shut against miners in the
early days. It was utterly impossible to do anything
prior to the passing of the Mining on Private Property
Act, and even now that there is such an Act, promoters
need to be men who are not afraid of preliminary trouble
or of law and clerical expenses either, to tackle a block of
ground covered by 31 citizens’ residences, and owned or
part owned by about 40 different persons.
« * * *
“ Pea Green ” : .Recently had a few days round
Arltunga, in the far north of S. Aus., and saw the mica
field, 50 miles out. There is a good waggon road from the
Arltunga Government ore-reduction works as far as
Claraville, eight miles out. From there on it is any sort
of a track, easily followed, however, by keeping in
sight of tinned-dog shells and the empty bottles.
A pub.-keeper on the route was eloquent on, the
subject of mica mining, and incidentally said that
his city grog accounts nearly doubled during the last
co.’s ten months’ existence there. He “can’t make out
’ow the Adelaide people don’t put more money into the
shows. There’s mobs of good mica buried in them
mullock-heaps yet.” Well, having seen the shows, this
writer can tell how no more money is available for that
area. No mining worth mentioning has been done ; old
mullock-heaps have been fossicked for a few cwt. of
mica worth about £75, at a cost, in one case, of
£lOOO. Waste, waste everywhere. At White Range
the same old wickedness of many sorts is still
rampant—whisky and gins ; the latter do a good deal
of the “mining.” Great institution this tribute busi-
ness! The Excelsior, a long way the best show there,
has been on tribute for three years ; co. not spending a
penny on the lease ; board drawing fees ; tributors crush-
ing at Government'works, which actually collects the
royalty for the co. The royalty is just enough to pay
offices exes., and leave a few shillings over. Didn’t see
Winnecke’s, but heard it was dead.
* * * *
Tarcoola Blocks, the Adelaide co. which is
putting up a better struggle with its far-north S. Aus. mine
than any other concern in that State, is getting in plant
to allow the deep ground to be tried. From flat surface
stone, followed for as. much as 500 ft. on the underlay, ■
£lOO,OOO worth of gold has been got out. The blocks are
a network of narrow reefs, and the chances are that they
either cut out altogether or junction at depth. A verti-
cal shaft will find out which it is. Costs are deadly:
cartage has to be done by, camel teams—they hitch the
ghostly beasts to waggons—and timber is so scarce that
for shaft work Westralian jarrah is used.
* * * *
Drilling will start early in December to settle
whether the old Kapunda copper mine (S. A.) was a sur-
face deposit, caught in the folds of the rock, or a lode. -.
Meantime there is a quantity of low-grade (l| percent.)
stone in the mine, of which a couple of truck-loads were
sent to Broken Hill to be experimented with. Fine
crushing and the Wilfleys make a concentrate that will
show a profit, with copper down to £BO. Point now is to
determine the approximate quantity of this stone.

North • Queensland’s wet season has started
earlier than usual with a fast fall of 4|in. This will make
things good for the tin-streamers, but is likely to make
things bad for such mines as the Vulcan, Dalzell’s, O.K.
and others that have haulage difficulties. Their output
will be more or less interfered with up to, say, April.

* * * *
Bulletin remarked a few weeks ago that
between then and 12 months hence, when Mt. Elliott and
other Cloncurry (Q.) shows will begin to really show their
paces, there would be some pretty games in the market.
One was played this last week, when shares jumped from
about 435. to 70s.—merely on buying-orders from London.
Fact is that the shares are so well held that, if a buyer
arrives, he has to pay to get in, and then a section of the
public always wants a leg-in in anything that has a rising
market. These latter are mostly weak holders, who
don’t buy for keeps ; and the chances are that before the
12 months are up, they will sell out at 3fts. or 355. Pro-
bably prices will be got down there or thereabouts to
oblige them.
Henry, who discovered the Oloncurry (Q.)
copper field 40 years ago, has hung on to the place with
pathetic fidelity. His dream of amassing millions still
visits him nightly. The confidence of a miner is
unshakable.

The Manual, a London publication, prints the
typical sort of yarn on which J. Bull is being hustled
into “ Barriers.” One of recent date was that the South
Blocks shaft is 900 ft. deep, and has seven levels devel-
oped. If J. Bull would divide these figures by three, he
would get near the truth. Presently, when J. Bull finds
out and swears, the bad name that Australia will get will
be put down to Socialistic legislation as usual.

One of the special points to be considered by
the Broken Hill British Mine in the extension of their
present mill, was a suitable rockbreaker. There are
several types of crushers at the Hill, but the management
of the British decided to purchase Austin Rotary Rock-.
breakers after exhaustive enquiry and practical work
with other makes. The Block 10 00. purchased two
Austin’s some time ago, and these were put to a very
severe test for capacity, horsepower, and wearing ability
—the result has been a triumphant and conclusive proof
that the Austin is the best machine at present offering,
crushing more material with less h.p. than other makes.
They have forced lubrication, double counter shaft
bearings, and are of heavy construction to withstand all
strain. W. and J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, are the sole
agents."

The Charlotte Plains Consolidated G. M.
Oo.’s new Havillah Shaft has just installed a high
lift pump to make all necessary provision against
any increase in the volume of water now that they
are opening up large areas of wash dirt. This instal-
lation is in addition to the large three throw pump
which has given such high efficiency and such constant
service since its inception. The new pump is of the high
lift turbine type, electrically driven, and is capable of
lifting 2,016,000 gallons of water in 24 hours against a
total head of 350 feet. The mechanical efficiency of the
pump is guaranteed at 71 - 5 per cent, and all repairs and
replacements apart from undue wear are likewise
guaranteed for twelve months. The pump has four
runners and four vanes made from best bronze and the
shaft is attached by means of a flexible coupling to a
Ganz three phase motor 440 volts pressure, 60 cycles
capable of giving 207 h.p. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have executed this con-
tract. This Company has had the manufacture of most
of the big volume deep alluvial lead pumping schemes in
Victoria and has met in each with unqualified success.* [A]

of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a

coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral

The Reavell Air Compressor is an entirely new
departure in compressor design, and by its numerous
good points is already finding considerable favor in Au -
tralian mining circles. As there are no suction valves,
the volumetric efficiency is at least 5 per cent higher than
•any other compressor. It is unrivalled for driving by
means of an electric motor, and is the only machine in
which driving gears can be altogether dispensed with.
The sole licensees for Australia are Babcock and Wilcox,
makers of the well-known water tube boiler, whose Aus-
tralasian office is at the Haymarket, Sydney.*

ABORGIN
ALITIES
“Skyp”: A policeman with a strange
report has struck terror into the simple hearts of
the blacks at the camp at La Perouse (N.S.W.).
Interested persons in high places have decreed
their removal to Kurnel, across Botany Bay.
The present reserve is to be made into a scraggy
park, where the sixpenny tram-trotter may dis-
port himself without the disquieting sight of a
black face. This is villainous treatment ; for on
Kurnel side the land is more exposed, the fishing
is poorer, and the men will get no sale for their
shell-work and native weapons, the sole degenerate
industries left to them.

“ Jans” : I wish to enter the North-East
Dunday tramway (Tas.) for the slow train com-
petition. Length of railway, Zeehan to Williams-
ford, is 18 miles; schedule time three hours.
That’s for the express. The heavy goods (fire-
wood) usually does a trip a day, though occasion-
ally it has to camp out on the hillside for the
night, and drags itself into Zeehan next forenoon.
“ Agricola ” : The lyre bird is just about
played-out as a subject for discussion in the
“Abo.” column. But there is one little pecu-
liarity about it which I have never yet seen
touched upon. It seems to take the most pride
in its dancing and mimicry in the depth of
winter, when the season for grand opera among
the other birds has not commenced. In the
spring-time, it is not nearly so joyous, and, any-
way, its performance is over-shadowed by the
hosts of warblers who are busy quenching the
silence of the bush. To get it at its best,
you must choose a miserable winter evening, with
snow half-way to your knees, and a knocked-up
horse under you. While you are hankering for a
season-ticket to Sheol, you may hear the feathered
lunatic in the gully below you, yelling at the top
of its voice all the scraps of last year’s warm-
weather songs it can remember. Then you do
not feel at all grateful for the music, and consider
its cheerfulness very much misplaced. It is at
moments like these that the Birds’ Protection
Act becomes a dead letter, and, if weapons are at
hand, the “lyre” stands a good show of becoming
an exceedingly ditto bird.
* * * *
A record just completed by a Scone
(N.S.W.) cow will take some beating. Outwardly
she s plain, ordinary “moo”; her unusualness
consists in the fact that for the past five years
consecutively she’s come up as the proud moo-ma
of twins. The fifth pair (males) arrived during
November, and bring her record up to ten calves
m a little more than four years. The offspring
were neither weak nor deformed in any way, and
all were successfully reared.

The Australian long-distance stock-droving
record has again been put up—as usual by a
Queenslander. In June, 1905, one Rose left
Lissadell Station, on the Ord River (Westralia),
with a mob of 3000 cattle for Charleville (Q.), a
distance of 3000 miles—one bullock per mile.
When about half-way across the Northern
Territory he encountered drought conditions, and
turned north into the ranges. He zig-zagged up
and down the water-courses for months, and
gradually made his way eastward, finally coming
into good country by the Nicholson River route.
At times it was necessary to send exploring
parties as much as 150 miles ahead to locate safe
camping places. Rose’s party consisted of twenty
men, and the cattle, to the number of 2600, were
delivered at Charleville in splendid condition, and
sold on November 14. Rose gets great praise for
his feat. The 2600 bullocks get killed.

“Douglas T.”: Out here in the flooded
part of Riverina (between Barham and Moula-
mein) shearers have their troubles just now. I
spoke to one a few days ago. He was literally
wading from a shed to the drowsy little town-
ship on the Murray. A long stretch of the
road he had to travel was covered with water, in
some places deep enough to reach his chin. Still
he looked happy and irresponsible, although han-
dicapped with a bike and swag attachment. His
cheque he intended to carry in his ear. He got
through all right, too, though how he managed
with the bike and Matilda is a puzzle. A citizen
such as this is of more value to the Commonwealth
than a cubic mile of Judkinses.
“Paul Cupid”: Can “ Prooshan ” or
“ San Remo,” or anybody who habitually carries
snakes about explain why he escapes being bitten,
or whence he gets the power (possibly from
Gehenna) to do with the squirming things what
others can’t ? Bike the above scribes, I knew an
Australian bushman who used to carry all kinds
of serpents. Sometimes he would call at a neigh-
bor’s on his way home from work and stay for tea,
and when paterfamilias was imploring grace on
the provender a serpent would crawl out of the
visitor’s shirt and look into the countenance of
the “old man,” who invariably tacked a fearful
swear on to the grace, and took a back somersault
and overturned the meal on to the floor. The
man walked into the local editorial sanctum one
day, to complain re an allegedly scurrilous par
published about him. He pulled a couple of
snakes out of his shirt and put them on the table
preparatory to fighting the scribe. The scribe
screamed for help, and three or four comps,
rushed in, but the injured citizen poked the right-
hand snake in their faces and kept them at bay
while he hit the editor with the left-hand reptile.
This man was never bitten, he did no hocus pocus
business with the snakes, he never tried to make
anything out of his peculiar power, and he could
not tell why a snake never tried to bite him. The
unbitten man calls for investigation.

“ Hassan ” : The green cicada is evidently
a believer in punctuality. Last year I heard him
first on Oct. 28. This year he chose the same date
for his appearance. And when one considers that
he is three years (or so it is believed) on the
earth, arid that he has no calendar nor watch, this
performance is one commanding respect. The
tjny ones, no larger than blue-bottle flies, which
sing on the reeds, usually make their appearance
a couple of weeks earlier, while the “floury
baker,” adorned with two white sacks in
which the sound organs are developed, invariably
waits until the end of December. And, by the
way, if the editor will permit me to mention the
fact—any aboriginality contributor in the Nor-
thern or North-Western Territory who wishes to
advance the cause of science (and my collection)
can do so by sending me any specimens of these
insects (locusts) which fly on to his hat. [Any
locusts sent to this, office will be thrown into the
ash-bin.—Ed. B.]

“ Huffin-Guffin ” : I was riding in one of
Bent’s trains from Ararat to Hamilton last week,
and the stately, casualness of the journey gave
me plenty of time to cake some observations. On
the private leasehold settlement around Willaura
(formerly Wickliffe Load) the generally healthy
appearance of the crops and the wholly unhealthy
appearauceof the settlers’ “houses” were notice-
able. The landlords impose strict conditions
upon the tenants to ensure, as far as human
agency can go, a crop that will pay the rent.
The tenants’ habitations are probably elaborate
enough to correspond with all they have left after
paying the rent. That does not concern the lord
of the manor-house, auyhow. All that troubles
him is the rent.
“ Bon ” : Concerning that porpoise on
which “Yarrum” held a literary post mortem.
(B. 18/10/’O6), I am puzzled over the mortality
caused by the eating of the carcase. On whaling
ships the sighting of a school of porpoises is hailed
as a fine opportunity of obtaining fresh meat, and
the most expert harpooner is deputed to the bows.
Following a successful strike the whole ship is
soon redolent with the appetising odor of fried
porpoise steaks. It is stated by whalers that the
flavor much resembles that of pork, and forms a
welcome relief to the monotony of the salt-horse
cask. Porpoise-catching is not a sport for the
tender-hearted, as the victim, on being towed
alongside, cries in a manner horribly suggestive
of a child in acute pain. “Yarrum’s” subject
must, I think, have joined the Stinking Fish
party, whose chief habitat seems to be the shores
of Sydney harbor.
* * * *
“ 8.H.D.” : I noticed, during a recent
trip to the Mallee districts of Victoria, an unex-
pected scarcity of rabbits. Inquired the reason,
and was informed everywhere that it was closer
settlement (encouraged by the bursting up of
such large estates as Springfield, Marlbed and
Karyrie) which had caused the bunny-famine.
Yet the rabbit at one time threatened to take
possession of the whole Mallee area, and in the
days of the great estates it seemed as if neither
man nor beast would ever shift him.
THE OUTBACK’S HOTEL AUSTRALIA!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bulletin, stand chided ; stand sworn at; cower re-
buked ! To tantalise us by advertising “ Hotel Australia,
12s. 6d. per day,” in your “ Abo.” column. What next?
When I lay me down to ponder where the bull-ants
nimbly crawl
O’er my weary limbs at noonday by the lonely barren
track,
Then in marble halls in Sydney, in sweet dreams I softly
sprawl,
Till the claret-visaged waiter, puffing, puts me on my
back.
Soon my brow is fanned by Phyllis, while my heels rest
on a star,
And my soul has found a haven where the pianolas
play.
In a hostel by the harbor where the wealth and fashion
are
I’ve dined like any shepherd king at twelve-and-six
a day!
Through the haze o’ recollections I remember once on
earth
In the woe-days I was grafting for King Cocky for my
keep ;
It was damper, bear and treacle—then I chuckle in my
mirth
To think that in the stripper through the starry night
I’d sleep !
Waitah ! Pass me, please, the chablis, just a slice of
pheasant, and
When you’ve cashed this doubtful fiver, bring me in a
bob cigah—
Ough! Is that a live electric wire that fell upon my
hand?
What! A bull ant—wandering loose in the Hotel
Orstraliah !
vie.

YES!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When city life and sordid strife grew raptureless to me,
A kind acquaintance impregnated with poetic fire,
Averred the pathless Bush, with its profound serenity,
Would iny dejected soul with peace and poesy inspire.
He said the Bush was tranquil—ever tranquil and
content.
That birds sang careless carols in the intertangled trees,
And winding bush-tracks wooed the wand’ring redolence
of scent,
The indolence of butterflies and industry of bees.
He raved about its breeze-blown hills, its moss-clad rocks
and creeks,
The brightness of its noonday sun, the radiance that
imbues
Its sunset skies ; its shady alcoves Contemplation seeks ;
Its blossoms and its ever drifting leaves, and crystal
dews.
And many other wild delights be pictured to my mind,
Till on a pilgrimage into the Bush I did embark,
And when 1 got well into it, the traits he had defined
I noticed, and discovered he was pretty near the mark.
The noonday sun was brig'ht. By snakes, it was —red,
blazing bright!
Some birds did sing', especially the crow, and no mistake
The pathless Bush was tranquil—yes, so tranquil through
the night
That I could hear the dingoes breathing as I lay awake.
I met no sordid mortals there, and evidently I
Was all there was—at least, the bush-flies thought there
were no more;
And to the haunts of Nature I was welcomed (warmly) by
Carnivorous mosquitoes, also ants in search of gore.
I found the sky exactly where he said I would. The
hills
Were there, large hills, good sudden hills, fine solid hills—
and he
Was right about the busy bees, their application thrills
The mortal mind—in fact, it thrilled some other parts of
me.
He said the dew would fall. It did—good, saturating dew !
Its crystal sheen I didn’t notice much, but it was wet,
Beal wet; and as he prophesied, the crickets carolled too,
All night; also the mopokes (I can hear the beggars yet).
I did recline upon the shaded grasses, thoughtlessly,
And suddenly arose and spoke a long and dreadful curse ;
I watched the dry leaves, drifting ever, flop into my tea —
They gave me indigestion, and I never had it worse.
The winding tracks he mentioned, they do wind all they
know ;
The Bush was rather pathless when I struck a mulga
scrub.
He said commercial clamor there was absent—it was so—
Conspicuously absent! Damned if I could find a pub.
But still I hadn’t met the weird mirage. I wish I had ;
The imitation even of some water would to me
Have been a comfort; but, unlike most wand’rers, I went
mad
Without mirages, and I’m just regaining sanity.
VIC.
ALFRED E. BRIDGES.

“ F.L. J.” : “ Agricola ” (B. 8/11/’O6) says
tame dogs cannot be induced, either by flattery,
bribery or coercion, to enter into any familiarity
with a “porcupine,” and, even if they did, the
result would be failure. “Agricola” should
change his brand of dog. I have seen as many as
seven “porks” killed in one night by the right
brand. They were hard nuts to crack, but the
dogs in every case had the same mode of attack.
When “porky” is discovered he commences to
burrow’, but, in soft ground, the dog can shift
twdce as much earth. The dog gets alongside and
scratches away until he is able to grab porky’s
leg. Then he carries his victim to some place
where the nature of the ground is in his favor,
and the rest doesn’t take long. [But how does
the dog get the porcupine out of his spiky casing,
even when “the nature of the ground is in his
favor ” ; and what kind of ground prevents the
spikes being indigestible ? — Ed. B.]
* * * *
In deference to Judkins of the unsub-
stantiated charges, the Vic. Agricultural Depart-
ment has decided to put “ the kybosh ” on the
egg-laying competitions which have been a
feature of its agricultural college work during the
past few seasons. It is well-known that the
students wagered freely on the results, and every
college worthy of the name had its tote, in which
some hens have paid large dividends. The long-
distance hen that was all behind in the early
stages of the race, but came with a rush in the
straight, laying a wild stream or cataract of eggs as
it travelled, often had only a single backer, and he
raked in everything. So the department has shut
down. The eggs and the odds were becoming too
much alike.

“Janor”: With one hand I seize my
crested wipe to remove the foam from “F.H.R’s.”
lips, and I grasp my best gold pen in the other to
implore you to decide as suddenly as possible
which is the corner of the Commonwealth where
“snakes are thickest,” as “F.H.R.” phrases it
(B. l/11/’O6). I want thick snakes. No snake
can be too thick. I’m in a hurry to take up
“ F.H.R’s.” defiance. I want to blow the froth
off that fiver. I’m not a stickler re conditions,
but stipulate that the slaying happens within
reasonable distance of a pub. ; that articles be
signed at Speewa head station, and that Crooked
Mick be judge. I’ll trot along my own referee,
and I don’t care a snake’s foot who referees for
“F.H.R.”
* * * *
Golden Grove (S.A.) has produced a new
and militant breed of rats, which got right away
with five young pigs in two days. The week-old
pork-babies disappeared mysteriously from the
face of the earth, and not till the stone paving of
the sty was pulled up was their fate explained.

Those who pass a Chinese vegetable
garden near Narromine (N.S.W.) may see the
humiliating spectacle of two white girls of about
16 working in the vegetable patch amidst a gang
of Chinamen. Can Bruce Smith, Cole and Co.
spare the time to go to Narromine?

“ Quan ” on prickly pear :
Am 1 Kidston ? “ Oaber ” (B. 8/11/’O6) evidently thinks
so. If not, why does he say “No hope in ‘ Quan’s ’ 2s. 6d.
per acre idea ” ? It had been asked (B. 16/8/’O6) : “ What
is Queensland going to do about its prickly-pear curse ?
“Oaber” (B. 30/8/’O0) gave the history of various un-
successful efforts to get rid of the porcupine vegetable,
and finished up by stating that “ Queensland looks to
Kidston to take action.” This was an insinuation that
no action had been taken in the matter. Well, I told
him about the action of Kidston, or rather the Minis-
tries with which Kidston has been, and is, associated,
and I gave him the official figures concerning the
amount of land taken up as prickly-pear selec-
tions. So why call it my idea? “Caber” says
closer settlement is the only remedy for the fretful
vegetable. I didn’t say that it wasn’t. But would he
know closer settlement’from an improvement lease if he
saw the two in company with a land agent ? Up to how
many acres in one block does he consider closer settle-
ment? Within the first few days of January next 60
prickly selections will be thrown open, two of which are
10 and 12 acres respectively. Is that anything like closer
settlement in “Caber’s” opinion? And the present
system of dealing with the spiky offence isn’t rapid
enough for him. Does he remember a certain proverb
about the building of Rome ? And because Foxton failed
to get any applications for a 50 years’ lease of the
Canning Creek lands, he assumes that Kidston’s Ministry
will experience a similar fate in the matter of beastly
pear selections. But the crowd with which Foxton was
politically associated failed to do a number of things that
Kidston and Co. have done. For one thing, it failed to
omit the amount received from the sale of public lands
from its revenue accounts. Anyhow, I would advise
“ Caber ” to study the figures I gave (B. 4/10/’O6), and lie
will see that Foxton’s marvellous record is already badly
beaten.

“ Janor ” : The “battler ” who drives the
whip-horse up at the mine is an original youth,
old for his age, and filled right up to the lid with
surprises. The lid flew open the other day, and
the surprise that poppied out was addressed to a
large snake, which had eluded all ordinary attacks.
The medium was the fruit of the hen. Removing
that portion of
the egg that,
under happier
auspices, might
one day have
sported feathers,
the snake charm-
er re-filled the
shells with a
mixture of tar-
taric acid and
carbonate of
soda, neatly
covered the per-
forations with
white paper, and
arranged the egg
show temptingly
inanestnear the
shaft. It was
surprising how
quickly that
snake found
them and swal-
lowed the whole
dozen. He rose
at once and took
charge of the
atmosphere, and
hurtled round
like an electric
sky rocket, full
of volts and
ohms and other
strange devils
galore. After a
whirling, flash-
ing and plung-
ingshow, hegave
an exhibition of
ground-tumbling
unique and earn-
est. The tool-
sharpener, who
had shovelled
sawdust when a
youth in Chia-
rini’s circus, and
was an authori-
ty, said it was
thefinest“turn”
of carpet contor-
tions he'd ever
seen. “Co’rseit
is,” said the Battler, “ain’t he a carpet snake?”
Meanwhile, the medicated reptile grew in girth
until he presented quite an anti-Sosh figure. After
failing dismally in the attempt to turn himself
inside out, he tried to crawl down his own throat
and remove the effervescence with violence, but the
effort was too much. He exploded with such a
loud report that the whip-horse bolted, rushing
along the horse-path through a dense shower of
scales and desiccated snake and egg-shells till the
tightening of the hauling-rope threw him vio-
lently, breaking his off leg. He is now dead, bo
is tlie snake. The boy still lives ; but, thank
Heaven, he’s looking for a job, which, in this
importing country, is nearly as bad as being dead.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

Dear Bulletin, —Yes, I know. “To
other men and 7.” And in a little tiling I fancied,
too (“ The Bard of Furthest Out ”). Read both
proof and revise, too. The thing hit me at mid-
night, and I haven’t slept since. “To other men
and me,” of course it should read. But then it isa
rhyme, so 1 can’t put it on the comps, this time
(though they be agreeable), nor the editor, nor
the sub., nor the devil. But I might blame it on
the other devil-—or the shade of my old bush
schoolmaster. He always used the first personal
pronoun in speaking, when he had a preposition
before it for an excuse—no matter what preposi-
tion. “ D’yer think the Lord is goin’ to alter the
weather for you or 7, me boy?” (re praying for
rain). And it gets me yet, when I’m hurried or
not feeling well. Or feeling too well. The school-
master with the highest marks in our district
always said, “Don’t do that no more.” And I
won’t, I’m afraid. Clarke Russell said, “All
hands was lost.” So I’m not alone. I spell
Friday with two “ y’s ” yet, and “anxciety ” thus
—with a “e,” as my old master always did it in
the copy-books. But Sir Walter Scott always
spelt journal “gurnal.” And Josh Billings was
worse. —Yours truly, Henry Lawson.

FAT’S WIFE’S ADVICE.
Socialism can be best exposed by showing that it is destructive of the family life.— Women': National League Manifesto.
Mrs. Fat (cheerfully) : “My dear, if you vote for the Labor candidate your comfortable little home will he taken away from you.”
Mrs. Unemployed ( drearily): “Yes, and who’ll get it ? Does any one want a home like this?”

PERSONAL
