“ He used to sneak down the back stairs
and go to the races.” —Willis, describing Crick’s
conduct when N.S. W. Minister for Lands.
Every State Parliamentary member for the
Federal district of Adelaide, with the wives
of the married legislators and the sisters of
the bachelors, signed the nomination paper of
C. C. Kingston. There was no opposition.
A recent defaulter, who got out of his
visible difficulties by suicide, owed his troubles to
neither gambling nor drink, but to the other chief
weakness of swift men. Now, no less than Hvo
damsels mourn his loss, or theirs. Theirs amounts,
in each case, to 7s. fid. per Week,
SO LITTLE NOISE TO SO MUCH BULK.
Reid is merely puffing up a palpable pretence.—Age.
GRANNIE : “ Lor lumme, a lion wouldn't shamble
about murmurin' like that. ROAR, FEORGE,
ROAR ! Remember, you're not a bloomin'
suckin’ dove.”
Premier Tom Bent, of Victoria, is con-
tracting the falling-off habit almost as badly as
Governor Tom Brassey had it. He seldom gets
in or out of a cab without falling off the step.
When he gets out of his motor-car somebody
always stands close up, so that when he falls (he
always does) he may cannon up into the perpendi-
cular again. At Netherley, last week, Tommy
fell off the plain level earth, and got stunned.
He tripped over a log, his protuberant wealth of
body having hidden the log from view. Tommy
was unconscious for a few minutes, hut recovered,
and made the usual incoherent sort of speech,
urging people to go on the land, or something like
that.
James Whiteside McCay, now standing
for Corio (Vic.) as the
anointed of Anti-Sosh,
owes his present posi-
tion as a barrister,
and an officah and
gentleman, to the fact
that he won a State-
school scholarship
when he was a small
white-headed urchin
at Castlemaine, some
years ago. That is
to say, if it hadn’t
been for the Socialistic
system which insti-
tuted scholarships, by
which smart boys are
able to gain a second-
ary education free of
charge, James White-
side might now be toy-
ing with the end of a
pick-handle and pay-
ing his dues to the
local branch of the
P.L.L.
A candidate named Tilly Brown is running
for Isaacs’ seat at Indi (Vic.). Mr. Matilda Brown
is a colorless person, as his name would imply,
and when asked what he believes in, replies,
“ Mr. Reid.” He has been urged to retire in the
interests of Cameron’s parrot, said parrot being a
notorious bird possessed by a bush publican.
Even the whizz and the trail of the motor-
car have their uses, the apple-growers of “ Zomer-
zedshire ” in the Cold Country reporting that,
owing to the dust or smell from these cars, there is
less blight in the orchards than there used to be.
Now we know why God made Willie-motor Kelly.
First He made little apples, and then He made
little Kelly.
“ S.S. ” : How are the mighty fallen!
Near Nicholson-street, Melbourne, I came upon
a small portable pie and coffee stall. It
sported a sign almost as big as itself, in-
scribed “Mrs. Birglit, Coffee Stall, Under the
Management of Gunga Brahm.” Gunga Brahm
is the big Indian wrestler who for some time dis-
puted the championship of Australia with Buttan
Singh. The pride of the Sikhs is now shop-
walker to a pie stall.
Richard Windeyer Thompson, a descend-
ant of Sydney’s first official Windeyer, and for
several years M.L.A. for West Maitland, died
last week, aged 74. He entered Parliament on
the votes of the sporting crowd, and represented
horse and dog interests, also football and cricket,
until John Gillies came along. Gillies was even
horsier and doggier—in theory at least—than
Thompson, and the stable and the kennel rallied
round him, and the late member retired chewing
the straw of disgust.
The unopposed return of Ben Watkins,
who isn’t yet quite 22 years old, for the State
electorate of Queenstown, gives Tasmania the
youngest M.P. in the Commonwealth. The
Speck has become so used to the slumbrous
regime of senile Doddery that this infusion of
riotous young blood is expected to result in mixed
bathing, or hansom cabs, or something dreadful.
Tile Christian name .of Toorak’s newly*
elected M. L. A., Norman Bayles, is the worry of
his life. When he got into the Victorian House,
it was explained to the reporters that nothing less
than “Norman” would meet his case—no plain
N. Bayles. This moved the other Bailes of the
House— “A 1 f ” Bailes, ex-compositor, ex-jour-
nalist, ex-footballer, ex-publican—to take action;
and when next the chairman of committees
“saw” this Bendigo Bailes he cheerfully called
out “Mr. Shrapnell Bailes.” Then Bendigo felt
happy; in the matter of fancy-frill front-names
it had let the world know that even Toorak
couldn’t beat it. Of course, one man is Bailes
and the other is Bayles, and Bayles is supposed to
be as much ahead of Bailes as Baillie is of Bailey,
which latter is only one remove from Bailiff, but
all the same the similarity of sound is beastly.
“ B.” : It is worse than a misstatement to
say that Joe Cook ever did any of the hard work
of the coal miner from the time he
ceased to be a pit boy. As soon
as he reached manhood he looked
out for and collared such light
jobs as those of weigher, check-
taker, time-keeper, union secretary,
and so on. To climb on his fellow-
workers’shoulders out of the hard,
badly-paid groove of the under-
ground manual toiler was his earliest
ambition, and Joe was an agile
climber.
The House of Reps, would no
doubt be so glad to see “Holy”
Hindle. He hadn’t been many
months in the N.S.W. State Par-
liament before, preaching in a
Methodist Church at Lithgow, he
shuddered over the unsober and
immoral crowd he had to associate
with. Bluff old George Dibbs, who
loathed that sort of Pharisee, was
for throwing Holy out of the
House ; but he was persuaded
that Holy’s constituents were only
waiting to spare him the trouble.
No candidate in a N.S.W.
electorate deserves more cordial sup-
port from Australian nationalists
than Robert Howe, who has under-
taken the task of displacing that
blatant humbug Wilks from the
Dailey (N.S.W.) seat. Howe is a
sincere Radical, an advocate of the
New Protection, a trades unionist
Of distinction and a man of ideas.
His face suggests Bryan’s and
tells of the-speaking power which he possesses.
Dailey should take this opportunity to return a
man in place of its old insectarian.
The just-deceased Patrick Maguire, of
Kangaroo Valley (N.S.W.), born in 1814, the year
before Bill Adams defeated Napoleon at Waterloo,
was reputed in his own circle during his latter
clays to be the oldest Freemason on earth. It
seems improbable, however, unless the banquets
had killed the others, or they had got their feet
tangled in the regalia, and had fallen down on the
implements of their craft, and broken their
necks.
Canon Boyce’s reluctance to take the
proffered gaiters of Bendigo shows that he isn’t a
mercenary apostle. Redfern, which is the
reverend one’s pastorate, was at one time one of
the best of the Sydney city parishes. Nowadays
it is an impecunious centre, and the struggle
against poverty and dirt becomes more accen-
tuated every year. There is hunger in the col-
lection-plate, and the wolf is already half-way up
Boyce’s aisle.
HOW THE ANTI-WOMAN SUFFRAGIST
HOPES TO GET ACROSS.
I will have the great majority of the female vote. —
Tory Candidate.
“ Ho, despised one! Bear me to yonder shore!”
Maoriland speculated recently on the
probable chance of a complete new Ministry.
Influenza badly bowled over Minister of Labor
Millar, killed Defence Minister Col. Pitt, and
left the genial Timi Kara, Native Minister, only
about two ounces of vitality to go on with. At
the time when Millar was battling with the fiend,
his wife died of apoplexy.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The list of Federal candidates reveals
some curious occupations. Most remarkable is
that of Philip Fysh, who gets a fat income from
Tattersall’s sweeps, and believes in Toryism and
the Dear Land policy. Fysh calls himself a
“legislator.” O’Malley, rather more modest,
fills in his occupation as “politician.” For
Wimmera (Vic.) there is an “author” standing,
and a “newspaper proprietor and landowner,”
both Tories and supporters of the Land Famine
policy. Andy Dawson says he’s an “amalga-
mator,” and everybody who has noticed the close
amalgamation that seems to exist between him
and Jack Wren marvels at his frankness. Three
candidates are just “gentlemen,” but not one
has given his occupation frankly as “bloke.”
Yet it takes up quite as much time to be a bloke
as a gentleman—that kind of gentleman.
The charity of the philanthropic is like the
grace of God —it passeth all understanding.
Wilfrid Lawson, the British baronet,
heavy humorist and Cold Tea advocate,
passed over Jordan recently. He left
£227,114. The eldest son and heir gets
the bulk of it, the widow receives £2OOO
a year, the other sons and daughters
£lO,OOO each. Two “faithful agents”
get £lOO apiece. Each household menial
of 10 years’ service gets a legacy of £lO ;
of 15 years’ service, £l5; of 20 years’
service, £2O; and so on—£l for each
year during which they ran swiftly when
the baronet touched the bell. The tem-
perance cause got £5OO. Now Cold Tea
is taking the hat round to collect funds
to build a memorial fountain in memory
of the labors of the wealthy deceased,
just as they are passing the same worn
human lid in Sydney for a memorial to
Ebenezer Vickery.
The dreamy-looking Ramsay Mac-
donald, M.P., and his bright-faced wife,
are just now objects of worship in Mel-
bourne Labor circles. Among Victorian
Laborities there are many mere blokes
whose presence in Parliament is hard to
explain, and to them the philosophic and
rather-visionary Socialist is a mysterious
person. They aren’t quite sure whether
to accept him as a noble leader or to put
him down as a Tory in disguise. Perhaps
the rudest shock to their feelings was
when they found that Mr. and Mrs. Mac.
were actually on terms of close acquaint-
ance with an Argus proprietor and his wife, who
visited them in London. Australia hasn’t got
used to fashionable Sosh yet.
Deceased ex-Governor Hampden’s N.S.W.
career was devoid of force, character, or value.
His family name and his father’s record in the
British Commons served him as pedestal, but
even that pedestal could not maintain him in
public notice. He affected to study history, but
his mind was so porous that half-an-hour after
closing a book he forgot what it was about. And
there was no tank under his mind for his for-
gotten history to drop into, so that it might be
found again later on. It just dropped right out.
Towards the end of his time in N.S.W., he
regarded even his few official society duties as a
burden, and it is surprising that he took the
trouble to live to 65. But perhaps it was that he
couldn’t take the trouble to die.
Tom Bent, talking over early Vic. Parlia-
mentary days, told the House of a fiery, flame-
colored Scot named Ferguson who turned up at
one sitting with his hair dyed coal black, whereat
John M’lntyre, in levity, called Mr. Speaker’s
attention to a stranger. Ferguson took it badly,
and heaved the Holy Bible in among Mac’s
features. “ I was strong those days,” said
Thomas. “I got hold of him !” There Tom’s
anecdote ends abruptly, and as Ferguson does not
reappear in history we are left to assume that he
died in Bent’s grasp.
Died at Bath (England) recently, aged 90
years, Dame Cooper, relict of Charles Cooper and
daughter of Charles Burton Newenham, who
settled in S. Australia in 1837, and was Auditor-
General and Sheriff. Charles Cooper was born at
Henley-on-Thames, England, in 1795, and came to
S. Australia in 1839, with an appointment as “ Sole
Judge.” The position was named “Chief Justice”
in 1849. He held office until 1861, when he resigned
and returned to England. He died at Bath in
1887, aged 92. Cooper Creek was named after him
by Explorer Sturt.
A letter from the north :
Old Tom Ellem, a North Queensland mining pioneer of
long standing, has laid down the pick and shovel. He
was born in 1841 on the Hawkesbury River (N.S.W.),
where the tall men come from, and went north in the
early sixties. He struck poor luck at Canal Creek, so he
organised a party to ransack the Burdekin basin.
There he zig-zagged over the face of the country
for 18 months with more poor luck ; the Burdekin
only yielded tucker, and kept the billy on a feeble
boil. In ’67 he opened the Cape field, and got the
Government reward of £lOOO. Two years later he Headed
a party which struck payable stuff on the Gilbert. A
month after Mulligan had opened the Palmer, Ellem was
there amongst the gold-seekers. Where Maytown now
stands, he got payable gold, and also at Limestone.
Three years later —’7s —his party penetrated to
the Hodgkinson, and worked there with vary-
ing success, but missed the Government reward
by a head. Usually the old prospector’s luck was from
poor to moderate. Ellem afterwards took to droving,
when the freight was £5O to £6O per ton between the
Hodgkinson field and Port Douglas (70 miles). But again
his luck was moderate, for the tick decimated his teams
in ’94, and he went back Oapewards with the pick and
shovel. He had great faith in the country about the
source of the Normanby, McLeod, and Palmer rivers, but
failed to hit the spot, and was obliged to keep the pot
warm, even if it wouldn’t quite boil, by occasionally
working tin ground around Mount Spurgeon. Cancer
was the cause of his death.
“ W.” writes about a politician and a
lady :
The Bulletin (somewhere in October) said that that
bulky and bull-like Tory, Gibb, was “making use” of Mrs.
G. in his fight against Bill Lyne in the Hume electorate.
The B. mistakes. It is Mrs. G. who makes use of G.
Gibb never had any political ambitions, but his wife grew
tired of seeing his large and unintelligent bulk lying
stranded all round the house, so she ran him as a candi-
date for Flinders (Vic.). Flinders grew tired of him, and
notified (not too gently) that he had better quit. But
they didn’t notify Gibb ; they notified Mrs. Gibb, over
afternoon tea. The prospect of seeing Gibb once more
torpid on the dining-room and drawing-room sofas alarmed
his ruler, and she hade him go forth once more, hoping
to unload his dull presence on Parliament through the
medium of some other constituency. She picked Bill
Lyne as another large, good-natured man who would bolt
in terror from a small woman with an active parasol.
That’s why Gibb nominated for the Hume. Don’t
suggest any more that Gibb is making use of his
wife. I know ’em both. It’s just the lady trying
to get her uninteresting husband off the sofa.
The Hume can elect Gibb if it likes, hut
Mrs. Gibb will really represent it. They used to say
in the Reps, that Gibb telephoned to his wife for in-
structions when a division was on. That is, whenever
his intelligence could grasp the fact that a division was
coming. Gibb doesn’t want to be in Parliament ; he’d
rather go to sleep on his own verandah. But he has to
do as he is told. If he would give up his habit of going
to sleep on the verandah his wife might let him off. But
he won’t ; so she is running him for Parliament again.
HIDE THOSE RUINS!
The Anti-Sosh cry is simply dust scattered by the
Reidites to obscure the real issue. —BARBOUR, candidate
for Kooyong (Vic.).
THE DEATH’S-HEAD PARTY: “Ruins? WHAT

“F.”: Apropos ancient mariner Captain
Charles, M.L.C. (B 22/U/’O6). The venerated
old salt did not make his pile dairy-farming. A
metal was discovered on his property at Kiama
(N.S.W.) —not gold, but blue metal, the only
deposit which retorts 20cwt. to the ton ; and the
captain’s cow pasture was dug up and trodden
into the streets of Sydney.
“Priam”: Canon Mervyn Archdall, of
Sydney, was one of the jokes of the Anglican
congress. The enthusiastic old gentleman spoke
on every theme that was brought forward, and
did it in a high, wild Irish voice that swept every-
thing before it. It was a shock to locate the
Voice and find that it wasn’t waving a shillelah
after all, and that it hadn’t a caubeen on its head.
Two members of the first Parliament of
Victoria under Responsible Government (opened
21st Nov., 1856) were present at the jubilee on
Wednesday—Charles Read, now aged 92 years,
and P. Finn. John Dennistoun Wood found
that he was too old to struggle over from
Tasmania and join the function. Charles Read
has j ust been pensioned off with £7B a year.

Dymock’s Circulating Library.
The question of a suitable Christmas present now
engages our mind. What more lasting pleasure and
what gift more appreciated than a year’s reading of the
latest and best literature ? We can supply it; our books
are all clean; they are covered in a neat green cloth;
every writer and author of note from the English and
Am erican publishers are represented by their new works in
quantities sufficient to enable every subscriber to get the
book they ask for, immediately upon application. Our
terms are reasonable, for £2 2s. per annum or 255. for six
months a resident in the city or suburbs may change two
volumes and two magazines as often as they wish ; for
21s. per annum or 12s. 6d. for six months one book and
one magazine are allowed. To residents in the country
we send a parcel of eight volumes at a time for £2 12s. 6d.
per annum or £1 Us. 6d. for six months. Special low
parcel rates have been arranged for on the railways.
Write or call for our catalogues with full particulars.
Dymock’s Book Arcade and Circulating Library, 428
George-street, Sydney.*

A WOM AN’S LETTER .
Sydney, November 27, 1906.
My Dear Arini.—
Several charming persons are leaving Australia
for visits or permanently. There should be a
law against charming persons leaving here
except for the purpose of having their charm
freshened up, so to speak. Charming per-
sons should be out on bail, and should not be
allowed to leave, except on giving solid security
for their early return, and extra-charming persons
should be paid to remain here permanently. Herr
and Madame Degen leave by the German liner on
December 8, and on the same date by the Moldavia
go the W. P. Mannings, to the extent of papa,
mama, and little daughter Trixie, who will “come
out ”on the other side. A Manning son, Fred., is
in England, and has lately done some critical
reviewing in dizzily high-class London weeklies—
the sort that have a ladder sold with them, so that
the reader can climb up to their mental altitude.
There was one that had a balloon sold with it,
but so many readers fell out and broke their
minds that it ceased publication.
By the Himalaya that left yesterday went Mrs.
Burdekin and two of her daughters. Also, a Lady
Tennant whose husband is said to have been
something in the beer line. When she sheds her
weeds (black doesn’t “go” with a rather swarthy
complexion and raven black hair) Lady Tennant
will look what she is—a handsome woman. She’s
in her prime, and has a most attractive manner
and voice. She did the Blue Mountains, stayed
at the Carrington at Katoomba, and at the
Medlow Hydro, and explored the Caves. Also,
she did not refer in public to the housekeeper at
either hotel as “the housekeeper person” or
“ that woman.” This achievement in ill-breeding
was reached by a Sydney woman with her hair
done pretty-cocky fashion.
But the most regretted of Saturday’s departers
is Captain Leslie Wilson, who has been A.D.C.
to his Excellency Rawson. He, however, says he
will come back in March, which bone of comfort
alone stopped a lot of Sydney women from swim-
ming after the vessel as far as the sharks would let
them. Curiously enough, in spite of his Adonis-
like outfit and the impressionable feminines, he
was never even alleged to be engaged to Cori-
sande This or Nancy That. All the other
unmarried Yice-regals are always being misfitted
with a white man’s burden. There must be some-
thing of the sea serpent in Captain Wilson ; he was
wise and slippery, and knew exactly when to
dive and how long to remain below. The Teeces, of
the A.M.P., have come back from a European
time. A daughter is to be married to one of the
Littlejohns presently. The youngest Teece son
is down with appendicitis.
Miss Lamb O’Neill, the fair and globular
Sydneysider, sister of a local doctor, is reported
all well from London and doing a round of country
house visiting. She was one of the most cultured
of the R.C. section here, and was haloed by the
memory of an interview with the late Pope Leo.
Mrs. Jack Want and her daughter stay at the
Australia for the space between the auctioning
of their household furniture and their departure
for England. • -
Miss Ruby Lane-Browne—the second girl of a
group of large-eyed Strathfield-line sisters, whose
frocking and hatting were always first past the
fashion post—is back in Sydney for a few months.
She and a sister have acquired the flat habit in
London. This might be called the elevated, or
upstairs, horizontal disease. Another sister who
married, here, a man-o’-war doctor named Pearce,
lives happily at Malta, where there’s a naval
garrison, lovely lace, and small dogs. Also
Malta is where St. Paul fried the snake. The
returned wanderer’s sulphur-colored hat, big
bunch of brimstone ribbon, brimstone straw,
pink roses, and brimstone feathers—suggestive of
wearing Gehenna on the head—are what They
were wearing when she left the fashion centres.
Two ancient Australian names were joined in
wedlock last week at St. John’s, Milson’s Point,
by Rev. H. C. Vindin, when Yera, youngest
daughter of G. G. Blaxland, of “ Kanimbla,”
Fitzroy-street, Milson’s Point, was wedded to
Mr. Herbert Charles Rouse, second son of the
late Richard Rouse, of “ Biraganbil,” Mudgee.
The families of Rouse and Blaxland date back a
century in Australia, and, like other families of
their time, they were largely engaged in horn and
hide. In the race for wealth, it is difficult to say
whose cow ran first, but there are many old
families which claim to be a good second. One
hundred years ago John Blaxland, who had
frontages on both sides of George-street, near
Market-street, finding that he could not sell live
stock, opened a dairy and butcher’s shop, and did
a “cutting trade,” much in the interests of the
poor of the time.
Someone writes (too many persons have acquired
the vice of writing) to point out (too many persons
are at heart semaphores and finger-posts) that it
is solicitor W. H. Drew who is to be married to
the step-daughter of Mr. Stitt, of the innocent
facial expression, not Mr. Charles Drew, who is
also a Gentleman One, but already provided with
a charming wife. Don’t know what W.H. stands
for, but if W. is for William, the initial-owner
must expect to hear, by-and-bye, whenever he
loses his temper, “Billy, you are boiling over.”
Anyhow, Drew is a good name for a solicitor and
suggests acquisition. And after all this I hope the
Drews, Charles and William, will both stand by me
with cheap law when I am run in for “lifting”
calves and lambs from travelling stock for the
land lam acquiring out back. It’s the only way
I’ll have of starting herds.
Another interesting engagement. Mrs. Andrew
Seton Chisholm, of Goulburn, who, in her prime,
was said to be the most beautiful woman in
Sydney, is to marry Mr. Waller, one of the
Commissioners of the Harbor Trust. He is the
step-father of that Dolly Yarden beauty, Mrs.
Scot-Skirving. Also, he has a daughter of the
fascinating name of Eastermorn, who is by
way of being a violiniste. One of Mrs. Chisholm’s
daughters is Mrs. Norman Alston of musicianly
fame.
Who is Mr. Harry Leader, of Sydney? The
British Australasian says he is a young Aus-
tralian writer of considerable attainments, living
in London, and that through illness his position
has become precarious, and unless he can voyage
to Australia soon his doctor says he will die. Mr.
Frank Gerald and Mr. Arthur Greenaway are
asking for subs, for the passage-money of the
disabled youth.
Same paper publishes an interview with, and a
portrait of, May Beatty. May’s fair face wears
the most cave circem dolosque (Maori for “ beware
the enchantress and her wiles”) expression
that ever knocked a man flat. By the interview,
May was born at Christchurch (M.L.), and
has faithfully adhered to two most important
traditions of the comic opera artist. “ She had
her jewellery stolen, and she was reared in a con-
vent.” Isn’t a third tradition necessary—that of
being a clergyman’s daughter ? Recall the chorus
of laughter that went up (in the book, “John
Storm ”) when Glory Qurvyle told the other
friskers that she was a parson’s daughter. “Same
old gag.” Miss Beatty says the London crowds
surprise her by reason of their lack of objective
and destination. People in Melbourne and
Sydney walk along smartly. Not one of all the
millions in London seemed to be in a hurry.
One day May was late, and she “raced along,
and the people stood still to gaze at someone
actually hurrying.” A back view of a racing
woman isn’t on any occasion like a daffodil
dancing—but a back view of the opulent-
figured May doing a sprint would give even a
George-street crowd pause in its alleged haste,
which is that of an express caterpillar. And May
is going into boy’s clothes for the Xmas panto, in
Dublin.
That hustling, plucky young party, Ethel
Marcus Clarke (her father wrote “ His Natural
Life ”), who was with the Bland Holt Co. for some
time, writes from California, where she is on tour
with a good road company. Ethel says she is
getting along “just fine and dandy,” but she
had a terrible time during the fire that followed
the earthquake. All her wardrobe became a
cinder. “ But dear, good Mr. Bland Holt sent
me a lot of nice frocks, and I was enabled to
take to the road in style again.” At time of
writing the co. which grey-eyed Ethel adorns
was wrestling with the famous “Monte
Christo” and doing huge business. Ethel —
who has taken her mother’s maiden name for
the stage—plays an old hag, and doubles
the part with that of the young soldier boy,
Count de Morcerf. Mighty versatile work this
playing of a crone and a gallant.
An Orange girl (sounds as if something about
Sweet Neil were coming) tells me that Orange
lost its coolness on the arrival of , Amy Sherwin,
so warm was its welcome of the soprano. The
E. B. Daltons—Mrs. E. B. has a pretty voice of
her own—rained roses over Amy, and Mrs. Simon
Kearney, whose play, long since accepted by Mr.
J.C.W., we are still waiting for, came along with
more roses and with a musical reminiscence of
Madame Sherwin’s debut —a pianoforte score of
“ Lucia.”
Wonderland (Bondi) opens next Saturday even-
ing. Premier Carruthers does the opening with a
speech and a corkscrew. Society and officialdom
have responded to their cards of invitation with a
unanimous Yes. Mrs. Anderson (Miss Eugenie
Duggan) and her little daughter are over from
Melbourne for the Event, and are staying at
Adams’ Hotel.
Miss Birdie Bruce, a pupil of Mrs. Quesnel, has
been honored by a request to proceed to Tasmania
to sing the soprano part in the “Messiah” pro-
duction. Andrew Black will do the Grand Roarer
role in the oratorio.
S.M. Herald, the other day, in its “ Page for
Women,” copied from a mildly abject British
magazine the statement that there are more
female applicants for places at court than there
are places to be filled. Yet here are the quali-
fications :
Two qualifications are an absolute necessity. The first
is the power to make one’s self agreeable at all times and
seasons. The second is the physical capacity to stand for
several hours consecutively.
And the delights of the life :
A lady-in-waiting must have no opiniops or moods
whilst on duty. If the Royal personage wishes to sit up
till the early hours, no fatigue must be shown. An atten-
dant on royalty must only be tired or sleepy, hungry or
thirsty, when her mistress is in a like condition.
But the mildly abject magazine forgets to mention
what happens to the lady whose legs give way
beneath her in the fourth hour of her consecutive
perpendicularness. Presumably she is dragged
out in disgrace, the hall being wiped with her
prostrate form as she is removed, and when she
has sufficiently recovered to tender a sincere
apology, her boxes are put out in the road in
front of Windsor Castle, or the Tower of London,
or wherever the place may be, and she waits till
she can bribe some passing carter to take her away
somewhere out of the eye of the world. On the
whole, it seems as if the favored royal attendant
might almost as well have been a Miss Sambo, of
the IX.S. slavery days. Miss Sambo’s mistress
also made her stand up a good deal. Sometimes
she made her sit up.
Young Herbert Casey, the bank clerk who died
suddenly at a dance at Wellington (Maorilana),
was a son of Mr. Walter Casey, of Manly.
News comes from England of the death (at
Chatham) of the young wife of Lieutenant Harold
Bedwell, R.N. She was a Sydney girl, formerly
Miss Frances Lyons, and married only a few years
ago. Another much-loved personality, Mrs.
Alf. Phillips, died last week. She was Miss Julia
Nathan, of Auckland, and was still in the early
twenties.
The Mother Rectress of St. Vincent’s Hospital
has sent out invitations for Friday, the 30th inst.,
when Mayoress Allen Taylor will present the
hospital ball cheque.
Canadian Commissioner Larke, who does some
sky-pilotage in his spare time, put a dreadful and
ill-chosen foot in it at the Town Hall farewell to
Mr. Stitt, the pleasant magnate of the Canadian.
Pacific railway. The Unpleasant Gentleman,
trying to say something nice about the Pleasant.
Gentleman, stated that he always took his
troubles and worries to Mr. Stitt, and in-
variably came away much relieved. When.
Mr. Stitt was gone, he said airily, the Canadian
colony here would be mere residuum, or words to
that effect. “Residuum” sounds so much like
plain “dregs” that the other Canadians present
had to hold themselves down on their chairs. If
they had let go even for a moment the chances,
are that the Stitt testimonial would have been
trodden underfoot in the tumult that would have
ensued.
An Australienne at present in London writes
to tell me that the British Australasian
readers are agitating for the starting of a fund for
stranded Australians. She suggests that Austra-
lians with the “strand” habit be kept at home, and.
so save the starting of the circulating hat. Perhaps,
we shall yet see this kind of advertisement notice:
“Friends of Miss Lute, the promising Sydney
soprano, are requested to meet at the Hotel Aus-
tralia next Wednesday at four o’clock. Business:
The formation of a fund to enable Miss Lute to
remain in Australia.” My correspondent goes on
to say that “ three singer girls recently arrived in
London who averaged £lOO or £l5O each, on which
to live and study until they could make a paying
appearance. And one of the warblers started the
‘ strand ’ act by paying £5O on her concert and
£lO for a dress. The musical world is hideously
overcrowded 1 And when the fact that she is a
failure is brought home to the music-maker she
rushes into journalism, with dire results.”
“Journalism,” as rushed into by a “failure”
singer, is most likely of the form that tells of the
arrivals and departures and frocking of the Lucas
Tooths, the Rupert Clarkes, and such large deer.
And as this kind of work is as easy in a mental
sense as being asleep in a comfortable bed,
naturally the market for it is also hideously over-
crowded. Physically it must be more wearing
than domestic work, for the society woman’s
reporter has to be fossicking around at all hours
of the day and night. A Queensland woman—
have no clue to her identity—has written in an
English paper a significant and logical warning to
these rushers into reporting. Their education is
defective, their reading of no account. They
have nothing fresh to say, and they say the old,
unfresh thing in the old stereotyped fashion. A
London editor often gets 40 reports of the one
society function —he can choose but one from the
batch. The secretary of a great London hospital
told the writer (of the article) that girls and
women in the freelance scribbling line come
to him by the score, hoping for a chance
par, and the kind man always has tea and
piles of sandwiches ready for them ; for, he said,
“they are always hungry and always tired.”
The Queenslander entreats this kind of freelance
to turn her attention in preference to the more
honorable and respected occupation of domestic
service. The Mary Ann is at least a person who
is in demand, and she need never, unless she is a
wholly execrable Mary Ann, find her life embit-
tered for lack of a sandwich.
It gives one a pain under one’s Swiss belt to
think of an Australienne having to be glad to eat
that kind Sec.’s sandwiches! Failure here in
Australia, either as a singer, or as a pursuer of
personal pars., or a writer of things beginning
“Among those present,” “The ballroom pre-
sented a brilliant appearance,” and “ A smart and
pretty wedding, ” must be so different to failure at
these things in the Great Blot.
A Sydney man dressed in grey clothes, with a
vest and shirt of the same shade as the green
fumed oak (fashionable furniture), and a tangerine
tie, is said to have quite a hair-trigger touch of
style.
The Maori shares with the Scot a natural
ability to play “goff.” In the first place, a few
wealthy Hawkes Bay natives, attracted by the
gaudy clothes and the beautifully-colored stock-
ings of the male “goffer,” decided that the labor
of smiting the gutta-percha ball was a small price
to pay for the privilege of wearing the festive
rig-out. Presently latent talent came to light,
and every Maori in the sheep-farming province
took to the fearsome game with vigor—the Maori
is ever vigorous in the cause of “ sport,” although
he abhors hard work. Finally, Kurupo Tareha,
a young rangitira, won the championship of
Maoriland, and the tangi which celebrated the
event lasted for months —it is reported to be still
lasting. At first sassiety was a bit surprised at
the intrusion of the aborigine, but the good-
natured Henare Hapuka said that he had no
particular objection to playing with the sassiety
herd, and sass collapsed in a heap on the turf.
Mixed foursomes are played between Mr. Whare
te Moana and Miss Millicent de Courcy, also Mr.
Godfrey Shorteash and Mrs. Maggie Ruatamata,
and after the game Mr. Whare te Moana drives
the party off to lunch in his yellow and red motor
car. There’s nothing bigoted about Mr. Hapuka.
On Cup Day in Sydney one bookmaker drove
a thriving business in a tea-room run by an
austere firm of irreproachable respectability. He
circularised his numerous clientele some days
before to the effect that he was to be found in
the Hard-Shell Baptist rooms, Pitt-street, and
would be glad to discuss business with any of
them over a friendly cup of the brown fluid. The
friends called round, and the bookmaker called
for tea for each friend and himself, and the room
did a roaring business. The cashier became sus-
picious, and counted the tea, and it was found
that between 10.30 a.m. and 5 p.m. the book-
maker ordered 148 cups. Now, he ordered tea
for two each time, yet there was no tea on the
floor, so apparently the bookie drank 74 cups.
About the 73rd cup, the proprietor was rung up
and informed of what was going on, and about
5 p.m. he arrived in a state of fury, and foolishly
cast a profitable customer forth with great indig-
nation. Such a man doesn’t deserve to prosper.
“ Rosna ” writes from Adelaide :
The death by burning of Mr. Edwin Crozier was a great
shock to South Australian squatting and sporting folk.
The deceased was one of a well-known pastoralist family,
which squats all over the State, more or less, and he was
generally popular. The accident that caused his death is
another illustration of the folly of reading in bed by
candle light.
• Bach Society, the champion noise-making club
in the steepled city, achieved a concert last week at the
Town Hall, and collected a large audience, as usual. The
Bach shows are always crowded shows, and are always
worth the pawning, in moderation, of the family plate.
The society is manipulated by Dr. Harold Davies,
who caught the first Mus. Doc. degree in Australia,
and has a considerable reputation for driving
choirs and steering melody round difficult corners.
Pianiste Elsie Hamilton and violiniste Nora Kyffin
Thomas, two girleens of foreign study, provided instru-
mental interjections, and cut a large slice off the loaf of
acclamation. All the musical folk of Adelaide put on
their worst clothes and went along. You put on your
bad clothes for a concert to make people think you were
so dazed with the joyful prospect of hearing such melody
as only a Great Soul can appreciate, that you didn’t
know what you were putting on.
The postal authorities, evidently having some super-
fluous leisure on their hands, amused themselves last
week by counting all the letters that left Adelaide for the
Ma Country by the Christmas mail; and discovered that
said Ma Country had increased in popularity since last
year to the extent of some 4000 letters. No doubt the
extra 4000 were written to the people who have been
driven away from here by the machinations of the Labah
Pahty.
Some excitement was caused at the Anglican Congress
in Melbourne, t’other day, by the reading of a paper on
the necessity for auricular confession, by a ritualistic Ade-
laide parson. Said parson, who is young, eloquent and
curly-haired, has been much worried by the attention*
of the allegstocracy, and although he shepherds
a somewhat poor and undistinguished suburb, the
Very Best People dash out to hear him every Sunday.
His ritualistic practices provide endless newspaper corres-
pondence, and endless romances. One she is said to have
marched up to the rector after the service, shaken him
enthusiastically by the hand, and remarked, “ Thank you
so much. I’ve enjoyed myself immensely. I shall be
delighted to hear you perform at any time.”
I think myself that auricular confession, when the par-
son is young and romantic-looking, would be a great
success, provided the penitent was also young, and, in
addition, feminine and romantic. She ought to kneel
before the object of her adoration in ordinary cases of sin.
In very bad cases she might lie flat on her face in an atti-
tude of picturesque abasement, which would draw atten-
tion to the abundance of her back hair. Then, when she
had confessed, and it proved that she had done nothing
very bad, and was merely a tender soul with abundant
back hair who took an unduly harsh view of herself, the
eloquent preacher would necessarily raise her up, and
call her “sister” or “daughter,”and speak to her like a
Dutch uncle—l mean like a father, with a mixture of a
brother and an apostle. In such matters I should say a
great deal depends on attitude.
Dolly Castles stayed on in Adelaide with Williamson’s
company, although she had no part in either “Utopia
Limited ’’or “ Princess Ida.” At the first night of the
latter, Dolly dimpled upon her chums from the audience,
looking a smaller and fairer edition of her sister Amy,
whom she joins in giddy London next year. In
“ Princess Ida,” Cyril was played by Henry Bracy, that
old Adelaide favorite. On the closing night of the
Williamson season, Dolly Castles warbled “ Poor Wander-
ing One” to some enthusiastic acclamation. The best
tones in the company, however, undoubtedly belong to
Olive Godwin, who played lead in “Utopia” and
“ Princess Ida.”
There is a movement on foot to organise a complimentary
concert, at the Semaphore, for the Doubleday girleen as
she passes through this part of the U'orld on" her way to
Germany next month. The German boat, by which little
Leila goes Parnassus-climbing, stays at the Semaphore
anchorage two days, and the show is fixed for Decem-
ber 14th.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 27, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Another infant junket is announced from the
Federal Vice-Regal lodge, and all the cherubs of
account are being put into curl and rehearsal for
the show. Every girl-child will have its knee-
joint trained to double up before a dummy, until
it is proficient in the curtsey. Last time Dame
Northcote gave a party, she had a poodle
and several caddies at heel, and the kids made
rather a burlesque of the ceremony. Some fled
past the dais in affright, others pulled up and
curiously viewed the doggie with its staid parlor
manners, others wept copiously with stage terror
at the moment when they should have bobbed.
Davne Talbot raised her gentle voice in speech,
the other day, on behalf of the proposed Exhi-
bition of Women’s Work. Lady Northcote
initiated the movement, and the “Stonington”
Grace supported the idea by a Town Hall cor-
roboree and a speech. She is herself a stitcher of
poetic designs, and she urged femininity to devote
itself to the needle and thimble cult, for the pur-
pose of making a great display.
The tired Talbottle has been moving round the
gum-tree area a bit, in a spasm of endeavor to do
or say something before adjourning for the bush-
fire season to the cottage at Macedon. He has
journeyed about and conversed with the hewer of
wood and the crop-grower. On one or two occa-
sions he has unexpectedly called at a settler’s
thatched cottage or a grazier’s snuggery, and sent
the womenfolk into tremors of anxiety and throbs
of joy by the visit. The question of what to do
and how to do it vexes the she-soul cruelly on such
occasions. The book of etiquette isn’t at hand,
and whether she should sit i n the presence of the
Exalted, or stand, or retire with her family to the
cheese vat till the V.R. magnificence had passed
from the land again, is a problem of moment.
A small squatting dame lets me into the secret
of her “terrible” embarrassment when the
Talbottle and bodyguard landed at her eyrie.
“Jack didn’t care now they came, or when, or
anything else. He couldn’t be made to care.
He’s a trying man sometimes. But I hustled the
babies into the nursery, and then felt like burying
myself in the garden well till the hour had
passed. Don’t think me a silly fool. I didn’t
want to do the wrong thing or look stupid. Tea
was prepared. Jack took some in the laziest,
most nonchalant fashion in the world, and left me
to worry the Governor along. The two aides
evidently mustn’t sit down without a written and
signed order or something. Also, they mustn’t
talk. So they stood looking out of the window
till their sphinxy attitude struck Jack, and he
blurted out would they have a whisky. You
should have seen their stained-glass window
expression. They declined, while the Governor
looked over his tea cup at his boot-cap with a
terrible air of dignity. Jack was a pig. After-
wards I cried. Temper and hysteria and Governor
were the causes. Next time I’ll ask them to have
beer. See if I don’t.”
On another occasion the “ Stonington ” pro-
cession, winding its way into the eucalyptus land,
arrested itself at “ Springbank,” the residence of
that veteran settler, Robert Laidlaw. On arriving
at the Heidelberg house of the hills, the owner,
who is now minuetting through his ninety-third
summer, was gathering a festive rose for his
buttonhole, and greeted the Ex. and party with
all the dignity and easy calm of his years.' The
cheerful ancient can talk Victoria better than
astute landowners half his age, and no doubt he
interested Talbottle no end.
A. knotty question is being discussed by the
Very Best People as to whether a hostess should
employ a pair of eye-panes on a fantastic prop in
inspecting her own cronies. The G.-G. dame has
her own particular style of perching herself on the
vice-regal dais and surveying the Bidden through
her lorgnette. It is suffered by the victims with
a certain degree of philosophy that deadens the
pain of the indignity. They reckon it better to
be bidden to the Government House quarters and
regarded with the interrogatory stare or magni-
fying orb of a curious and interested Ex. than be
left to chew the cud of neglected humbleness
at home. But the present grief is that an ordi-
nary Toorak hostess, who—according to the voice
of Blander, uttered through the false teeth of
Aspersion grew prosperous on the sale of
washing blue or something in the laundry line,
should have the audacity to bring a pair of inspec-
tion glasses, erected on a jewelled prop (another
laundry emblem), and review her guests with these,
assuming a haughty goose-neck the while. Only
that the lorgnette was chained to the hostess’s
person on the last occasion of great pique, some
dear crony would have grabbed it and thrown it
all the way to Collingwood.
As a wind-up to the tea and muffin rallies that
made Menzies’ a pleasant port of call for many
faded and gone Tuesdays, a big charity show is
billed for the approaching Saturday by Napoleon
Boffard and Co. The proceeds go to fatten the
very thin funds for the Memorial to Australian
Bills (Tommies are not our type), who spilt their
life’s gore for Mother Britain in South Africa.
There’s a stupendous programme to justify the
high-priced tickets, and the Very Best People are
likely to crowd along in the Cup frills that badly
want all the airing they can get before the brief
span of summer passes.
The difficult bachelor gets better catered
for every day. A hostess now pays him the
compliment of submitting the list of invited for
his edification. If it falls short of his ideal, and
the bachelor objects to some of the bidden, the
crushed and tottering hostess has actually to lose
his company. Otherwise she has to shunt the
undesirables, or arrange things so that the eligible
is never, during all the picnic hours, constrained
to be within sight of the unfortunate guests who
have earned his disfavor. Think of the mental
calisthenics of that hostess 1
It’s marvellous how the fashionable grieves
about tbe spelling of his or her name. One of the
most important sports hereabouts frets and fumes
when a redundant “ e ” is carelessly added to his
cognomen, and a lively Tooraketty Miss almost
weeps when the absent-minded write her down
“Edith” instead of “Edyth.”
The chicks of the Solomons of the Bench are
inveterate songsters. The Maddens were the first
to brand the nigger plantation lullaby a proper
parlor lilt. The Cream followed, in large spon-
taneous clots, as it were. Now the brainy Miss
D. Chomley practises scales and soars to song in
the track of a local Signorita. Mrs. Vallange and
her young, unattached sister are preparing to
platform it for charity in song. The pair have
skated and danced no end for the deserving sick
and poor. Now they warble to help some dis-
tressed fund or other, and when they butcher
themselves for a public charity who can cavil?
The Justice Isaacs folk has said goodbye to
flat life for a while. When they return from
Sydney, the family of the new Solomon will drift
up to Macedon, there to idle with bush-fire raid
Bridge diversions through the summer.
The new Toorak representative in the House of
Babel and Bent has settled down into his collar.
In the House Mr. Norman Bayles already looks
like an old, staid Parliamentary battler, and his
stylish “haw” is fading away like his spring
buttonhole of blue blooms. Few members say
“ Haw, Mistah-ah-Speakah ” after their first
session.
The other day Mr. George Fairbairn, the
squatter-member of great altitude and cash
tonnage, had to declare a Pincushion and Bran-
Pie Fair open for a host of females. To see that
M.L.A. unfold his lengthy limbs and stretch to
his top inch for the opening utterance of the
Penny Dip fete was a ludicrous matter. What
does the Penny Dip need a curtain-lifting speech
or benediction for, anyhow ? Does the Bran-Pie
attract an extra copper on account of the poli-
tician’s laying on of gas ?
Poodle parades are the latest hobby of the
charity hostess. The dame who lets the multitude
in on her lawn to buy socks in the cause of a
creche, or pickles for the good of the Immigrants’
Home, now evolves the idea of charging admission
to a dog show as a means of fattening the funds
of some good cause. Such a Poodle Fete and
Parade is now in course of preparation.
The Rymills went back to the Holy City after
the Cup junkets. What the young dame thought
of the whirligig—regarded with the eye of newly-
married dignity—after enjoying several gee-gee
seasons as Miss Shylie Blue, the S. Australian star
flutterer, it would be interesting to learn. There’s
such a mighty gap between frisking it with a
swarm of adorers through the Cup whirligig and
floating quietly through with a permanent mate.
The Carnival Hop, being arranged by Mrs. W
Green and her nymph-like daughter, is on a
colossal scale. Several hundred small fry are to
amble, hop, glide, twist, evolute and whirl
through a bill comprising all the fancy pirouettes
and new measures of 1906. The Town Hall will
be the scene, and the date will be December 10.
It is the grand season of Kids. Grown-ups
retire with a blast air from the show-case and the
front social windows. The knickerbocker and
short-coated fry take all the room that’s to spare.
Last week a flock of lovely youngsters took the
floor at the Masonic Hall, and went through an
evening’s pirouette without any squabbles or
casualties. Tommy danced with Toddles like
any full-sized dandy, and Miss Jeannie Brenan—
the presiding Terpsichore—fluttered over the
scene like a large guardian butterfly in chiffon
wings of delicate coloring. It was a delightful
function.
Postmaster Outtrim quits the service and takes
a restful jaunt abroad. He proposes a leisurely
journey with his life-mate and melodious son, who
leaves the comic opera ranks for that purpose.
This son, some time ago, relinquished the surplice
and left off pedalling the church organ to foot it
in comic opera. The struttings and warblings of
two years spent with the giddy mummers have
failed to destroy his angel look. He must surely
one day drift into curacy and shovel hats.
A young man of msthetic tastes and a lingering
love of the Gynthias of a Keats world writes to
Tryphena in horror of the summer cricket girl.
“ She plays on a green within view of my window
at South Yarra. She is a shocking young person.
Ido not know which is worse—to see her standing
spread, keeping wicket, windmill fashion, or
climbing into the air, all arms and legs, to catch
a ball. She is not pleasant fielding even, but
sometimes she can run and not remind one of an
elephant waltzing. It is, however, a trying- sight
at best. At hockey she’s hoydenish and rough.
At cricket she is unpleasantly vulgar.” And he
winds up with a prayer to stay the gentle maiden
from wicket play.
The local needle-dodgers get another piece of
ostrich tail in their collective cap. One of the
most knowing beauties in this quarter has put
her trousseau—a dozen and a half of everything,
with a Sabbath-day’s journey of bebe ribbon on
each—into the hands of a local stitcher. This,
too, on returning from the world’s centre, where
the engagement was made, and where, had the
bride-to-be so desired, the wedding garment might
easily have been ordered. “Melbourne wants
some licking,” this gentle Toorakite murmured in
referring to the matter.
A shockingly tired son of a Victorian wool-
gatherer was roused from slumber the other night
by the servants’ alarm of burglars. “Go away,”
droned Adolphus. “Go away. Clap your bands,
all of you, and get the bad men to clear out.
Beastly nuisance of you, breaking into one’s
sleep.” Accordingly, tbe assorted staff went back
to bed in disgust. Adolphus is an Eton product.
The pallid countenance and the shovel
hat of the curate takes all the room in the
trams and bun-coops of this city. Wesleyans,
Baptists, and the more fanciful religionists are
swarming over the city. Conferences of the sky-
pilots and the annual growl of the sin-sbifters are
the cause. One day the shovel hat disappeared
from view, and no longer blotted out the horizon
with its inky numerousness. It had assembled en
masse at Bishopscourt for a lawn rally, and the
city had room for a little giddiness again. At
that corroboree the State and Federal Exes, were
divided in the Scriptural fashion of tbe sbeep and
the goats, and were provided with tea and wafer
in the Bishop’s parlor, while the crowd browsed
on buns in the outer tents of plenty. Looking
round at a Stonington collection of the gaitered
and shovel-lidded last week, when the Talbottles
hosted the godly, I ruminated on the matter of
pallid and unhappy sky-pilots. In the Christian
interest oughtn’t reverends to look cheerfuler?
Who wants to follow the dyspeptic and gloomy
cleric to a mansion in the sky, if the prospect of
the mansion makps like that ?
The De la Pbe'Y'BaSfefdrds— brother and sister
—submit a bill of warble to the interested this
week. The he-Beresford has made a hit with the
aristocracy that gather round when he raises his
voice in song. A he-warbler has only to choose
ihis tailor discreetly and charge his glance with an
■electric soul-current to move society to earnest
■enthusiasm and the purchase of more concert
tickets.
A feature of “Dr. Wake’s Patient” on Satur-
day night was the amount of floral offering that
went up to the Patient and her stage relatives
from the Princess’ audience. Miss Beatrice Day
.and Mrs. Brough hauled in rafts of roses, and tall
masts garlanded with carnations, and woodbine,
and other fragrant trailers, while Miss Emma
Temple also gathered many fair, green tokens.
The three comediennes won the tributes from
women friends. Yet the woman-friend’s devotion
to her own sex seldom runs to any expense.
A he-admirer of Melba forwards me word of
the lady’s re-appearance in Italian opera at
Oovent Garden. He describes her “ Rigoletto ”
performances as a sublime treat, and says “Bats! ”
likewise “Bunkum!” to the report as to the
deterioration of Melba’s top C. “ Honestly and
impartially,” he writes, “she was never better in
all her career. There wasn’t the slightest flaw in
any number. Every note was as perfect as her
incomparable voice and great art could make it.
She’s cold, I grant you, but I want no better
thrill from any singer on God’s earth.”
Manager George Tallis dreads the cherry season
for his Royal Comic Operatics. Already Mr. R.
Roberts is laid low, with the usual appendicitis,
as a result, ’tis alleged, of nibbling the stoned
fruit. The co. has been warned not to gaze on the
cherry when it is red, and the host who spreads it
before any of the ladies of the show is to be re-
garded as a bitter enemy of gaiety, and a Judkins
in intention.
This is from a base man: —
One hears too much about the vice of great cities,, and
not enough reference to the shocking immorality of
small communities in the healthy country districts.
Attacks on female character seem to have been habitually
misdirected since the early dawn of polite literature. It
has always been the giddy lady of fashion who was
exhorted to mould her morals in emulation of the vir-
tuous rural housewife, as though human life were purified
in pettiness, and lovely woman acquired fidelity and art-
lessness from her ignorance of the frivolities of civilisa-
tion. Two eye-opening stories of matronly virtue in
out-of-the-way districts have come from Hungary within
the past few years. The latest narrative alleges that
a number of weary husbands as well as tired wives have
been poisoning their domestic partners at the instance of
two women, who supplied the deadly drug. Probably
the men in most cases wanted to be free from the nuptial
tie, but it goes without saying that each lady poisoner
had a further husband in view. Anyhow, the practitioners
in the previous story were all married women—scores of
thtm—-and the purpose in every case was to clear the
way for another venture in connubial bliss. The male
sex collectively can get a glow of conscious pride from
the thought that a lot of married women will
do anything to get a second, third, or fourth
husband, or more ; but the individual man who dwells
amidst the wickedness of a populous city feels grateful to
Providence for being preserved from the temptations
of the Simple Life. The modern provincial white woman
is fairly represented, one supposes, in the small towns
and villages of Hungary, which is a light-hearted country
where the picturesque dances come from. Now, it seems
that when a Hungarian matron sits squeezing the cow
with the crumpled horn she is apt to be wondering how
many grains of strychnine she can safely administer to
her husband in a pint of milk. Considering that the
modern woman is a faithful study from the antique as
regards her poisoning proclivities, it speaks volumes for
the chivalry of man that through all ages he has gone
writing odes to his best girl.
A Melbourne landlady gave a new boarder (an
art student) notice to quit because she observed
on one of his bookshelves Wake Cook’s work on
“ Anarchism in Art and Chaos in Criticism.”
The high and mighty hash-princess remarked
that her ’ouse had always been a decent one,
thank ’Eaven, and she wasn’t agoin’ to ’ave no
anarchists ’angin’ round, not ’er.
The ideal picnicker is a peculiar type. She is
energetic, cheerful, accommodating. If her lot
is cast with a he-companion, and that person is
(say) a botanist, she’ll harken to his leafy enthusi-
asms and be content with the few straw-like re-
marks she casts into the current of his floral talk.
If he is flirtatious she’s not unwilling, but earns
her thrill with a mild sensation of joy. She isn’t
ardent, she isn’t dull, but just the happy common-
place medium, and to collect as many as possible
of her type round the tea-billy and the sand-
wiches is the grand hot-season ambition of her
life. Of course the pair who, without warning,
find themselves to be twin souls, and who have to
be whistled up from a mossy dell where they
have been swopping dreams and squeezing hands,
presently give picnics best. They find it best to
al fresco on their own.
From Brisbane:—
The committee of the Local Children’s Hospital is in
the agonies of a big river-side Fete, to take place on
Saturday next. The proceeds are to be devoted to the
building of the much-needed diphtheria ward. (During
the last outbreak of that disease, cases that had just
come off the operating table, where tracheotomy had
been performed, were often to be seen being tended on
mattresses on the floor—every bed being full). The com-
mittee is run by the portly editress of the Courier.
This astute dame believes in the drawing power of the
military, and has enlisted all the miscellaneous Napoleons
and Caesars that were hanging round. A full-blown major
is to run the Aunt Sally, and the absent-minded Carroll will
handicap for the billy-goat races, and there is some idea
of obtaining the services of the subs as waiters in the tea
room. Unfortunately for the harmony of the situation
almost every committee lady wants a Napoleon to herself,
a id there isn’t enough of the hero of Austerlitz to go
.ound.
The blow has fallen, and private sec. Frank Newton’s
long and willowy presence will soon grace the Govern-
ment House menage no longer. Ostensibly he goes to
join his regiment, but it appears likely that his distinc-
tion-without-a-difference regarding the sending out of
last season’s vice-regal invites may have had much to do
with it.
On Friday afternoon, Lady Chelmsford, accompanied
by Miss Du Cane, presided at the annual meeting of the
Girls’ Friendly Society. The only sensible suggestion
made during some very confused proceedings came, as
usual, from her Ex., who looked cool and smiling in a
pale green gingham made for the heat, with a pretty and
shady hat. Miss Du Cane was her own smiling self in a
pretty heliotrope print and an awful mushroom hat. It
is only the fact that I own and wear one of equal plain-
ness that prevents me further enlarging upon the short-
comings of the toadstool in question. I may even admit
that my hat is the more reprehensible of the two.
A weird tale is drifting round regarding an indiscreet
male being. It is said that he laid his heart and
affluence at the feet of a golden-haired widow,
but she, after considering matters, declined the
alluring offer. The fatal decision reached the would-
be bridegroom in the writing-room of a certain Sydney
hotel. He rose to the occasion. “ Here, boy,” he called,
“ what’s the name of that woman that runs the at
?” “Miss , sir,” replied Buttons. A few
moments’ scratching with a “J ” nib, a letter was
entrusted to the boy for delivery to the lady, and the
engagement was announced next day.
Yours Affectionately,
TRYPHENA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
It is claimed as a virtue in anti-Socialist
J. 0. L. Fitzpatrick, who is standing for Calare
(N.S.W.), that he, when a N.S.W. State
member, _ vigorously demanded that the out-
standing indebtedness of farmers for Government
seed-wheat should be wiped off the slate by a vote
of Parliament. This was carrying beneficent
Socialism to the point of repudiation. The
hollowness of the Anti-Sosh argument is con-
cisely illustrated in this effort to popularise
FitzP. —the Repudiator.
Old Tory Bill Knox, of the Surprising Hat,
is an alleged Protectionist candidate for Kooyong
(Vic.). Knox was elected as a Protectionist to
the first Australian Parliament, and ratted mag-
nificently. He voted to abolish the Protective
duties on tobacco, sugar, vegetables, spirits, bis-
cuits, butter, cheese, candles, paper, and textiles,
and he helped to cut down the proposed 20 per
cent, on woollens to 15 per cent. He still calls
himself a Protectionist, but it is up to Kooyong to
write off Bill as a bad debt.
To show the undesirability of G. H. Wise,
Allan M‘Lean’s opponent in Gippsland, the
ARGUS quotes a Labor secretary’s letter to another
branch :
gefWise) is a good democrat, though he cannot see his
way to sign the Labor platform. For the State Parlia-
ment he advocated a land-tax, and during the railway
strike he sympathised openly and practically with the
men.
An electorate with a candidate whose enemies
hope to kill him with charges like that ought to
be able to pick the right man in the dark.
IN A FUTURE STATE.
REID : “ Who are you? ”
THE HORROR: "I’m your election bogey. How
do you like me now we’ve met!
The Chinese Commissioner, Hwang Hon
Cheng, who is now shaking a loose skirt in these
parts, was lunched at Adelaide by the representa-
tives of the United Asiatic League, and Mr.
Bhagat Singh urged Hwang Hon, when he got to
England, to induce the British Government to
compel Australia to let the brown and yellow multi-
tudes of Asia swarm into this country. And Hon
girded up his pretty frock and was understood to
say he would do his best.
Foreign-trade has evolved in Molden a
choice candidate for Bourke (Vic.). “Yes,” he
said at Brunswick, the other night—“ Yes, I con-
fess lam a Freetrader; but I won’t vote for Free-
trade if you return me.” In short, Molden be-
lieves that Protection is a curse ; but be will put
upwith the curse if only Bourke will put up with
Molden.
In sizing up election probabilities, Sydney
elegraph has flattered itself that Anti-Socialism
is making such strides in Westralia that its partv
is sure to gain in that State. Yet the election for
vreraidton has resulted in the unseating of the
Ministerialist, and is the fourth seat won by the
YVestralian Labor party during 1906.
Lieut.-Kernel M‘Cay, the Anti-Sosh can-
didate for Corio (Vic.), was asked a disconcerting
question at Geelong. The Kernel had just
finished a lonpr rambling address, consisting
mostly of what Deakin had said of him, and what
ii - ^e a-k- When he had finished, he
called as usual for questions, whereupon some-
one asked : “ Will the candidate state his policy?”
And it proved that a policy was the one thing
the Kernel didn’t have about him. He had his
silk socks and all his pretty clothes, but be hadn’t
his policy.

land House of Fat on the Wages Board Bill adds

rag-man in Brisbane, and a member of the biggest
boot-manufacturing firm—Beirne, rags; Davey,
boots. Beirne attacked the Bill furiously, and
did more to kill it than any other member;
whereas, right through, Davey spoke and voted
m favor of Wages Boards. He kept hammering
into that wooden-headed House that good wages
couldn t injure the employer, provided all men
paid them. But it was all wasted.
The editorial silence last Monday in the
Anti-Sosh press, when the result of the Castle-
reagh (N.S.W.) election was known, was so heavy
that the carts broke down with the papers.
Oastlereagh is a part of the Darling electorate.
George Reid lately made a special trip there in
the interests of Anti-Sosh, and, from the press
accounts, the seat was as good as won from Labor
man W. G. Spence. Yet the State Labor candi-
date won on Saturday by about 400 votes, while
the late Hugh MacDonald won in 1904 by only 14.
Premier Bent is probably beginning to
have some respect for the system of Elective
Ministries. Though Education Minister Saehse
has been told by almost every newspaper in the
State and by many fellow-politicians what they
think of his conduct, he still sticks like glue to his
billet. He knows perfectly well that, had a
really strong man been at the head of the Oppo-
sition, his behavior would mean certain defeat to
Bent’s Ministry. Bent knows that too, and most
likely he doesn’t feel so good as he did towards
the fine old British system under which the House
can’t fire out one objectionable Minister without
firing out the whole Government.
At Hursfcville (N.S.W.) the other night,
Johnson, the No-Work-For-Australians can-
didate, announced, as proof of his own
iricorruptibility, that he had been approached
with money bribes by McKay’s agents to
vote for the harvester duties. He men-
tioned another M.H.R. who had also been “ap-
proached.” Well, what did they do about it ?
Did Johnson and his friend get anybody gaoled ?
Is Johnson merely a liar, or is he an unpatriotic
person who allows scoundrels to go round corrupt-
ing Parliament, and then, instead of getting them
punished, makes capital out of their crimes?
Tait is earning the heartfelt hatred of the
begging Vie. municipalities. The councils have
to maintain their own roads, and they feel
hurt. Most of them have to bring gravel
and other road-material from a distance.
They think Tait ought to cut down
his freight charges for the material,
and hold out their hands for backsheesh.
They tell him that their roads are cut
up by the waggons that cart timber and
firewood and wheat to his stations, to be
carried to Mel-
bourne at rates
that are a dead
loss to the rail-
w ays, and
they think
he should
make the loss
bigger by in-
directly pay-
ing for road
repairs. But
Tait says
that the stuff
must come to
the by - sta-
tions, good
roads or bad
roads, and that
the municipalities
must repair the roads whether he helps or
not. So he shuts his face, all but enough of it
to admit his cigar, and points to the door.
Cameron and Bent are both in favor of
helping the municipalities, but Tait isn’t,
and when Tait doesn’t want to do a thing
he just keeps on not doing it, and smokes hard.
And Tait is right. The hard-begging shires get
quite enough subsidies.
Wilks, the Balmain (N.S.W.) Foreign-
trader who wants to expose the iron works
at Mort’s Dock to all the blessings of
foreign competition, asks indignantly why
the Labor men don’t propose to nationalise
the brewing industry as well as the tobacco
industry. Perhaps it is because beer isn’t
the same huge monopoly that tobacco is.
Perhaps because they aren’t the wild confiscators
Bill Wilks says they are. By way of an equally
good conundrum—why has William Henry Wilks
been for 12 years an alleged Freetrade politician
who never once tried to get the heavy Protective
duties taken off the beer industry?

narrow-minded Brown, to spend public money in
Queensland, and most commendable to do it in
Indi, Victoria, provided Indi elects Anti-Sosh
Brown.

“ Forty-six years ago the cry was ‘ Unlock
the Land ’ and that is the cry to-day.”-—Outtrim,
M.L.A., on the jubilee of Vic. Parliament.
Two Mandarins of the second-class pale-
yellow order of the Joss invaded Maoriland
recently, and asked it to show them round its
factories and tell them how Ward ran a Govern-
ment. Also, swinging their pig-tails violently,
they pointed out that China was getting a navy
and army of its own, and “then we will see about
this poll-tax and a few other things you put on
us.” And Maoriland, quoting Ward’s assertion
that the country has seven million cartridges and
more or less of a rifle for every 50 men, stands on
a heap of scoria and says, “Let ’em all come !
There are only four hundred millions of ’em,
anyhow.”
Dr. Jimmy Graham, who is running
against J. O. Watson for South Sydney, has
made the remarkable discovery that the N.S.W.
State railways are “ Private Enterprise,” because
they are run by a Commissioner. Their great
merit, in Dr. Jimmy’s opinion, is that they are
what he calls “ Private Enterprise.” Therefore it
seems reasonable to assume that Dr. Jimmy
believes in selling the railways to a private com-
pany so as to make sure that they will remain in
the beautiful state of Private Enterprise that he
says they have got into —but which, of course,
they are liable to get out of again unless they are
sold to a private co.
An instance showing how the State fares
when it bumps up against the interests of the
land monopolist. On the Queanbeyan road
(N.S.W.) is a town named Hall, situated amidst
hundreds of acres of State land. Two
or three miles further along the same
road is another town, built on freehold
station land. At Hall (the town on State
land) there is no public school, and the
children have to walk from there to
the public school, an expensive stone
building built on the squatter’s private
property. At Hall (the town on State
land) the people asked for a police
station. The request was granted, but
the station is to be built at the town on
the squatter’s land. That the depart-
mental authorities should thus deli-
berately remove a town from State land
on to private property appears a matter
that calls for investigation. Also, since
this expenditure of public money means
increase of the value of the landlord’s
property, it calls for a graduated land-
tax so that the public may get some
at least of its own money back.
“J.R.” writes.:—-
John Bull’s appetite for the marvellous seems to be
infinite. We were fed for days on sensational stories of
the progress of what we were told was a real Boer inva-
sion of Great Bushman Land—the Kalahari Desert in
fact, a territory more arid and desolate than anything
Australia can show. People who don’t take every word
their daily paper tells them for granted wondered at the
stories of these raiders “commandeering” the popula-
tion, and shooting, or threatening to shoot, those who
didn’t want to be commandeered. And then the Cape
police took a baker’s dozen of unarmed, starving, footsore
swag-men, who seem to have been out looking for sand
and rock. And the yarns—“ A cordon of police 150 miles
long- ” ; “all the fords of the Orange River guarded,” &c. !
The principal swagman, Ferreira, seems to be some sort
of Dago. As for the alleged commandeered inhabitants,
f there were any, they “ escaped ” before the police came.
The electors of Dailey (N.S.W.) have
been given in the candidature of Howe, a
young Australian of great ability and force
of character, a chance to wipe out the Wilks
scandal from the Federal legislature. Wilks
is a scandal, a dismal insult to the intelli-
gence of the democracy. To conceal his poli-
tical ignorance he seeks support by braying
about Rome and making excited comments on a
Pope who lives thousands of miles away, and is
probably as little interested in Australia as Aus-
tralia is in him. Balmain, which is the centre of
the Dailey electorate, is a place of narrow, crooked
streets, which shelter quite an unusual number of
conventicles and little Bethels. It is really a
working-man’s suburb, and should vote Labor, but
somehow the folly of sectarianism has infected the
voters, and they have in the past voted for Wilks,
the Freetrade insectarian, whose policy would
shut up Mort’s Dock and Lever’s soap works, and
all the other Balmain industries, and give the
work to foreigners—and mostly Papist foreigners
at that.

THE FIGHTING TEMERAIRE.
—(After Turner).
Mr. Reid will be isolated and deserted in the new Par-
liament. — AOE.
Going to be broken up.
Prime Minister Deakin puts it mildly when
he speaks of “the vague and general way so
characteristic of our opponents.” Compared
with the average Reidite, the goat hesitating
between two clothes-lines is a model of decision.
And in tangibility the amiable lunatic, who
declared that the dream of his life- was a state of
gas and gaiters, could give points to Reid every
time.
The bricklayers’ strike in Melbourne
seems a childish affair. Both sides should remem-
ber that common sense is their best friend. An
appeal to the arbitration of D.C.L. Whisky—the
Spirit of Reason—should not be neglected. With
D.C.L. presiding and inducing that warm glow of
good fellowship so favorable to wise acts, baseless
disagreement melts like the fogs of old-world
conservatism beneath a warm Australian sun.*
A Good Razor. —Can you tell a good razor
from a bad one? Very few men can. We had the privi-
leg-e of watching a clever hairdresser at Messrs. Craig and
Aitken’s business premises in George-street. It appears
that each of the celebrated “ Plum ” razors is tested in
the following fashion before leaving the shop. It must
split a hair—lengthways. It must ring like a bell. The
silver steel of which it is made must be well-tempered.
It must stand being steeped in boiling water and then
immediately immersed in icy cold water without turning
“ blue,” and so on. There is one thing certain to the
man who orders the Plum Razor—he is getting as perfect
a shaving tool as human ingenuity can provide, and at a
cost of only 10s. 6d., post free.*

The Piano of the Future
The New Pianola Piano is an upright piano of the highest
grade, with a Metrostyle Pianola inside its case . It is in every
particular a perfect piano, and, in addition, contains within its
case a complete Metrostyie Pianola, the only Piano-playing
device endorsed by the greatest living musicians.
It can be played by the fingers on the keyboard in the
usual way.
It can be played by means of a Pianola action within its case.
There is nothing to move up in front of the keyboard. To change the
method from hand-playing to Pianola-playing it is only necessary to slide
back a panel in the front of the case, and insert a perforated roll of music.
There is no outward indication which catches the eye to distinguish the
Pianola Piano from the heretofore type of Pianoforte.
