SPORTING
NOTIONS
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen blundered badly
in allowing the Goulburn-Sydney road cycle race to clash
with the opening of the Summer Nights meeting, at
Sydney Cricket Ground, on Monday night. So of
104 acceptances in the road race only 47 faced
the starter, the majority of the others de-
clining to jeopardise their chances in the more
lucrative struggles of Monday night by toiling over 132
miles of dusty, hilly road in the face of a keen ,head
wind. As it happened, a cynical Providence opened the
windows of heaven on Monday afternoon and evening and
let fly so much rain that the meeting was postponed. So
the sacrifice was all in vain.
The race was a great struggle. It was 8.50
when Tom Larcombe started off alone to overtake the 46
starters, the limit riders having left a full hour before.
The wind was almost dead ahead, and when it wasn’t in
the teeth it came slightly from the side, which was as bad,
or a shade worse. At Marulan Larcombe had failed to
gain a minute, and the wind was growing stronger. Fifty
miles eliminated seven riders, and then a whole crop of
punctures broke things up considerably, destroying-
otherwise good chances. The field had become a succes-
sion of little groups, the members of which were practi-
cally pacing one another. Only the scratch man rode
alone and unaided. At Moss Vale, Jefferay, the Parra-
matta rider, who had left with 45 minutes start,
had caught all the riders in front of him. At
Pioton only 18 men were timed, and at Liverpool
it was evident that scratch man Larcombe was hopelessly
out of it, Jefferay and Burr had led consistently from
Oarnden, and the prize lay between them. Ten miles out
from Liverpool, after a sensational struggle, Jefferay
managed to drop his rival. Burr came again with a
mighty effort, but though he gradually drew up on the
leader, he failed to beat him. Eight thousand people
were waiting at Ashfield to see the finish, and burst into
a mighty cheer as Jefferay rode home, closely pursued by
Burr, with Slattery and Buck third and fourth. Jefferay
won £3O cash and a £2O Austral bike, and £lO for winning-
on Continental tyres. Burr appropriated £5 6s. cash
and a gold medal valued at the same amount, and for
fastest timer a Speedwell bike worth £2O. Slattery got
four guineas cash and a gold medal worth two guineas.
Duck won a trophy valued at £5 ss.
* * * »
The coining Austral Wheel Race promises to
be a most unholy frost, the nominations numbering only
half those of the previous year. The fact that the prize
money has been cut down to about half of what it was at
the last meeting seems to point a moral. Much has been
made of the fact that the distance is two miles, but, as it
was two miles previously, there doesn’t seem to be much
in that. Of course, the rider would prefer to toil, puff
and sweat only a mile for his lucre, but if he can’t get it
at the end of a mile he has always seemed quite willing
to go another mile, so long as he was sure the boodle was
there. That seems to be the secret of the trouble—he
has to plug the whole distance, and find only half the
money awaiting him.
» • * *
How the cycling prize-money goes down ! Fol-
lowing the slump in the Austral prize-money, the A.N.A.
Wheel Race, which used to be worth £5OO, has dropped
to £l5O, of which the winner receives only £4O, and the
rest is to be divided among the Ist, 2nd and 3rd class
sections of the race. The best prize of the meeting is now
the A.N.A. Gold Stakes, which is still worth £75. The
Gold Stakes is a scratch race, run over half, one and five-
mile heats, with a three-miles final.
* * * *
Although Saturday was an ideal day for cricket
around Sydney, it was an off day among the batsmen,
and the top score was G. L. Garnsey’s 83 for Burwood
against Redfern. Alphabetical White made 79 for North
Sydney against Glebe, andhis'club-mate, Hopkins, backed
him up with 61. Whitting, of Balmain, slogged Sydney
bowling for 71, and was at last run out." Oranney, of
Central Cumberland, and a player named Noble, of Pad-
dington, made 50 and 56 against Gordon and Middle
Harbor respectively. Altogether the batsmen made a poor
showing. The crack bowler of the afternoon was
M‘Cloy, who bowled only one ball, and with it ended the
innings: 1 for 1. Carty and Winning also bowled well
for Paddington, Carty securing five wickets for 35 runs,
and Winning three for 22. On the other hand, Middle
Harbor produced only one bowler of merit, Whiddon
taking six wickets of the nine that fell for 79 runs. Hill,
of Waverley, bagged five of University for 59. Garnsey
and Deane bowled splendidly for Burwood against Redfern,
the former taking five lots of timber for 19 runs, and the
latter felling five trees for 25.
* * * *
If the team as selected for N.S.W. meets
Queensland on Dec. 7, it looks (on paper) as if Banana-
land will have a hard, cold time. N.S.W. cricket
supporters brag that their team could defeat the rest of
the Commonwealth. Certainly it is a strong team,
though still not so strong as’ last year’s. Mackay is
away, O’Connor is gone, Carter is not Kelly, while Noble
and Trumper are not in such form as of old. But, on the
other hand, none of the other States have made any
progress, and the boast that the Cornstalks could beat
the balance of the Commonwealth is not mere arrogance.
*. *
So far this season, “has-been ” Syd. E. Gregory
has scored in premiership matches 15 not out, 24, 182,
and 29 not out; a total of 250 for twice out; average
125. Compare this with some others, who are not on the
has-been list: Duff, 31, 2, 15 ; total 48 ; average 16.
Hopkins : 5, 39, 0, 61; total 105 ; average 26J. The
great Trumper : 54, 9, 37, 38 ; total 138 ; average 34J.
Also the great Noble : 23, 56 not out, 32, 56 ; total 167 ;
average rather over 55. Why, compared to the “has-
been,” the other fellows are mere blokes.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association is £6OO in debt,
and is applying the pruning knife with a vengeance.
Even the umpires are now requested to pay their own
fares, and supply their services and white coats for nix.
The team that is to tour Westralia goes under an arrange-
ment by which players will be allowed a stated sum for
expenses, but nothing for loss of time, unless the tour
turns out a financial success—which, no doubt, it will. On
the other hand, the Association is trying- other speculations
with a view to raising the wind. Garnsey recently com-
piled acollection of “ Cricketana,”andthe Association has
bought it for £4O, and will publish it. It is to be hoped
that if the book turns out a success the Association
will give Mr. Garnsey a further share of the profits, £4O
being a miserable amount to pay for a really good collec-
tion of cricketing reminiscenses. In a community like
Australia such a book, if properly written, ought to go
like ripe cherries.
* * * *
The death of Canon T. B. Tress, in Victoria,
last week, removed a very old cricketing figure. Away
back in February, 1862, the late reverend gentleman,
then a youth of 18, played in the match between H. H.
Stephenson’s First English XI. and 22 of Bathurst, and
was responsible for the fall of eight English wickets,
catching and bowling three of them himself. Till Tress
did the fell deed nobody believed that a mere Australian
could bowl an All-Englander.
Cricket umpires have a much rosier time of it
in Melbourne than in Sydney just now, in spite of the
decadent state of the game in the southern capital. There
they receive 7s. 6d. for an afternoon’s work, and 15s. for
the full day. In the recent colts’ match it is said that
the-Sydney umpires received only os. for the two days.
The danger is that this sort of retrenchment may lead to
the Association getting the cheap and nasty brand of
umpiring—it all depends on the enthusiasm of the um-
pires.
* * * *
The ancient players’ trouble broke out again in
Sydney cricket last week, when M. A. Noble, “without
consulting anyone else whatever,” wrote to the Associa-
tion, suggesting that it should rescind the disqualifica-
tion that prevents any of the “ players ” holding office
for three years. In spite of some deplorable speeches,
among the worst of which was that of President Trickett,
the Association said it would see the players “ blowed ”
first. Wild rumors were flying about that the letter was
really an ultimatum, and that unless the disqualifica-
tion was lifted the players would refuse to take part in
inter-State matches. And the Association said, in effect,
“ Let ’em.” Which was a very correct and proper
attitude. The sentence was only imposed after careful
and mature consideration, and to have lifted it would
have been a confession that it was harsh and unjustifi-
able. It would have been most unfortunate had the
Association shown any signs of weakness. To have done
so would have been to invite more trouble in the near
future, and to acquire a name for much bark and no bite.
Far better to acquire a name for no bark and a large
bite. There is little danger of the players throwing
themselves out of first-class cricket; anyhow, if they do,
they are not indispensable. There is a lovely crop of
bright young players coming along.

Of 25 cars that started in the Dunlop Reli-
ability Test only nine finished, and some of them in a
most damaged state. Seven of the original 25 were De
Dions, of which four finished, and among them they won
the second prize of the competition and two class prizes.
Against this must be placed the fact that only De Dions
started in the single-cylinder class. The starting Dar-
racqs numbered four, of which only one finished, winning
the first prize ; of the other three, Allen’s ran four days,
Holmes’ three, and Stevens’ one. Two Tarrants (Aus-
tralian-made) left Melbourne. One of them ran four
days; the other made the fastest time for the competi-
tion and won the third prize in the competition and the
class prize for O division, which was a most excellent
record. Of the four Talbots only one finished, the other
three being put out during the first day. There was
only one Decauville in the struggle, and it was biffed out
at the end of the first day. There was also one Clement-
Bayard, and it, too, saw only one day of the Test.
Kellow’s 40h.p. Napier had hard luck, but got through,
and, being the only one of its division left in, carried off
the class prize. The solitary Argyll also dropped out
after the first day. Of the two Ooventry-Humbers one
ran right through, but secured no prizes, and the other
lasted only the first day, while the two Beeston-Humbers
were both outed at the end of the second day. Reads
like the record of a naval battle of the first magnitude.
The De Dions ought to have scored well. In
Glass A they had no competition ; of the seven that
started in Class B two were De Dions, two were
Talbots, one Tarrant, one Beeston-llumber, and one
Bayard. In Class 0, of 10 starters, two were De Dions,
three Darracqs, two Coventry-Humbers, one Clement-
Talbot, one Argyll, and one Tarrant. Of the unfortunate
Class D none were De Dions, and the field was made up
of one each of Napier, Talbot, Darracq, Decauville, and
Beeston-Humber. Evidently the automobile will have to
be made a lot more reliable before the man of moderate
means can go in for road-racing as an amusement. The
car built for good European roads is not nearly strong
enough for the rough tracks that are dignified by the
title of the King’s highway in Australia, and even if men
prefer foreign-made engines, this competition has proved
beyond a doubt that the wise man will get the frame of
his car and all the rest of it made here by people who
have a knowledge of the ways in the land of Bent and
Carruthers. The arranging and supervision of the Test
cost the big Dunlop Rubber Co. £4OO. Also, it cost some
of the competitors the price of practically re-building
their gimcrack importations.
* * * •
The Hog in the Car, accompanied by his
Hogess, is beginning to make periodic appearances on
the roads round Sydney, and sufficiently often to make
one of two things necessary. Either motorists must be
compelled to carry a detection number, at least 2ft. high,
on the back of the automobile, where pursuers can see it,
or the people who don’t drive cars must go armed and
ready to shoot at sight. The latter alternative had a
most salutary effect in France, where the automobilist
now goes warily. In Germany the Imperial Government
passed laws which make the Hog responsible for the
maintenance for life of anyone he cripples, and for the
support of his or her children till they are able to sup-
port themselves. A doctor and his daughter, while
cycling near Manly (Syd.), were nearly run over by a car
that came round a corner at headlong speed, taking both
sides of the road at once, and only a couple of weeks pre-
viously a man was run down from behind a carload of fat
publicans, who had to pay heavy damages. Careless
driving of motor cars should be punished by a heavy fine
and imprisonment, but the first thing is to detect your
Car Hog, hence the need for the big number.
» * * *
The recent Thousand-miles Motor Reliability
Test was a triumph for the Continental tyres. The
winning car was shod with them, and so was Grimwade’s
Tarrant, which made fastest time and won the third
prize. Nine of the competitors pinned their faith to
Continentals and found it justified, never having to
change or make repairs of any sort. The prizes
carried off by cars shod with Continentals were the
Dunlop Cup, the Garland Cup and Motor Blue Riband
for Australia, Dunlop Gold Medal, Mota Trophy and
Pratts’ Cup. The winner of the Goulburn to Sydney
cycle road race on Saturday also used Continental tyres.*
* * * *
N.S.W. Attorney-General has announced that
he will introduce a Bill to legalise trotting at betting
matches or betting at trotting matches—it is all one.
Presumably, it will be a very short measure of about
three clauses, making any place where trotting meetings
are held a licensed racecourse for the time being, or
something of that sort. But, anyhow, it will be a huge
and inglorious backdown. If trotting races are necessary
to improve the breed of trotters, and facilities for betting-
are necessary for improving the trotting races, then
betting is necessary in anything that depends upon com-
petition, and should be allowed at boxing matches,
bicycle meetings, athletic gatherings and sausage-eating
competitions ; so why not let ’em all come ?
* • • •
Universal Provider Samuel Hordern is building
a rare and lovely motor yacht to the design of the well-
known naval architect, Walter Reeks. It will be the
largest motor yacht in Australasia, and one of the world’s
front-rankers, measuring 110 ft. over all. With the ex-
ception of the engines and pianola, everything in the
yacht, and the yacht itself, will be of Australian manu-
facture. Down below, the main saloon will take up 20ft.
of the width of the hull, and the owner’s state-room will
be lift, long, joined by a 7ft. bathroom. Next to that
will be two other state-rooms, each 9ft. long. The rest of
the space is to be taken up by an after, or overflow, cabin
and state-rooms, an engine-room, and forward a kitchen
and fo’c’sle for the crew. On the deck will be a music-
room, 21ft. by Bft., a pilot or wheel-house, naphtha tank,
and sail locker. The bridge deck will be 38ft. by 16ft.,
and around it will be the, boats, consisting of a 20ft.
motor launch, a gig and dinghy. In order to utilise every
inch of space the yacht will have cruiser bow and stern.
There will be three engines of the English Thornycroft,
make, each driving a separate shaft and propeller, and
each of 100 h.p., and capable of shoving the boat 15 miles,
an hour.. The two masts are to be hinged, and can be
lowered, so that the boat can pass under any decent,
bridge. Even the beautiful Bronzewing will have to lower
its colors before this craft.

People who had laid their reputations and
week’s pocket money that Chris. Webb would show the-
way home in the Sydney Flying Squadron’s blue ribbon
event on Saturday received a bad scare. Old man of the-
harbor George Holmes blew the Donnelly along so fast,
that it looked anybody’s race till right on the finish, when
Christian Webb managed to get a lead of 38 seconds*
which he maintained till the line was crossed.
Twenty boats turned up to the start, but Arawa and.
Young Jack, getting entangled, drifted on to Clark
Island, where the Arawa stayed for a time. Young
Jack got off and took an honorary part in the:
race. Vision, ably navigated by C. Abbot, went away
with a great rush and led the flotilla to the Lightship,
with Donnelly and Australian close behind. When the:
ballooners and ringtails flashed white against the grey-
green water Donnelly and Australian drew up very quickly..
Vision did not use a ringtail. Donnelly went to the front,,
and Vision subsided into third place. At Clark Island,
Australian had supplanted Donnelly, and Vision was.
still some 12 seconds in front of the fourth boat. In,
the latter part of the race, Crescent bowled along at a
great rate completely hidden by a huge balloon er, but,
failed to make good, for shortly afterwards the nor’-
easter died down considerably, and then Australian’s huge
sail did its deadly work, and Webb managed to shake off
his bull-doggy opponent. The race ended with Aus-
tralian first home with 38sec. to spare, Donnelly and!
Vision second and third, and Scot 22sec. behind Vision.
¥ * * *
Sydney is sending a couple of yachts to
Melbourne to take part in the races for La Carabine
and Sayonara Cups. Mr. Brockhoff will navigate his
11-ton Rawhiti from Sydney to Port Phillip himself, while
the Heather will go over on the deck of a steamer.
Norman Murray will manage the Heather in the racing.
The Sayonara is now the property of well-known man-
about-town, “Billy” Newbigin.
* » * *
A Maoriland man has just been fined £2 10s.
for catching a trout with a worm for bait. Just where
the iniquity of catching fish with a decent worm comes
in is not plain. Had he caught it with an artificial fly or
a beastly minnow made of metal or rubber, he would not
have been liable to punishment. Perhaps the law is for
the protection of the worm. It is quite obvious that it is
much kinder to the trout to catch it with a real worm
than an artificial bait; it at least gets the worm, whereas
with the artificial bait it not only loses its life but gets
done in for the bait also. So the law must be intended
as a kindness to the worm.
* * * *
When Hindu wrestler Buttan Singh lost his
championship at Melbourne Oyclorama building, the
other Monday, like a true Asiatic he played a mean
trick on the winner. It was understood and ac-
cepted as a fair thing that the brown man’s third
match with Clarence Weber would be his last appear-
ance. He would retire on his long championship,
which only Hackenschmidt had been able to inter-
rupt, unless the white boy could beat him this time, best
two out of three. He stipulated for 60 per cent, of the
gate money, win or lose, and would wrestle on no other
terms. Even this clause in the contract was accepted as
a reasonably unsportsmanlike arrangement. Buttan
Singh is a man of 50, and his age entitled him to con-
sideration, although “ his looks didn’t pity him." The
Buttan who took the floor t’other night—a picture of
muscular condition—seemed 15 years younger than his
age for wrestling purposes. Weber, bigger than ever,
showed an aggressiveness which belied his mild, anxious
countenance, but the physical strength of the Indian’s
defence was almost as notable as Weber’s superior agility
and trickiness.
Hackenschmidt had beaten the wonderful
veteran two years ago by grabbing him suddenly and
giving him a giant swing before landing his two shoulders
on the mat—a “ flying” fall. Weber was unable to get a
swing at Buttan. Probably even the great Hacky couldn’t
have worked the same point a second time on the Asiatic.
So the contest for the first 10 minutes was a succession
of “Nelsonie” efforts by Weber and clever escapes by
Buttan. Then the men got tangled together, and the
end of the bout was that Buttan pulled one of Weber’s
legs into a position favorable for “ screwing ” the knee
and foot, and Weber saved his agonised limb by giving the
fall. He just banged the boards with one hand in token of
submission, and Buttan went back to his corner a fortunate
Hindu. Nevertheless, he was palpably out of love with
his job. The unwonted combativeness of his young foe-
man had gruelled the dusky one, and as he sat waiting
for Weber, who had limped away to his dressing-room for
massage, his lips moved as though he were praying to
Buddha or Mahomet to set up complicationsin the white
man’s twisted knee and put him hors de combdt. But
Weber came back, and started to go for Buttan more than
ever. Getting him on the mat in a trussed-fowl posture
he pressed his head and shoulders down and worked at him
with vigorous persistency. They were playing for keeps,
and Buttan was getting a bad time. Presently both men
were on their feet, and Weber rushed |at the Indian and
bore him back against the ropes. Again he grabbed him,
flung him to the mat, and, pouncing upon him,
got a hold which nearly gave him a fall right
away. Buttan saved himself for the moment with the
aid of an adjacent boundary rope, but Weber retained
his hold and pulled away at the brown, greasy body until
he had it flat on its back—dead beaten. One fall to each.
Then did the cowed Buttan sacrifice the one great and last
chance of his Australian life—the chance to earn a mighty
ovation. He couldn’t hope to avoid defeat in a final bout,
except by accident, but he had made so good a fight
against the strong young man that 3000 people were pre-
pared to hail him as a 50-year-old Hero if he would only
stand up like a sport to receive his quietus. If he had
bowed to the inevitable and put himself down on the mat
without assistance from Weber, it would have been a
graceful proceeding. With 60 per cent, of the gate-money
assured to him there was but one inexcusably con-
temptible thing that Buttan could do —and he did it.
After waiting to be rubbed down he got up from his chair,
and on his own initiative, or at the suggestion of Jack
Graham, he slunk out of the ring. It was announced
that he was suffering from a sore mouth and “pains in
the head,” an excuse which might be urged for many an
unbeaten boxer at the end of the first round. So Weber
was left to win the championship through Buttan’s
default.
It is seemingly the native vanity of our colored
brethren, far exceeding the vanity of the weakest white
man, which prompts them to retire from a wrestling
match rather than stay to get beaten in a satisfactory
manner. Weber has now lowered the colors of both
Gunga Brahm and Buttan Singh, but neither of them
would give him the satisfaction of being declared the
winner in the presence of his adversary. Gunga, after
receiving one fall from Weber in Wirths’ circus ring a
while ago, was not game to continue the contest. He
simply slunk off, and caused unpleasantness. The same
disappearing trick was practised by Buttan last week.
He wouldn’t stay in e\ idenee to be declared a loser, and,
if he had been downed in che first bout, he would doubt-
less have escaped at that early stage of the proceedings.
The fact of his having fought out (and won) his first
match with Weber, after los.ng the first (flying) fall by an
unexpected cross-buttock, merely proves that he was con-
fident of his ability to pull through. For the future.it
will be advisable to ensure a proper formal ending to a
wrestling match by a clause in the articles requiring both
men to be present in the ring when the result is declared.
Why not? Even if one of them were dead the announce-
ment could do him no harm.

One of the saddest results of the Wade Anti-
Gambling Act was the dissolution of the National Sport-
ing Club of Sydney. On its site has arisen the colossal
Brennan’s Amphitheatre, which will be devoted exclusively
to boxing and wrestling exhibitions. The cld social hall,
where the members periodically gave themselves up to
music, song and liquor, is now the vestibule of the great
emporium. The bar and the billiard tables have
* one, andso have the bathrooms where the hot, dusty
members took a refreshing dip before settling down to
enjoy themselves for the evening. The boxing hall is a
splendid place. It is tall and airy, and has
a sliding roof; and from the furthest point
m the hall the spectator can sit and get an
uninterrupoed view of the scrap. There are no galleries
in the new structure, and the enthusiastic barracker up
aiott will not be able to drop peanuts with impunity on
xT^i. Cai *™ below who roars for the other fellow.
IN either are there any pillars, and in the tiers of seats 5000
people can sit and see without interruption.

I\wa 8 in this new structure that Billy McCall
met Mike Williams, of Africa, and, to the everlasting-
shame of a Sydney audience, was hooted savagely be-
cause he had met a better man than himself, and was
no longer the fighting machine he once had been. It was
certainly a very brief battle, but while it lasted it was
hard, and Mike Williams’right is a filthy thing for any
man s jaw to bump. From first to last it was merely a
question of endurance. Both the big men slogged with
a v.- u 6ll i. ni, °kt, ev ery blow got home, and it was a case in
which the harder and stronger man must win. McCall is
not as strong as he used to be before he wrenched his
shoulder in that fight with Scanlan, and his blows seemed
to lack their old-time force. Possibly Michael is an
unusually hard man. Also, Michael hit faster and landed
two blows to McCall’s one. In the first round the Waler
wa,s beaten badlly on the ribs and narrowly escaped
being outed by a vicious uppercut. In the second
half of the round Williams punctuated the battering he
gave McCall’s ribs with a few bashes in the face and round
{I 1 ® P°bility generally. McCall landed blow after blow on
Williams’ head, but the South African bored in and dealt
him a smash on the ear, which made him stagger. While
he was dazed Williams whirled his right to the ribs three
times in quick order, and down rolled the Australian. In
the second round Williams attacked with the fury of a
wounded lion, and always his right landed on that one
spot of McCall’s ribs that was now covered with a red
mark. McCall smashed Williams heavily in the face, but
the African landed him on the mouth with the left, and
hooked him on the jaw. Before he could do more McCall
hit in the face with both hands, but even as Williams
reeled his left swung to McCall’s jaw, and the Australian
went down for eight seconds. Resuming, Williams left
the ribs alone and battered McCall’s head and face. Mac.
did his best to do as he was done by, but the flesh wasn’t
able, and the bell brought respite. In the third, Wil-
liams landed on McCall’s stomach, and McCall sent a
pretty left to the throat. Williams rushed in and shot
oyer McCall’s back, and as he came up McCall caught
him in the stomach, and then took a rip in the same
locality himself. With a yell of pain the African rushed
in and caught a chop on the neck, and a drive on the
jaw. He came again and got a couple home on the face
and jaw. Then McCall was beaten to the ropes, and bat-
tered on the sore spot of his ribs some more. His
strength had given out, his blows were weak, and only
the bell saved him. He went to his corner a wreck, and
after a brief consultation the towel was suddenly jerked
into the ring, and McCall—the great McCall—joined the
pathetic army of the Has-Beens and the Weres.

With the bait of thirty thousand dollar purses
floating around his head, retired champion pug. Jim
Jeffries is seriously thinking of returning to the ring,
but is said to be worried about finding a foeman
worthy of his hash. James has no great opinion of the
rising crop of heavies, and declares that he will not take
money under false pretences. All of which is very noble.
The little town of Goldfield, in Nevada, which gave a
$30,000 purse for the Gans-Nelson affair, offers to put up
a $40,000 purse if Jeffries will fight, and it is the thought
of this huge wad of coin that is making the farm down
Los Angeles way seem mighty slow. The Gans-Nelson
affair seems to have been a shady transaction—the usual
U.S. shady transaction—right through. First, negro
Gans alleges that he was offered $25,000 to go under to
Nelson, but refused, being sure that he could win the
fight and have the pleasure of giving Nelson, whom he
hates, a real good hiding at the same time. Then, the
night before the fight, Nelson suddenly intimated that he
would not go on unless Gans agreed to take the
loser’s end of the purse, win or lose; and, rather
than let the fight fall through, Gans agreed, and so got
only $lO,OOO of the prize money for the fight he won.
The promoters are taking special precautions to prevent
anything of the sort happening again, and in all future
fights at Goldfield the articles will specify that the
winner must receive the winner’s share, and no secret
arrangement must be entered into for a division of the
prize money on any other basis. This will do an awful
lot of good—in theorv.

The big Dunlop Rubber Co. had no reason to
complain of its luck in the Thousand Miles Reliability
Test, as it again secured the best percentage. No less
than five of the nine cars that struggled through to a
finish were shod with Dunlops, and secured the Dunlop
Amateur Cup, the Baillieu Trophy, the first prize for
Class B, and the Proctor Trophy. Last year 13 out of the
16 cars that got through from Melbourne to Sydney were
Dunlops, and in 1904 the first five to finish used the same
trusty tyre. Dunlops were also to the front in the great
Tourist Trophy, run in the Isle of Man recently, and keep
up their reputation on both sides of the globe with
splendid percentages.*

The popular anti-Sosh gag among the
farmers now is that, though the progressive land-
tax doesn’t touch them yet, it is only a beginning
—“a preliminary attack,” Sydney Telegraph
calls it. Last year, in N.S. Wales, there were
only 18,593 men who held farms of from 200 to
1000 acres. In holdings of over 1000 acres there
were 36,824,443 acres, or enough for 61,370 farms
of 600 acres each. If the big holdings were cut up
into, say, 600-acre farms, there would thus be
61,370 men ready to stand side by side with the
other 18,593 in defence of their comparatively
small holdings. The 18,593 farmers possibly may
not be over-safe now; cut up and settle the big
estates, and they will be at least four times safer.
“Star”: You affirm that capital is not
being driven out of this country. Well, it will
be. I am expecting a considerable legacy pre-
sently. I am not going to stay in a country
whose Government is not prepared to defend me,
and my family, my money, and my property.
Not much. Further, I mean to take my nine
boys and girls out of a land which encourages
yellow persons to live side by side with me, and
so expose me to the chance of one day finding
myself grandparent to a half-caste. Now tell me
of some clean land of courageous white people
where I and mine will be secure, and where there
is a good, reliable army and navy to defend my
property.
Some of these curious Tory “ ladies ” who
circulate unscrupulous falsehoods about the
alleged intention of their opponents to confiscate
all property, break up the home, violate the mar-
riage tie, &c, make pathetic allusions to their sex
when an audience becomes restive under their
coarse abuse and crude slanders, and the intima-
tion that no real gentleman would interrupt a
lady is heard quite frequently. It is hard to say
where to draw the line in such matters. This
writer once saw a shining light of anti-Sosh call
on a policeman not only to interrupt but to arrest
a lady, and yet her statements were quite as
accurate as many made by the ornaments of the
Women’s Reform League, and no more incoherent.
■The policeman called her case “disorderly con-
duct,” too.
This is the attitude of the Anti-Sosh party,
as outlined by George Reid at various meetings,
towards the graduated land-tax for bursting up the
big estates that are blocking settlement near the
centres of population : The party is wildly in favor
of closer settlement, but at the market price of the
land (that is, the price the owners ask) it admits
it would be unprofitable to buy the land for closer
settlement. And any attempt to make a man
sell, even at the market rate, is “spoliation” and
“confiscation.” So, although the party and its
leader hanker and yearn for closer settlement,
they will do nothing to encourage it. It’s “un-
British ” and “ Socialistic ” to do anything. Men
who want to go on the land and engage in the
great primary industries, please note.

When Hawthorne hoped to get the Nepean
(IN.S. W.) seat he ran as a staunch Freetrader with
I lotection for the Lithgow ironworks as a side
line. Now he runs for Newcastle, and poses as
an anti-Laborite while advocating “Justice for
the Newcastle coal-miners.” A slippery person
is Hawthorne.
Some person with an extra-violent pen has
been writing, in a leaflet circulated among the
Melbourne building-trade strikers, that The
‘IS dSpXs US X’fcttS 1
ment of working conditions as regards hours and
labor.” This paper remarks briefly that it didn’t
say anything of the sort. What "it did say, and
says some more, is that Australia can’t afford to
start fooling with a six-hour day and things of
that sort (1) while its ports are so widely open to
competitors who work 10 or 12 hours a day ; (2)
while it is so deeply in pawn to a foreign Cohen ;
(3) while it has such a vast amount of Defence
and other work to do and pay for.
The three Vic. Anti-Sosh candidates for the
Senate make a rather quaint announcement: —-
They believe that the machinery of the State should be
used in such a way as will afford facilities to individuals
to help themselves.
It is wonderful—simply wonderful—how these
Private Enterprise people rely on “ the machinery
° f State,
Ex-State-Premier Twaddell is again per-
vading N.S.W. Assembly with a motion to the
•«?**» ‘!“> N.S.W. Legislature should select a
-b edeial capital on its own, and fix Lyndhurst as
the site. The Australian Constitution says
clearly (clause 125): —
The seat of Government of the Commonwealth shall be
determined by The Parliament.
and clause 1 (chapter 1) says just as clearly that
“ The Parliament ” is the Australian Parliament,
not the N.S.W. Legislature, nor Twaddle. And
the Australian Parliament has already selected
Dalgety. Also, it is none of Twaddle’s business.

N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley completes his
twentieth year of office to-day, having been
appointed in succession to James Martin on 29th
November, 1886. Previous records: Francis
Forbes, October 1828—July, 1837 (under 14
years). James Dowling, Puisne Judge, August,
1829; Acting Chief Justice, April, 1836; Chief
Justice, August, 1837 —September, 1844. Alfred
Stephen, Puisne Judge, April, 1839 ; Chief
Justice, October, 1844—November, 1873 (twenty-
nine years). James Martin, November, 1873 —
November, 1886. Julian E. Salomons, 15th
November—29th November, 1886 (not sworn in).
F. M. Darley, November 29th, 1886—. The
barristerial profession awaits the filling in of that
hiatus, and some of it is getting pretty old while
waiting.
“ Wurrym ” from Perth :
For a merchant, one Stubbs (nicknamed “ Spitty ”),
Mayor of Perth (W.A.), has original financial ideas. He
thought he saw a chance to make a profit out of a con-
tract to supply the Westralian Railway Department
with dog spikes which he proposed to import, of course,
from Germany. But a beastly Melbourne man got in
with a lower quotation, so Stubbs was left. At a Mayor’s
parlor shickerette—not a bust, you know—at which John
Forrest was present, Stubbs lamented the alleged injury
done to Westralia by Federation, and quoted this particu-
lar instance in which work is to be done in Australia in-
stead of in Germany. “Look what this State has lost
through letting the contract to an outsider. Why, if I’d
got the contract, the Government would have had to pay
duty on them spikes, and then they’d have got their
proportion of the duty back as revenue. The Melbourne
man will manufacture the spikes, so they won’t be im-
ported, and the Government won’t have to pay duty, and
therefore,” &c., &c. It is wonderful logic, but of such
things are the grievances of Secesh.
Bruce Smith, at Burwood (N.S.W.) :
Many Australian politicians and people live in a fool’s
paradise. They forget that races chat are on the crest
of the wave to-day may be in the hollows of the sea to-
morrow.
True, many politicians and people are living in a
fool’s paradise, and a lot of B. Smith’s friends are
of the number. These short-sighted people tell
Australia to look to Britain for defence, and want
to flood Australia with colored aliens, who would
be useless as fighting units. Sane Australians
realise that it is very foolish to depend so wholly
on Britain because, although on the crest of the
wave to-day (as B. Smith puts it), it may be in
the hollow next week. Then it will be no use
hollering to Britain to save us. Sane Australians
want their country to be White, and to provide
its own defences, so that, if that marine catas-
trophe should overtake the Ma Country, Australia
will have at least a chance of swimming.
Dr. Philip Muskett, of Sydney, who has
just produced another book about babies and the
way to kill them, counts up 341,578 infants who
died in Australia between 1884 and 1905.
“ Infants ” means children under two years of age,
and 341,578 works out at 42 a day for 22 years,
including Sundays and holidays. Half this num-
ber might have been saved to become citizens of
the Commonwealth had proper food and nursing
been given them. Of every 1000 infants fed on
artificial foods 252 don’t want feeding after the
second year, while only eight per 1000 of breast-
fed infants die. The doctor suggests that printed
instructions on “Howto feed the baby ” should
be sent to every mother, and that a nursing
league, with lectures and demonstrations in baby-
management, should be established. Milk depots
and an infants” sanatorium and foundling homes
would save more young Australians for Australia
than all the immigration proposals in sight could
achieve for years to come.
As soon as the Melbourne Argus had
printed a letter calling on tradespeople to refuse
credit to the Melbourne building-trade strikers,
the Civil Service Stores cut in with help to the
inen by arranging for supplies on wholesale
terms. That system was first introduced to Aus-
tralia during the four-months strike of miners at
Broken Hill (N.S.W.). Not a penny in cash was
issued all that time; strilce-pay was distributed
in “coupons,” exchangeable for goods, bought
wholesale, and distributed at the strikers’ own
depdts. The saving was so great that it took
fourmonths to starve out men who otherwise would
have gone down in three.

“ Shell ” in defence of the scow :
Re your paragraph (l/11/’O6) about the Maoriland scow.
Regarding the “ series of mishaps” and the “ troublesome
habit of turning turtle,” you are wrong in both cases.
The genuine, square-bilged Auckland-built scow is one of
the finest things that ever swam, and will stand up to
anything. During the last 10 years, not more than a
dozen scows were totally wrecked. Two were never
heard of again, and it was considered that their sails
were blown away and they drifted to the eastward of
Maoriland. The rest were lost by carelessness or
ignorance. There are Maoriland sailors who will go any-
where in them. These scows command the pick of the
men on the coast, and are never in want of crews. This,
I think, speaks for itself, or rather for the scow. All the
fuss that has been made about them has been caused by
people ashore, who know next to nothing of the subject.
Severe cramps, colic, and diarrhoea are
promptly cured by Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera,
and Diarrhoea Remedy.*
From a Yic. Parliamentary reporter :
Consider the artistry of Vic. Champion Stonewaller
Gaunson. During the present session of the Vic. State
Assembly, his speeches in Hansard occupy nearly as
many pages as those of all the other members put to-
gether—an achievement of which any waster of public
time might reasonably be proud. But Gaunson is more
than a time waster. As compared with the stonewalls of
Johnson and Wilks, his most able competitors in Aus-
tralia, his work shines like that of a practised mason
beside that of the selector who builds his own mud
hut. Johnson erects random rubble, plain, dull,
colorless, and in every respect just Mud. Wilks gives
his hearers cheap, meretricious stuff, which excites
the admiration of the people who think the jerry-built
suburban villa very fine. But Gaunson’s stonewall is;
tooled ashlar, such as the Parthenon might be built of.
Gaunson is always entertaining. He leaves not a dull
spot in a five-hours’ speech, and not a bit of it is cheap or
gaudy. He is astute enough to be studiously courteous
to the chair and to his opponents. He knows exactly how
to dodge the forms of the House, and still he is able to
touch on every subject under the sun in discussing the
amendment on clause 5 a “that the word ‘the’ be
omitted, with a view to substituting the word ‘a.’”
In advocating the word “a ” he will allude in
passing to the cuneiform inscriptions of the
Babylonians, show their influence on the tick-pest in
Queensland, argue from the basis of the regulations for
tick-prevention to the destruction of Rohzjestvensky’s
fleet, and, when somebody objects that he is digressing,
show, by the most complicated process of reasoning,
how all this is intimately connected with the running of
ponies on four-furlong courses. From the manufacture
of saddlery by the ancients, Gaunson will briefly—which
means in his case for about two hours —outline the legis-
lation of the Tudors to cope With witchcraft, and, inci-
dentally—but always in direct relation to the merits
of “a” over “the”—describe the home-life of the
residents of Padua in 1015 A.D., with Shakspearian
references to back up his contentions. After a few
remarks on Alpine climbing, the court of Versailles prior
to the French Revolution, and the progress of bacteri-
ology among the Aztecs, Gaunson will sit down, having
accentuated his points by relating 14 excellent anecdotes
and given 38 quotations from the Talmud, “Mr. Dooley
in Peace and War,” and other standard works. As a
cheerful, pleasing squanderer of the public time, which
is money, Australia has never before seen the like of
D. Gaunson.
Tasmania, it is alleged, has a pretty big
land scandal to clear up in the shape of wholesale
“dummying ”on King Island. But scandals can
smell very high and very long before they draw
attention in slow Tasmania. The Tory nose is a
very patient nose.
An impudent proposal was made in the
Anglican Synod, at Melbourne, to change the
name of the Anglican Church to that of the
“ National Church of Australia.” Australia isn’t
taking any National Church ; especially it isn’t
taking, as “National,” an imported church, full
of imported parsons, and imported bishops, who
almost invariably go to England to die, unless
they perish so suddenly that they can’t get away.
The first prize for an original poem in con-
nection with the Toowoomba (Q.) Austral Festival,
held at the beginning pf November, was won by
Mrs. M. Forrest, of Brisbane, whose poetical and
prose contributions have frequently adorned the
pages of The Bulletin. The verses which won
the blue ribbon are entitled “The Land of the
Sun.” George Essex Evans was the judge, and
G.E.E. knows a poem from an improvement lease
when he see them.
John Lewis, M.L.C., one of the hardshell
Conservatives of S. Australia, has announced that,
despite the result of the elections, he, for one, is
not going to give in over the franchise-reform
question. And The Bulletin almost hopes that
John won’t. If John’s House will only “ blow
the people ” violently enough, there seems a very
good chance of the" people finally deciding to
“ blow ” John’s House. And it can be blowed (or
blown) by the peremptory stoppage of supplies
till it passes the Bill, for its own abolition.
The aspirations of the Kelly family, of
Sydney, are various. Willie-motor Kelly’s great
ambition is to count out the House of Reps. His
little brother, T.H., is seeking re-election as a
second-rate alderman in Sydney City Council.
T.H. has the family weakness for heroics, and can
speak about a drain as if it were the battle of
Marathon. In the municipal elections T. H. tries
to carry on his back ex-gaud-mayor Hughes, one of
whose titles to fame is that whilst in office he
officially received Major Taylor, the black cyclist.
The utter hypocrisy of the so-called Pro-
testant Defence Association in Victoria, in choos-
ing three solid Tories as candidates for the
Senate, is revealed by the fact that not one of
tbe whole nine candidates in the field belongs to
tbe R.O. Church. There was no need to select
candidates against “Rome,” for “Rome’’hasn’t
a candidate running. But, seeing that the nine
were equally Protestant, the P.D.A. urges the
public to vote for the three most virulent Tories
by way of protest against “ the errors of Rome.”
The P.D.A. seems to think that progress is
“ Rome.”
Thus “ Squidge ” :
Victoria’s great T. Bent has a vast scheme for dis-
possessing the Western District squirearchy and setting
up in its place a nation of prosperous small landholders,
but the idea is moving along with the same old leaden
hoofs that most of Thomas’s decent schemes walk on.
Thomas has announced that there will be no legislation
on the question this session. He might as well have said
“ this Parliament.” It is plain that the ear of the adi-
pose Thomas has been chewed by the Western squat-
tocracy. Th * end will be that the squatters will get
their railway for nothing, and either keep the land, or
sell it to Bent at its value, plus its value as an heirloom,
plus the enhanced value caused by the railway, and plus
the artificial value created by exemption from taxes and
by artificially created land scarcity and by specially low
railway rates, likewise plus any casual swindle or side
issue that can be heaped on top of all the other plusness.
The Commonwealth’s Commission which
is inquiring into Captain Barton’s administration
in Papua was, at latest, plodding through its un-
pleasant job under circumstances that a Commis-
sion doesn’t often bump against. There isn’t any
newspaper within coo-ee to print the evidence, or
give an inkling of what allegations are made.
The Commission, however, crawls as far over that
difficulty as it can by every morning posting up,
outside its wigwam, a type-written report of the
previous day’s proceedings. When the last rare
boat left tbe possession, Chief Judge Murray—well-
known in Sydney as head of the Irish-Australian
regiment —had been giving evidence for a day and
a half; and the general impression seemed to be
that, after his vigorous remarks during that
period, British Papua would hardly be large
enough to comfortably hold both the Adminis-
trator and the Chief Judicial Officer.

Sir, —Your journal poses as a democratic
one, yet we find, in your last issue (a considerable
Quantity of severe language is here taken as read).
>o you tbink it just that your correspondent
should seek to set the minds of your readers
against the slaughtermen without stating their
case in the slightest degree ? Was there a definite
rate fixed for these slaughtermen by the Wages
Board as stated ? Was your scribe aware that
the majority of the men were brought over from
Maori land for the new freezing works of Angliss
and Co. to slaughter lambs for export; that they
were assured of immediate work on arrival; that
they had to wait nearly two months for employ-
ment ; that they asked the extra rates until
Christmas, to make up for their lost time, and
were refused ? Seeing that they held a good
hand, and were being shown no consideration,
they resolved to demand the increase until further
notice. Now, with regard to the large wages
said to be earned. Do you not think a man earns
threepence for killing, skinning, and partially
dressing a lamb? And if he has attained that
degree of skill to be able to treat 100 lambs in one
day, don’t you think he earns 25s ? Perhaps, dear
sir, it was your printer’s fault that the article
appeared (more severe language here omitted).
Yours (rather vigorous expression taken as
read), 0.

Queensland has started an Immigration
League of its own. The secretary is a man who
speaks of England as ‘ ‘ home. ’ ’ Also the concern is
backed up by the Mail, which believes a good
deal in imports to keep the Australian artisan out
of work, and is solid for the preservation of the
great estates to hinder land settlement, _ and is
anxious that the Kanaka should be retained to
keep the white immigrant out of a job.
“ Poor Hawk ” : Died at Bourke (N.S.W.)
on Saturday, 24th instant, old Mrs. Hathen, in
her 91st year. The old lady was mother of the
Stock Inspector at Bourke, and had been 52 years
on crutches, but had possession of all her faculties
till the end. Her husband had but one leg ; her
son, the Stock Inspector, has only one leg ; and
his son, an auctioneer, has the same misfortune.
Three cork legs and a pair of crutches in a straight
: line is surely a record. The horse was the cause
of the mishap in each instance.
It is indeed curious how many alleged
Single-Taxers are in Reid’s camp denouncing the
proposed graduated land-tax as confiscation.
Hirsch in Victoria, and Lonsdale, Johnson,
“Holy” Hindle, Longmuir and Joe Cook in
N.S.W., all profess to believe in heaping all the
taxation on land, even if they thereby confiscate
every penny of its value. They profess their
readiness to take 20s. in the £ from the land-
lord, and at the same time appeal for the land-
lord’s support by declaring a proposed tax varying
from nothing up to 4d. to be sheer robbery. Is
there any honesty left in the Anti-Sosh push ?
Seddon is dead. When his life came to
its sudden end, he had on the stocks a national
pensions scheme, a wholesale revision of the local
government system, a reciprocity treaty with the
Commonwealth, and a revision of the tariff on
free-breakfast-table lines. Now they have all
been abandoned. The reciprocity treaty was
rejected by all parties. The revision of the tariff
is an unmentionable subject, even the alleged
Freetrade farmers voting it dangerous. The
local government bodies will struggle along as
heretofore, and the national pensions scheme has
been declared by all parties in the House to be too
complicated for anyone but Seddon to explain and
organise. Even his system of managing the
House has been abandoned, and where Seddon
drove with the iron hand of the autocrat for 14
hours a day, Joe Ward coaxes along for about
eight—or did till Parliament closed.
“ J.C.L.” writes :
So “ F.Z.” (“ Inky Way,” B. 10/25/’O6) is of the opinion
that it is more costly to live in London than out here. I
am afraid he is getting into the luxurious habits of the
modern Londoner. I have lived on the same income in
London and in Australia, and find that I can purchase
more in London with it than I can here. The Englishmen
of to-day are so given up to luxury that they seem to
imagine themselves as so many gods, deposited on the
earth to be “buttered” and “ soft-soaped” by a set of
oily and grasping foreign hirelings. Greece and Rome in
their tottering days were as nothing compared with the
debauchery of modern London. (I am not Judding.)
While England dissipates at the altars of Bacchus and
Venus in London, astute Germany hovers around seeking
weak spots where she can throw out one of her
rapidly-extending tentacles. As the Daily Mail (one of
the few papers that is not sleeping to please the Kaiser)
recently pointed out, the escaped German clerks and
waiters in London, if formed into legions, could give the
armed populace of Smoke a very bad time of it. But
this kind 'of talk jarred on the Englishmen, and
consequently the mammoth circulation of this popu-
lar halfpenny began to waver a little, and its policy
had to be toned down to meet the wishes of the
“Hupper” and moneyed classes. Even the thirty odd
millions forming the lower class, or “no class,” are
rather tired of being told that England is going to the
dogs, which merely provfes that nothing will stop this
nation hastening dogwards. With an empire on which
the sun never sets, and the Monkey warriors of the East
as allies, the English are deluding themselves with the
idea that the earth’s surface will never change its color
except to take on a little more red. Now lulled by the
sweet Empire refrains of German bands, she, sheep-like,
awaits the day when larger bands of more warlike
Teutons will swarm over the empire. What a fine setting
Australia would make to the imperial German crown !
A cry from the gallery :—-
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly has lost much of its
attractions for the man who climbs to the gallery. The
big “ draws ” are gone. Even Harry Levien smoulders.
Ashton has taken the character of a churn, Hogue of a
coffin, and Carruthers of a Christmas present for a good
boy. Crick, Norton, Willis, and Meagher are engaged
elsewhere, and the old type of burly fire-eater, such as
Copeland and Fletcher, appears extinct. The House is
tolerably well-behaved, but the good behavior seems the
result of want of animal spirits or strong convictions.
Anyway, the Chamber in Macquarie-street seldom showed
such an uninteresting collection of mediocrities. As a
Parliament, it could go out of Australian life to-morrow
and never come back, and history would take no note of
the day it left.
One of the things that help to make up
the so-called “Federal extravagance” is the
ghastly extortion of certain States as the price of
carrying the Federal mails over State-owned rail-
ways. In several places, though there is a railway
at hand, the Australian Government sends its
mails by coach simply because it is cheaper to
do that than use the railways of a soured parochial
Government. As a side issue, Tasmania has
always clamored for, and has got, a swift mail
service across the Straits from Melbourne, but it
has taken five years to persuade Tassy to fit in its
railway service with the mail steamers. All that
time the breathless steamer, running under
Federal contract, nearly burst its boiler to get the
mails in early, and then they often lingered for
hours at Launceston before the State train con-
decended to move.

“Dux”: The thick atmosphere of Grey-
mouth (M.L.) courthouse was rendered somewhat
denser one day last week when a Chow came up
on a charge of running a pak-a-pu den. The
case was a clear one, and the interpreter—another
Chow —smiled affably as he imparted the intelli-
gence to Hoo Fan that the finding of the court
was “Guilty,” and the fine “Ten pounds, and
interpreter’s fee, 10s.” Hoo Fan spoke a spell,
and the interpreter, still smiling, informed the
P.M. that the prisoner desired time to pay the
fine. He also volunteered “Hoo Fan, he goot
fella; he pay allittee.” “Very well,” said the
beak; “the court gives Hoo Fan one month to
pay the fine and interpreter’s fee.” And then
there arose the cry of a strong translator in his
agony : “No savee, boss, no liavee, no catchee;
he owee you ten poundee, allittee; he owee me
ten shillin’, no ! Payee me now.” But the court
adhered to its decision, and the interpreter went
out and wept horribly.
Considerable interest is being shown in the
inventions of Mr. Miller Reece Hutchinson, an agency
for which has lately been opened in Sydney. Briefly
put: One instrument, called the “Massacon,” conveys
vibrations to the ear, and in so doing massages and
reinvigorates the affected parts. The remarkable results
from this hold out great hopes to all those afflicted
with deafness. The other instrument, the “ Acous-
ticon,” is undoubtedly the most powerful sound inten-
sifier hitherto invented. This is now being used in
various churches, theatres, concerts, etc., and its
installation in the Arbitration Court here, last week, was
very successful, and caused much comment. These
inventions are now on view at the offices of the “ Acoustic
Patents, Ltd.,” in the Equitable Buildings, Sydney and
Melbourne.*

In the N.S. Wales holdings of from one t°
30 acres, 263 out of every 1000 acres are under culti-
vation. In the holdings of from 30 to 400 acres
145 out of every 1000 acres are cultivated. But
in the holdings of over 1000 acres, only 28 acres
Out of every thousand are cultivated. If the big
holdings (over 1000 acres) were cultivated to the
same extent as the small holdings, 9,560,000 acres
Would be under crop, instead of 1,018,243 acres.
That is to say, if the big holdings (over 1000 acres)
in N.S. Wales Were forced by a land-tax into the
same degree of cultivation as the small hold-
ings, there would be another 8,542,000 acres
of cultivation —room for 30,000 farms of close upon
300 acres each, with over 700 acres adjoining each
farm for grazing. And still, when there is men-
tion of a half-dozen farm-immigrants arriving, the
Lands Department is thrown into consternation;
and a desperate Government hires a Commis-
sioner to fight his way into such places as the
Pillaga scrub to find out if anything more human
than a wombat could make a living there, suppos-
ing the Government were prepared to subsidise
him liberally.
In the Nineteenth Century Lionel Giles
translates a Chinese proclamation from the famous
viceroy, Chang-chi-tung, now circulating through-
out the Hunan province. Some extracts :
The military strength of the Chinese is good in quality
as it is vast in quantity. The great drawback is that
people will not give their whole minds to the subject.
For, out of regard to the common saying that “ no son
of Han will go a-soldiering,” there are a great many who
refuse to pay serious attention to military matters ; and
thus it is that the strength of China is decaying day by
day. It follows that we must now begin to occupy our-
selves with the subject in real earnest—forming volunteer
corps, practising boxing, single-sticks, musketry and
shooting with heavy artillery, entering' the standing
army to follow a soldier’s profession, joining military
colleges to study and practise the art of war. Every
citizen must have military training.
It is on the edge of that volcano that “ Defence ”
Minister Playford totters with senile chatter
about Australia being well enough defended with
its present army of 15,000 or so and its proposed
gun-barrel factory.
Had George Turner* retired from public
hfe when the Deakin Ministry went under in
1904 . h « loss , to the Political life of Australia
would have been more keenly felt than it is to-
day He was llot by any nieans an C) ld Parlia-
mentarian—he was only 13 years in active politics
when he became Treasurer of Australia—but he
early gripped the confidence of the public with a
pair of strong hands, and came to be taken as a
Parliamentary necessity. His alliance with Reid
in 1904 proved the old truism, that no man is in-
dispensable, and George to-day shuffles off the
stage without a tear.

The property of Messrs.

40,835
acres
At TOOGOOLAWAH RAILWAY STATION,

36 RICH DAIRY and AGRICULTURAL
FARMS
(60 to 236 Acres) to be sold by Auction at the
Alexandra Hall, Toogoolawah, Saturday, Dec. 15,’06, at 3 o’clock
Mr. John Forrest, Managing Director of Moreheads Ltd., one of the leading Experts
in Stock and Station Business, writes after inspecting:—“l have no hesitation in saying that as
Dairying Country combined with Agricultural and Fattening, Cressbrook has no superior
in Queensland, in fact, acre for acre, I do not know where it can be equalled.”
Mr. John Cameron, M.L.A., one of the highest authorities on value, writes During the
last 3 or 4 years I have inspected and valued all the largest Freehold Estates in the Upper Brisbane
River District, and X consider the Cressbrook Estate is by far the most valuable
of them all.”
Mr. E. A. Pickering, the widely known judge of Dairy Stock, who has settled on the
Estate, writes on 2nd November, 1906 “I consider this Estate to be one of the finest freehold pro-
perties in Australia, comprising the very best agricultural, lucerne, lamb-fattening and dairying land.”
THE CRESSBROOK ESTATE
Consists of Rich Alluvial River Flats with Black and Chocolate Soil (workable under any climatic
conditions) and.easy sloping agricultural ridges, none of which, in the portion for sale, exceed 100 feet
in height. It is magnificently watered by the Upper Brisbane River and numerous creeks
and lagoons, and not a single hoof was lost in the great 1902 drought.

0
An election leads strange creatures to put
their heads out of their holes and beckon with a
friendly claw for public support. Edward Horne
Wittenoom, who, as Westralian Minister for
Mines, issued the “ 10ft. drop” regulation, has
crawled into the light as a candidate for the
Senate. The 10ft. regulation was that which
limited sinking for alluvial on mining leases to the
depth named, and it caused the demonstration
that nearly amounted to a revolt, and which
resulted in John Forrest getting prodded in the
tail coat with his private secretary’s brolly. In
those days “ 10ft. Ned,” as Wittenoom was called,
was so fearful that the Kalgoorlie rebels would
take him out of his bed and hang him that he had a
guard of police on night duty at his house in Perth.
At Geraldton, lately, Wittenoom “ opened his
compaign” with bleatings like this : “There
ought to be a Westralian party to look after
the interests of the State, as a whole. He was
always opposed to Federation, and would help
the movement to withdraw from the Union.
Those who had time and money should come for-
ward and serve the country. He had no definite
policy, and was uncertain what course to follow,
as the Senate was only a House of Revision.” And
that is the best a man “with time and money”
can do. Wittenoom doesn’t even know that the
Senate is something different from a State Upper
House, or legislative chucker-out, and calls it a
House of Revision. And apparently he- can’t
realise that he is himself an Object of Derision.
That good andreliablecondiment, “Montserrat”
got an undeserved blow beneath the belt recently,
' v^ n e " a fr ? ni ° ld st , ock a bott ’ e containing a
minute quantity of salicylic acid preservative. When the
Australian Parliament forbade the addition of preserva-
tives to articles of food the Montserrat people made up
their sauce, the basis of which is Montserrat Lime Fruit
J ui(, e, without adding any of the forbidden substance
whatever, and experience has proved that the lime fruit
juice is quite sufficient preservative to keep the sauce
good for a very lon „ time inde ed. Previous to the passing
of this legislation the manufacturers used to add to each
bottle a very minute quantity of salicylic acid, and there
may be stiil a few of these bottles stored on grocers’
shelves. But all the sauce made since the passing of the
Act is free from preservatives of any sort.*
Thus YVobinson, of Wannon (Vic.), on
Nov. 20
He had believed in Freetrade for a number of years,
but he was willing to make such alterations in the tariff
as were proved to be necessary to save the life of any
industry.
No Protectionist will be gulled by that. Australia
needs an industrial birthrate —it doesn’t merely
need to keen life in the few industries it has.
This is the position of the party which
denounces the proposal to impose a Federal
graduated land-tax as a means towards breaking
up for closer settlement the estates of those_ great
landholders who block the increase of population: —
(1) Closer settlement is a good thing because we want
population.
(2) Therefore, anybody who blocks settlement is bad.
(3) All the same, we shouldn’t do anything to him.

'T'HE great popularity of both the Accordeon and the Mouth Organ has induced us to import the very latest combination
of these two instruments. Anyone accustomed to the Accordeon. as well as those who perform upon the Mouth Organ, will
find the Clarionette Accordeon easier to play than either, and the harmony it produces is admitted by qualified musicians to
be much more effective and sympathetic than that of any other wind instrument. The Clarionette Accordeon enables the
performer to play the air clearly and with good emphasis, while the accompaniment—alto, tenor and bass—can be effectively
introduced without drowning the tun®. Persons unable to play any other instrument will have no difficulty with the Clario-
nette Accordeon, the special Book of Popular Tunes that we present with each instrument being so clearly arranged thatno
knowledge of music is required. Anyone able to read ordinary print (not inusie) can play the tunes at sight without the aid
of a teacher. The player has merely to press the keys as indicated by the very simple system of numbers, at the same time
supplying the wind through the mouthpiece, and the music is produced—soprano, alto, tenor and bass complete—with a
volume of melody resembling a small orchestra. In order to speedily introduce this novel instrument, we have decided to
sell a limited number at the wonderfully low price ot 9/6 each, carriage paid and packed in a neat box. and with each
Clarionette Accordeon we will present the Book of Popular Tunes complete with words. Send P. 0.0, or Postal Notes to

Managing: Director
J. E. SCANTLEBURY.
Old “Jarrah Jack” Forrest appears to
have gone must since his return to Westralia, and
is stampeding about the country, trampling on
friends, enemies, and what stands tor his prin-
ciples alike. In one wild and tearing speech he
formulated the doctrine that no member was of
much use to the State unless he had a big bank
account. The sort ot man that was needed was
he who had “independent means.” And Wes-
tralia at the statement, for the very
type of man Forrest was barracking for —Alec
jjatheson—had, by his absence from the Senate
(he was away getting measured for a lot of new
clothes, or something like that) caused the loss
of the Transcontinental Railway Survey Bill. If
he hadn’t so much independent means to buy new
clothes with he might have been at his post, and
his vote would have saved the Bill. What has
Deakin to say about these gambolings on the
part of his elephant ?
Wild horses can’t drag from old, fat land-
lord Gibb, Bill Lyne’s opponent at the Hume, a
statement, as to which party he belongs to. This
is how the Foreign-trade paper report always
runs
In replying to this question, Mr. Gibb aroused the
sympathy and applause of a great majority of the
audience. He said that he would not be bound to any
leader or party. He had never been elected as the
nominee of any' party', and would not be now. If the
party chose to select him, that was not his responsibility.
He had always been elected as an independent candidate,
and would continue on those lines to the end.
This is all very beautiful, but if big landlord Gibb
gets in he must sit in the vicinity of Reid, or
Deakin, or Watson, or else he must sit on the
floor. Is large, rich shuffler Gibb going to sit on
the floor ?

ONE of the sights of London, and one you should not miss, is the magnificent six-story building just erected by STEWART DAWSON & CO.
for the better reception of the many visitors to London from Australia. It is miles ahead of any building anywhere devoted to the Jewellery
business. Fireproof throughout, it has superb Shipping Offices for the prompt despatch to our Australian and New Zealand Stores of all the new
things as they appear. There are Three Showrooms, Home Offices, Reception Rooms, Two Electric Lifts, Strong Rooms, and last, but by no means

PAIN.
Death is hastened by ignorance. Nature has endowed us with the sense of pain solely in order
that, by seeking relief, we may avoid playing into the hands of Death. When we feel pain we are out
of health. Pain long endured is a strain upon the system which Nature cannot withstand. Many of
us, however, go on suffering certain kinds of pain, just as if Science could not come to the help of her
sister Nature. There is, for instance, no pecessity for anyone to suffer from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
A nee mi a
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
The pains caused by this long list of disorders are but Nature’s means of letting us know that the
kidneys or liver are for some reason unable to perform their work efficiently, for all of these
disorders are produced by the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons, which would be
thrown off naturally if the kidneys and liver were doing their duty. Few people realise how import-
ant in the scheme of life is the healthy action of the kidneys and liver, therefore the following
description of the functions of those vital organs will be of the deepest interest to many.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proporrion
of the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition of
the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if particles
float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy and no time must be lost
in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful
jllness will result.
The Liver. —ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood, converts
it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood gradually, as the
latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is com-
pletely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their life and are use-
ful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness,
Anaemia, Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected and the liver remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research directed specially to diseases of the kidneys and
liver was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary to find a curative agent which
would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these organs being so immediately associated
in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and after many disappointments the medicine
which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was.at length discovered. Warner’s Safe
Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver, and, by restoring their activity, these vital
organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poisons,
the presence of which, in the system, are the cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago,
Backache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache,
Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles and General Debility. Warner’s-Safe Cure cures all these disorders
simply by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the reason why cures effected by Warner’s Safe
Cure are permanent cures. [7l

Charleville (Q.) is making a first venture in beef
export, to Siberia. Frozen bullocks to the number of 136
have been sent away to Vladivostok as an experiment.

This letter to the Age marks the extreme of
genteel sweating: “I am a bank clerk, with stainless
record, age 29, salary £l2O per annum. My employers
forbid me to marry until my salary reaches £l5O. With
luck, if I behave myself, it will be eight years before that
comes to pass.”

“Val”: Re cotton grown by Dr. Thomatis, of
North Queensland (B. 25/10/’O6), authorities do not allow
the product all the virtues claimed for it. A recent
letter from the secretary of the British Cotton-growers’
Association stated that Caravonica was “too rough.”
On the other hand, he said that a hybrid cotton, dis-
covered in a cotton field near Brisbane, was much
preferable, and was worth 12d. per lb. Latter is said to
be remarkable cotton, and great hopes have been built on
it. A correspondent of a southern paper, writing from
London about the Cairns cotton sent to England recently,
said it was of “a harsh, rough character,” valued at Bd.
to 9d.
* * * *
The present high price of copper has led to the
discovery of a rich vein of the metal in the backyards of
Sydney and Melbourne, where the careful housewife now
has to chain her boiler to the frame or otherwise glue it
to the premises, lest it disappear unaccountably in the
night. The übiquitous prospector has also turned his
attention to the telegraph and railway wires ; the other
night about a mile of signal wire, near Essendon (Vic.)
station, was calmly cut away and removed. What with
sheet-lead annexers from roofs, copper prospectors, shop-
lifters, pocket-exploiters, etc., hardly any article of value
can be trusted to remain where it is left for five minutes
unless it is chained down—and even then it is 10 to 1 the
discoverer will steal the chain.

“S.R.”: Notice that some Melbourne ice-
cream contains from 10,000,000 to 40,000,000 bacteria per
cubic centimetre. In unsewered Brisbane some of the
ice-cream vended by the peripatetic Giovanni comes out
of places so dirty that I don’t believe a self-respecting
microbe would be found there dead. One Giovanni was
accused the other day of making use of the salt droppings
from the hides in certain pelt warehouses. The store-
men, it appears, were in the habit of sweeping the floor
rubbish out into the gutter ; and as a good percentage of
this consisted of salt shaken off hides, Iceacreami's salt
bill was just about as much as the numerousness of
snakes in Iceland. Iceacreami swore, etc., etc., etc. ;
but he was fined. I forget how much he was fined ; but
I think it was 3d.

“ Remo” : I don’t know anything about Maori-
land’s pounds of butter that weigh 14foz. (B. 8/11/'O6), but
I do know something about Australia’s yards that only
measure 35in. I worked in one store for two years before
I found out that its yards measured 2ft. llin. The
sudden activity of the local inspector of weights and
measures caused new measures to be affixed to the coun-
ters. I wen. as a raw kid to the rag trade, and this was
how the corruption of my morals began : “ When mea-
suring off yards of any soft, stretching cloth, such as
flannel, etc., keep taut between the hands, but measure
off smartly, so that they won’t notice the stretching.”
In this way a competent assistant cribs many yards of
stuff per day for his boss. My first boss was a liberal
man, though. He said, “ Always give the customer an
extra inch or so over—and be sure she notices it.” A
Commerce Act! By heavens, Australia needed it badly
—worse than the drinky monk in “ Falka ” wanted the
contents of the jar.
» * * *
“Laura M.” : A couple of months in a time-
payment shcp is an experience. I found that the bulk of
the t.p. army consists of anxious-looking mothers with
big families, which, as they explain with a sigh, are
always “ wanting something.” Some of them, when asked
the Christian names of their husbands, pronounced “ Percy
Frederick” with conscious pride, while others, whose
husbands were John or Bill, deeply resented the question.
“Husband’s occupation, madam?” produced the same
varied result. A" greengrocer’s wife was invariably
ashamed, while a wharf laborer’s missus gloried in her
boss’s daily graft. The lady who must replace a lost
wedding-ring as quickly as possible, and the man who
had promised the partner of his bosom a birthday
present, but had spent the cash on a hilarious burst, both
seemed to come hot-foot to our establishment at the
critical moment. The overdressed lady, the smart shop
girl and the civil servant were instantaneously branded as
bad marks ; in fact, the t.p. tabernacle is the only place
where the poor but honest are really welcomed. We
were invited one Tuesday by a whiskered clairvoyant to
supply him with a wedding suit for the following Friday,
and when informed that we couldn’t oblige him, he
wrathfully accused us of interfering with his matrimonial
plans, as the wadding would have to be postponed.
Among our clients we mustered two clergymen, one
artificial limb maker, three actors, several policemen and
tram-guards, many boardinghouse-keepers, one monu-
mental mason, and a bejew'elled, peroxided lady, whom
all the girls regarded with the eye of dark suspicion.
The overtures of a once famous politician, who is also
connected with the Bar, were quietly but firmly declined
the day before I stepped out from the business.
READY MADES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All day she works at the sewing machine in the factory
opposite,
Where the staring, blindless windows gape in the glow of
the summer light;
And all day long come the clang and whirr from the
dusty, sun-flecked room,
Looking on to a narrow city lane, where the tall, black
chimneys loom.
Her pale face bending above her work ; thin hands on the
fabrics laid;
From the morning bell to the closing bell she toils at the
“ ready made.”
A sparrow hopping athwart the sill glances in at the long,
bare room,
And it brings a breath from the city parks, where the
jacarandas b oom.
Up from the street, on the other side, comes the lilt of a
passing band,
But her eyes are fixed on the level seams that slide from
, beneath her hand.
In the heat of the summer days she works her treadmill
from morn till night,
Behind the blindless window-panes in the factory op-
posite.
She sees the line of the pale-blue hills and the river, like
sheeted glass,
Hears the cattle tramp to the branding yards all over the
wild-oat grass ;
And she sees the harebells about the plains and the wind-
mill on the rise,
The long, green sweep of the tasselled corn that is grate-
ful to aching eyes ;
She sees the sheep in the wool-shed pens and the myalls
about the ridge,
And she hears the swish of the brown flood-tides race
under the swinging bridge ;
And she sees the gate by the ruined yard where young
Love and Laughter met,
While the night wind scattered the honey sweets of the
fragrant mignonette ;
She sees the jasmine’s petalled stars and the white of the
ti-tree flower,
And she rocks afloat in the cedar boat for one still
delicious hour.
She sees the skies that are far and clear as the eyes of a
dreamer’s dream,
All the while her needle flies in and out over the hard,
white seam.
Her hair is faded, her face is thin, her hands are gaunt 1
and white,
And she works the treadle at “ready mades” in the
factory opposite.
QUEENSLAND.
