The London papers record that after re-
peated applications extending over the last 40
years, James Collyer, who went through the
Crimean War and the .Indian Mutiny, has been
granted a pension of ninepence a day. Sydney
had just such a case in another Crimean man, an
ex-captain of the Royal Engineers, retired with
over 20 years service. The poor old fellow was
finally granted what they called a “Compassionate
Allowance ” of a half-penny per day for each
year’s service, or tenpence per day in all.
“ Pro Patria ” writes :
Whenever I suffer again with insomnia I shall hie me to
hear Josiah Symon hold forth on Federal politics. His
speech in the Adelaide Town Hall last week was all
drowsy twaddle, and most lame and impotent. This
Foreign-trader is a feeble platform speaker, despite his
undoubted genius and power as a forensic orator. It was
the tamest political meeting I ever attended. The sparse
audience, mostly comprised of the legal partners and
many clerks of Sighmoan’s big law firm, was listless and
bored. If this condition were not actuated, it was
certainly aggravated, by Symon’s protracted rasp.
Jawdge Reid’s ablest lieutenant attributed all the
imaginary ills of the Commonwealth to the “ diaboli-
cal evils of High Protection." His outrageous state-
ment that the proposed Federal graduated land-tax
and the compulsory repurchase scheme were “gross rob-
bery by violence ” was worthy only of the Father of Lies
or the Mother of Ignorance. The statement shows that
the bald-headed knight—Cook and Reid are bald, too —is
a very incompetent constitutional lawyer. Symon uttered
a few patriotic sentiments—green places in a wilderness
of wail—but they were very far apart. It saddened one
to think that within actual range of the. sound of Sigh-
moan’s raucous voice there lay, sick almost unto death,
on a bed in his Eagle Chambers’ office, the greatest Aus-
tralian of his time—C. O. Kingston.

SAVE
YOUR
The condition of the stomach bears the same relation to
one’s health that the foundation does to the house.
Without a good foundation, the house cannot stand.
Without a good stomach, the health must fail.
Digestion will make and indigestion will break the
STOMACH
walls of the stomach. Perfect digestion
will build and sustain, while poor digestion
will tear down health’s foundation. The
reason for this is that the stomach must receive and prepare all food for entrance
into the blood, and from the blood into the tissues. If the stomach is not in
perfect condition, the nourishment will be improperly prepared—imperfectly di-
gested, and, instead of the health-giving and strength-increasing properties of the
food being contributed to the tissues, stagnation ensues, the blood becomes impover-
ished and poison is forced into the system.
The weakest organ of the body gets most of this poison, because, for want of
strength, it is less able to resist it, and, as a consequence, disease begins to show
itself first in one of the weak organs, which is usually a very important one. It
may be the lungs, liver, kidneys or heart, and, in the case of women, it may be on e
of the organs peculiar to their sex. Such symptoms as shortness of breath, coated
tongue, pains in the stomach, palpitation of the heart, fullness after eating, bowel
complaint, ulcers in the mouth, sallow complexion, constipation, diarrhoea,
dysentery and sour belching are all symptoms of indigestion.
DR. SHELDON’S DIGESTIVE TABULES relieve every symptom of
indigestion and restore the stomach and digestive organs to perfect health. This
famous remedy has effected a permanent cure in thousands of cases of chronic
dyspepsia as well as ulceration of the stomach and other serious stomach diseases.
DR. SHELDON’S DIGESTIVE TABULES represent the natural juices
of digestion as they exist
in a healthy stomach
combined with tonic and
DR. SHELDON'S
reconstructive properties. They are
absolutely harmless, perfectly palatable,
never doing harm, always doing good.

Brown Brother.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN these South Seas there dwells at ease
(Or easy dwelt at one time)
A simple man, of color tan,
Who deems his days all fun-time,
This earth is no drab vale of woe
To him, nor place of pining ;
How should it be to folk who see
A glad sun always shining ?
Prone in repose, his bootless toes
Are mostly skyward pointing ;
While, by his side, a blushless bride
His bare skin is anointing.
(As I have said, just overhead,
His ways are free and easy ;
And such a thing is comforting.
Although a trifle greasy.)
His fertile land is undermanned,
And yields him facile living,
And festive times; in tropic climes
Dame Nature is all-giving.
He need not cheat that he may eat,
Nor cringe and crawl for money ;
Right from his birth, his life is mirth,
Myrrh, frankincense and honey.
He owns his soil, he knows not toil,
Unless we white men find him ;
Is placid in his “ state of sin,”
No narrow tenets bind him.
He cannot tell the way to hell
Till kindly Christians show him.
Simple and bland I called him, and
I called him as I know him.
He pays no toll to save his soul
To bishop, priest, or deacon ;
But looks for light at death’s dark night,
To some old heathen beacon
That sheds a gleam upon the stream
Which drifts him to Bulotu —
That Promised Land, whose spirit strand
The brown departed go to.
Nor need we groan, and, mournful, moan
That such a soul must perish ;
His does not seem much more a dream
Than that we fondly cherish.
We grope in space, and vaguely place
Our heaven in some high land;
Brown brother he hunts through the sea
To raise a mirage island.
His final aim seems much the same
As ours when thus we view him ;
And ’tis our greed, and not his need,
That takes our torn faiths to him.
It gives one pain ; he thrives amain
While his own gods advise him,
But withers when our mission men
Set out to civilise him.
My umber pal, most pastoral,
Recumbent, dreamy chappie !
’Tis good to see him, pagan, free,
For, heathen, he is happy.
He lazes, laughs, his kava quaffs,
A dormant foe to bustle ;
His home a green, idyllic scene,
His lullaby palm-rustle.
Ah, lucky Jim ! And well for him
If we will only leave him
To lounge and snore, as heretofore,
Our strivings merely grieve him.
Oh, let him rest! Are they not blest
Who undisturbed can slumber ?
And let him keep his Isles of Sleep—
They are so few in number.
N.S.W.
TALAFEKAU.
The Babe of Elysium.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FIVE of us had a place out in Paradise
Road, Waverley, and we’d struck trouble
with housekeepers. We’d had three in
five weeks, and the one we had was leaving next
morning, and we’d no one to take her place. It
was a bad position. We held a meeting of the
Elvsium Bachelor Company in the smoking room
at 3 o’clock Sunday afternoon to decide whether we
should wind up the concern and go back to hash-
towers or not. The president of the company
summarised the situation, reported what efforts
he had made to secure a competent housekeeper,
and wound up with :
“ And now it remains with honorable bloques
to say what they are going to do. I’m sick of the
whole show myself, but ever so much more bilious
at the prospect of going back to the hash-towers.
If honorable bloques decide in favor of hash-
towers we had better all go together, and see if we
can’t boss the roost. When honorable bloques
have discussed the situation I will put the
question, and ”
A small, faint knock on the front door inter-
rupted him. The president glanced at his watch
with a scared expression, and then, reassured,
went to the door. We heard a woman’s voice say :
“ Egscuse me cornin’ roun’ on Sunday afternoon,
but I heard yous wanted a housekeeper, an’ I
thought I’d come round in case you wasn’t suited.
I want the work real badly ! ”.
The president paused so long, that we suggested
loudly that he should bring her in. She was, so
to speak, our absolutely last throw for retaining
Elysium. Well, in she came; tall, well-made,
with gracefully sloping shoulders ; but her dress
just shrieked poverty—poverty dire and awful.
Her face was pinched and her eyes hollow—filled
with the pathetic hunted look of a kangaroo at
bay. The president explained the duties in a
sonorous rolling voice that would have been worth
a hundred quid to the man who read the Declara-
tion of Independence. We pointed out that the
man at South Head was not interested. The
president stammered, paused, and then collapsed ;
but, recovering immediately, asked for references.
She passed over a couple of dirty envelopes
from which the president extracted a pair of very
creased references. He looked at the first and
then at the girl.
“Jeanne Loader, eh ! Were you not in service
with Mrs. Arlington?”
The girl’s face went ghastly, the hunted look
became intensified, she nodded admission.
“Hum ! Where’s your baby now? Dead?”
The girl reeled against the wall. Little Joe,
the Jew, swore softly. Rice, the jeweller, looked
up with a glance of keen, critical interest.
Morley, the draughtsman, snapped out:
“What the devil’s that got to do with us ?”
“ I think we are entitled to know ! ” snapped
the president.
“ Me baby’s at home where I board, an’ that’s
why I’m here. I want a place so that I can look
after me baby properly. I’ve bin round heaps of
places, but they all want a girl to work fer
nothing, or at most half-a-crown a week, when
she has a baby. But I must have a place with
decent wages, ’cause it’s a bottle baby, and I must
have money to buy it food.”
“ Of course you must,” said Rice.
“ What infernal wretches women are to sweat a
girl with a child ! ” snorted Morley.
“They ought to be hung by the heels till
dead,” hissed the president, in a terrible voice.
“You can start right away, this afternoon if
you like, and get an advance against wages,” said
Joe, the practical Jew.
The girl buried her face in her hands.
“We pay 15s. per week, and allow ss. per week
for outside assistance with washing and cleaning.
If you like to do the work yourself you can
have the whole pound, and the ss. will enable you
to get a little girl to nurse the baby. You’d
better have it here with you.” Then turning to
the shareholders, he remarked, “The Boss of the
Children’s Department told me they never thrived
unless they were with the mother; and it seems a
bad mother’s better than a good nurse,” he went
on, as though fearing we might think him over-
generous.
“I’ll come in the morning,” said the girl with a
sob. Joseph, the Hebrew, pursued her to the
door with some money.
♦ * * *
Next evening we all came home in the ’bus
together, speculating what the kid would be like.
We were half afraid that it was a mistake to
allow her to have it with her; but Morley said
he thought it would be best, otherwise she would
be always running out to see it.
“Suppose it yells like Hades all night?” in-
quired the president.
“We can get some stuff from the chemist to
keep it quiet,” said Joe, with a benign expression
that took halt the Jewishness off the contour of
his nose.
“Besides, they only yell when they aren’t fed
properly. We must tell her not to bustle it with
its bottle. You see, they’ve got to be kept pro-
perly upended and fed slowly, so that as you pour
the milk into ’em the air comes out; otherwise,
they bubble, and that makes ’em yell. It’s all
quite simple.”
“You’requite wrong, Rice,” said Morley. “It
isn’t that at all. The trouble is that they don’t
feed ’em properly, on the right stuff, you know.
They think they want rich food —cream almost —
whereas it should be very much diluted. Plain
cowjuice gives ’em indigestion. We must tell
her to mix plenty of barley or lime water with
the milk. Perhaps we’d better go right on to the
chemist’s and get some for her.”
Twenty minutes later we all tramped into the
chemist’s shop, and demanded a large bottle of
barley water and a bottle of stuff to quieten a
baby when it yelled.
“And some lime water, too,” interjected Joe,
the Jew, from the back.
“And what about those things it sucks when
it’s asleep, orsomething. You know—comforters,
I think they call them,’’ burst in Morley.
The chemist regarded us in a dazed sort of
manner for a moment, and then he seemed to
grasp the situation, and waded right in. It was
half-an-hour before we got out of his shop, loaded
up with the following articles —I remember it
as though it was but yesterday :
3 assorted comforters.
3 assorted teething-rings.
1 bottle of soothing syrup.
1 tin violet powder.
1 tin Fuller’s earth.
1 jar vaseline, white.
X box Oastille soap.
1 puff box and puff.
3 tins Allenbury’s food.
2 Allenbury’s feeders.
1 Allenbury’s food measure.
1 copy “ The'Baby—How to Feed It.”
3 bottles malt extract for the mother, to keep her up.
1 bottle toilet vinegar to put in the baby’s bath,
guaranteed to make it sleep well at nights if properly
fed.
1 bottle aromatic cascara, a specific for “ bubbling,”
and guaranteed to materially help the baby to sleep well
at nights.
There were several other things which the
brigand behind the counter said were indispens-
able, but which he took back when he found we
hadn’t sufficient money to pay for them. As we
passed several pubs on the way home, we began
to regret that we had listened so attentively to
the voice of the chemist and to think that perhaps
we were a little too generous to the baby we had
not yet seen.
As we got near the house the president stopped
grumbling, and asked solemnly how a man with
only 50s. per week could bring up half-a-dozen
babies if he had to get all these things every week in
addition to paying ordinary household exes. We
ruminated over the ghastly problem till the presi-
dent opened the door and let us in, and there,
propped up in the midst of cushions on the dining-
room sofa, with a little girl of about 13 cooing to
it, was a tiny black-haired baby, dressed in a
white frock of very coarse muslin. Its forehead
was half its face, its little cheeks had a faint bulge
on them, its mouth was a little pink bud near the
bottom of its face, and it looked up at us with
wide, deep eyes, as clear, serene and beautiful as
the sleeping, moonlit ocean; then it smiled
and waved tiny, russet-tinted hands and feet
at us. We stood round it in a ring, and watched
Rice open negotiations with it by saying “ Goo ! ”
and holding out a dark-brown finger, like a dead
ti-tree root. It smiled again, grabbed the finger,
and tried to cram it into its mouth. Then the
startled mother rushed in, and, snatching it up
with many apologies, hurried it out into the back
regions.
In another moment she was back, without the
baby, inquiring would she serve dinner.
The president waved a hand towards the pack-
age on the table with an imperial sort of gesture.
“We brought those few things home for the
baby. We tried to get some barley water, and
the lying dog, who happened to be out of it, said
it was made fresh every day at home. Anyhow,
we got everything he had that was useful.”
• * * *
Well, that baby was soon at home. We used to
watch it being fed, and occasionally we used to
nurse it. We observed that the mother knew all
about it being kept upright, and, after she ex-
plained barley water to us, we suddenly stopped
giving her advice about how to look after it. In
quite a little while we ceased to dread hearing it
cry, and informed the president that a peculiar
kind of sneeze with which he was afflicted was
much worse than the cry of the babe. He ignored
the remarks about the sneeze, but stretched him-
self out and delivered a short lecture on the why
and the wherefore of a baby’s yelling :
“I’ve found out,” he remarked, “that this kid
uses its yells instead of speech. When she wants
anything, or wishes to attract attention, she just
yells. She has to, being unable to speak, and she
goes on yelling till whatever is wrong is righted.
It is a most excellent, thoughtful provision of
Nature. If the kid were to ask in ordinary
speech, its requests would be ignored, or it would
be told to wait, or, worse still, it wouldn’t know
what to tell. But the yell is compulsory, and it
goes on yelling till you have found out exactly
what is wrong. The kid, you see, is only aware
that something is wrong, and leaves it to experi-
ment on the part of the attendant to locate the
trouble. Wonderful ! ”
“ That’s so,” said Rice. “Whenever she wants
to be held upright, while lam nursing her, she
just yelps, and immediately she gets changed to
the position she desires, she smiles and says
‘ goo ! ’ Cunning little devil, eh ! ”
“ The thing I can’t understand is how the deuce
she makes all that new tissue and bone and long
black hair out of thin milk and barley-water, and
at such a rate, too,” said Joe, the Jew, thought-
fully. “ You j ust pour it into her, three egg-cups
full at a time of each, and a spoonful of lime-
water, and she grows like fury. She’s doubled
her length and bulk since she has been here.
Marvellous, blanky well marvellous ! ”
But Joe himself admitted that was not nearly
so amazing as the awful lullaby she inspired Rice
to compose. We caught him humming it to her
one morning while he walked her up and down
the balcony. I can remember it quite well, for
I’ve sung it to myself all these years every time I
thought of the black-headed baby :
Oh, the baby bunting on the Austral strand !
Oh, the baby bunting, she’s got out of hand ;
And the little baby dear
Is out upon the beer.
As we go marching through Georgia.
And while we talked and frivolled, the little
black-headed baby went on growing, to Joe’s
increasing amazement, till over two years had
passed away. Then we held another meeting of
the Elysium Bachelor Co. And a sad meeting it
was, too. The end of Elysium had come. The
black-headed baby sat on a rug by the ferns in
the fireplace of the new Elysium at Woollahra
Point, playing with a doll old Rice had brought
home to her the night before. We were sadly
perplexed, for we didn’t know what to do with
her, and she had a curl round every one of our
hearts.
“I thought we might appoint a treasurer, and
send along a few bob each week for her keep and
education. She’s got no hope in the world if we
don't provide for her !” said the president, sadly,
and with a catch in his throat.
Rice bent down and caught her in his arms.
She nestled up against him as though she had all
the right in the world to do so.
“ It’s such a complete scatter,” said Joe. “All
but two going away from Sydney, and Rice going
to start a place of his own for his mother. I’m to
be married in three months also, and I’m sure my
wife would raise Cain and Beelzebub if I took her
home. I’ve a good mind not to get married,
darned if I haven’t! ”
Rice smiled.
“ Don’t worry, boys; I’m going'to adopt Effie,
and when my mother sees her she will just love
her. She lost a baby girl about the same age as
Effie. Anyhow, I’ll have her adopted before the
aged parent arrives, and the position will just
have to be accepted ”
“ What about the mother ? ”
“She’ll be all right—she can always earn a
good living, and it is better she and Effie should
lose sight of one another for a few years at any
rate. Later on—perhaps ! Who knows ? ”
And next day Elysium dissolved. Effie went
away with Rice to the new home that he had pre-
pared against his mother’s arrival. Effie’s mother,
red-eyed and broken-hearted, took up her quarters
in a city boarding-house till she could get another
place. It had been a terrible sacrifice, but she
had made it that Effie might be “ brought up as a
lady.”
* # * •
Well, about six months ago I began to think
very much about the little black-headed baby. I
had made a bit of money, and I thought I would
go back to Sydney and try and drop across the
little maid. She might not be too well off, for I
never had any idea how old Rice was fixed. He
was very close. I hunted round, but could not
get on his track. The firm he used to be with
had disappeared. 1 advertised, but never an
answer. Last night I was at the big theatre
down there, and I looked round the gallery first,
then the stalls, and then the circle, with my glass,
to see if I could see any sign of her as I pictured
her. But mostly I kept my eye open for old
Rice. He couldn’t change. Well, he wasn’t
there, so I just drifted out, and got on a boat for
a blow, and to think things over quietly. I went
round and round, getting off nowhere, just smok-
ing. Every time I came to the Sydney wharf a
deck-hand came along and said, “What’s the
game ? ”
At last I got up and wandered over the
fangway, and there, walking down to the
oat on the other side of the wharf, was
old Rice, with the loveliest young girl you
ever dreamed of on his arm. He was
browner, more gaunt and grim than ever.
But he was in evening dress, and the diamonds in
his shirt front twinkled like fragments of the
evening star. I walked up and touched him on
the shoulder:
“Put it right there, old man, and tell me
where’s the little ”
But he had gone. I saw him walk that girl
across the plank on to the boat, and disappear.
Then, all in a moment, he came back and nearly
wrung the hand off me.
“Do you know who that was with me?” he
asked.
‘ ‘ Of course, the little black-headed baby—she’s
a credit to you, old man. What took you off in
such a hurry? ”
“Well, it’s this way. I was afraid you would
ask about her mother. Just pretend you believe
her to be my daughter, and are meeting her for the
first time. Now come along home with us.”
“ Where’s your mother ? ”
“Ah, poor mother, she only lived about ten
years after she came to me. She was passionately
fond of Effie.”
“ But Effie’s mother—where is she ? ”
“Oh, of course ! O, she’s all right, curse her.
She’s our housekeeper. Effie has no domestic
problems, the housekeeper solves all of them, and
when there are no servants does all the work
herself. She is devoted to Effie, and the man
who marries Effie will get a splendid housekeeper
as well as the prettiest woman in all Australia for
a wife.”
“ And Effie —does she know ?”
“Never dreams of it—which explains my recent
conduct. I wanted to prime you. You’ll be dis-
creet, of course.”
“Dumb as an oyster. Of course, the mother
knows ? ”
“ She does—she does, worse luck ; and makes
me pay for the privilege of being thought by
Effie to be her father. If you could contrive to
take the old lady away and lose her you would
oblige me greatly. You see, she blackmails me
for keeping quiet. She blackmailed me to take
her as housekeeper when my mother died,
and she has blackmailed me ever since;
but, as she planks it all into the Savings
Bank for Effie some day, I don’t know that I
mind very much. Now, come along, and I’ll
introduce you to my daughter—put me away, and
I’ll drop you over the side.”
Well, I went across, and spent the evening
there. She’s a lovely girl, and as charming as
she is beautiful, but, somehow, she is not the
same. It doesn’t seem possible, you know. And
I feel sorry every time T look at the heart-hungry
mother who yearns to caress her daughter, the
daughter who doesn’t know her ; and I think old
Rice might have done worse than marry her. She
is much better-looking than he is, anyhow. They
want me to stay over there with them, but I
prefer to be by myself, and just go across to
dinner when I want to have another look at the
beautiful girl that the little black-headed baby’s
turned into. Somehow, I think I’ll hunt round
and see if I can’t find another little black-
headed baby girl all for myself.
C. A. JEFFRIES.

The Young Heart.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IF I lift the corner of the blind that is
carefully arranged to shade the light from
my old eyes, I can see the girls playing
tennis on the lawn.
They put me here in a comfortable chair with a
book and some fancy work, told me I looked a
charming old lady, and went out with their
racquets. Gwen said the doctor had ordered me
perfect rest and quiet, and that if I follow his
instructions, and don’t take too active an interest
in anything, I may live for years. Now, who
wants to live for years ?
It means being set down like a doll in a corner
and told to be good, while the people who are still
young and strong of body enjoy the light and
laughter and all the dear foolishnesses without
which life is—gracious Heaven ! —only a slow
mouldering to the grave !
I was always a frivolous girl, and they called
me butterfly, and they said that I would never
grow old. But I did, you see—at least my body
did, for my face is quite a network of wrinkles,
and I wear a lace cap—a perfect granny of a cap
—over my iron-grey hair.
Last week the girls went to the Show, and I
was taken in a cab and put in the grand stand,
with several old ladies of about my age, who
talked of their servants and the merits of their
clergymen. The girls went off with the men they
are engaged to, and Gwen came and told me
afterwards that they had been very flighty and
ridden on the merry-go-round. What would they
have said if I had expressed my wish—and I did
wish it, in spite of my years—for a ride in one of
those flying boats? I can picture the satirical
lift of Beth’s eyebrows, and the way in which
Gwen would ejaculate “Moth-er!” and the
astonishment of the old ladies.
I value my daughters’ good opinion, and I
didn’t want to shame them before their fiances, so
I held my peace ; but if they had known the
rebellious thoughts that rioted behind my lined
forehead—well, perhaps the young men, who are
very eligible, might have considered that I was
not the best companion for my own daughters,
since youth can do with grace what respectable
age must eschew.
And now, while they laugh and joke in the
tennis court, for they are jolly girls in a perfectly
conventional way, how little they realise that I
want to be there too, sitting on the damp grass if
need be, and being delightfully foolish, feeling
young again in body as well as in heart, while I
listen to their careless chatter !
I was not married very young, for all my butter-
fly ways. I was engaged several times, but I
always got bored, until I met Willie.
Dear, you are very still now away on that back-
block selection, where we struggled so long, and
your grave is level with the yellow grass plain,
and at night there is no sound about your bed
but the wind in the silky oaks, and the shrill cry
of a water-hen from the lilied swamp ; and you
never thought I was old, did you, Willie? “Sweet-
heart,” you said to me on our honeymoon, “you
are not very wise. I think you will never be any-
thing but a child !”
Two years afterwards how you laughed when I
held up my baby boy for you to see. “ Here is a
new toy for you to play with,” you said. But—
my baby died. And it was nearly seven years
before Gwen, the eldest of the girls, came. When
Beth, the youngest, was a year old I left the
selection, and the old days of striving with
drought and flood, behind me, and came to live in
town. And I left you behind, too.
Perhaps if my son had lived he might not have
made me sit in corners and take care of myself.
He might have been like Willie; he might have
understood.
The girls have had every advantage, and they
are going to be married next spring, and I am to
live with Gwen. They have never given me any
anxiety; they have always been perfectly well
behaved.
Willie and I were not! We did the most out-
rageous things sometimes—went camping out in
the scrub, and got bad colds, and nearly starved,
because we meant to live on the pigeons and scrub
turkey we shot, and we never managed to hit
any 1 And if it hadn’t been for the surveyor in
the Government camp, who gave us some salt
beef, I really think we should have died! But
how we laughed, oarrying home the salt beef in
a bit of old flour bag, and how Willie used to
hold out his arms to lift me over the big moss-
grown, rotting logs (I believe he went the
loggiest way on purpose), and I was happy, dear
life—how happy and how reckless of appear-
ances ! I can never remember what I wore,
except that my hat was as often off my head as
on it, and that Willie began to scold me for risk-
ing sunstroke, but changed his mind and kissed
me instead.
Now the girls choose my bonnets, and are very
keen on something suitable “for my age,” and
everybody says what a fortunate woman I am to
have such kind, thoughtful daughters. I suppose
I am. I can’t complain any way, but is there
anyone in the world who really wants me now ?
The girls will soon have their own husbands and
homes, both of which they will no doubt manage
beautifully, with a strict regard to the eye of the
world.
I never was a good housekeeper. Willie always
ordered the dinner when he didn’t forget, and
when he did, we had sardines. The girls won’t
let me eat sardines now; they say they are indi-
gestible. Beth has attended health lectures, and
Gwen is going to marry a doctor. Between
them, I daresay they will keep me alive much
longer than I want to be kept. They will make
me walk slowly and go for short tram rides, and
will never let me get properly, thoroughly tired,
so that I can sleep and dream all night that I
am young again.
I shall never settle down into taking an interest
in Mrs. Sowerby’s cook, nor in the wickedness of
Mrs. Denby-Cross’s last housemaid, who left in a
hurry and took the silver teaspoons by mistake—
nor in the Mothers’ Meetings and Rescue Societies.
I suppose people must be rescued, and that magic
lantern entertainments for South Sea Islanders are
an excellent institution, but I always wonder why
church mission people are such gossips and find
out their neighbors’ backslidings so easily, and
seem quite disappointed if they haven’t any ! I
shall never feel a chastened pleasure in detailing
my symptoms to a select group. I shall always
forget to put my cap on straight, if one of the girls
doesn’t watch me all the time. I shall always feel
disappointed when they go into Trott’s, the con-
fectioner’s, for afternoon tea, and order for me the
usual sponge cake—stale because that is more
easily digested—and the glass of sterilised milk,
while they—because, forsooth, they are young and
hearty—indulge in those dear little cakes with the
pink icing on the top, and all sorts of nice, deadly
things inside them.
I am not really wanted here any more. The
world is for the young. The sunshine is for the
young; the moonlight on wide waters and the
sound of music floating over from the town, the
warm clasp of a man’s hand about your own, are
for the young. The budding roses in the park,
where you can lounge on the grass and see the
blue sky laughing through the oreaking, whisper-
ing bamboo shoots, are for the young ! Fancy
me lolling on the grass ! It would give me rheu-
matism, and the girls would scold. One of my
future sons-in-law, if I were so foolish as to insist
on sitting out of doors, would carry out a camp-
stool for me, and someone would put a wrap aver
my shoulders.
And they say—if lam careful —I may live for
years—many years !
M. FORREST.
The Night I Spent in Quod.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I SPENT a night in Quod last year—in plain,
unvarnished Quod;
And I shall marshal swiftly here the
myriad thoughts which trod
Across my brain that dragging night, behind the
bolts and bars—
Behind the door which hid from sight my valued
friends, the stars !
The world went by in grim review—for from that
quiet cell
Had marched a motley, nameless crew—l seemed
to know them well.
They all came back and sat with me, those
shadow-felons odd ;
And there we held grey company, the night I
spent in Quod !
With sunken eyes and shoulders bent, the pallid
legion sat;
All hopeless and impenitent—the scum of For-
tune’s vat.
They spoke no word, but on their souls the vivid
cyphers burned;
I scanned the blazing, fateful rolls, and knew
how much they spurned
The plastic lies and sophistries which wrap the
lives of men—
The paltry, pale theologies, diluted ten times
ten!
I laughed at Churchianity; how cheap the par-
son’s God
Among those wrecks appeared to be—the night
I spent in Quod.
I spoke my thoughts aloud, and one gave forth a
bitter cry;
“I was,” he said, “in days long gone, apilotto
the sky.
I guided souls across a sea which I had never
crossed —
That Gulf of Grim Adversity, where many men
are lost !
I stood upon the wharf—l waved directions from
the shore;
And I concluded all were saved, for they returned
no more.
Butonce I ventured —once I steered” the voice
oflchabod
Reverberated sad and weird, the night I spent in
Quod !
Another shadow lifted high his puny, shaking
paw;
“ And I,” he said, “ in years gone by, I made the
precious Law.
I tabulated punishments—l made a bitter code
For those who dwell in Ishmael’s tents and go the
devil’s road.
I swore that I would conquer crime—that I would
shelter pelf;
Behold the ghastly jest of Time —where am I now
myself ?
Where am I now?” he cried again. “The
chains my soul corrode ”
I saw a shoreless sea of pain that night I spent in
Quod 1
And yet another hoarsely cried—his voice was
like a scar;
“And I stood on the further side—was not as
others are;
I was the Upright Citizen—Respectability
And all the high Commandments Ten were typi-
fied in me !
The siren voice of Self-Esteem made music in my
breast—
Whene’er I crossed life’s turbid stream that voice
shut out the rest!
I only thought of Mine and Me—l patronised my
God”—
I saw a foundered Pharisee the night I spent in
Quod !
The others sat with burning eyes—the voiceless
multitude
Whose unarticulated Whys in flaming symbols
stood.
I spoke for them, and said: “Behold! What think
ye now of these
Whom ye sent forth in days of old to cross un-
charted seas?
For these ye made your bitter codes, for these ye
made the laws;
They tramp alway the evil roads of vice and crime
because
Their fathers’ blood is in their veins—their fathers’
ways they plod.”
T called the Three the Sons of Cain—that night
I spent in Quod !
“Aye, ye are Cains, ye Three,” I said. “Ye
regulated well
The great machine which surely sped these others
into Hell.
The parson and the Pharisee, the man who made
the laws;
The ushers of eternity—ye are effect and cause !
Go now and lead these brothers hence, to what
they ought to be—
God’s surplus of omnipotence is rotting use-
lessly ! ”
They rose and left me in my cell—like phantoms
grey they trod;
A slender ray of sunshine fell, and it was dawn in
Quod 1
